## Preface

Field Manual (FM) 3-08 provides doctrinal guidance for planning and executing casualty evacuation
(CASEVAC) at all echelons. It emphasizes considerations, planning, and coordination over tactical vehicle
loading techniques. CASEVAC involves evacuating Soldiers from the point of injury (POI) to a medical
treatment facility (MTF) and coordinating nonmedical transportation assets to complete the mission. The
manual explains the differences between CASEVAC and medical evacuation (MEDEVAC), assigns
CASEVAC as a commander’s responsibility, and outlines the planning and training required for CASEVAC
operations. The principal audience for FM 3-08 is all members of the profession of arms. Commanders, their
staff, and leaders at all echelons should refer to this publication regarding the planning, training, equipping,
and execution of CASEVAC operations and techniques.
Commanders, staffs, and subordinates are responsible for making decisions and actions that comply with
applicable United States (U.S.), international, and in some cases host-nation laws and regulations.
Commanders at all levels are expected to confirm that their Soldiers operate in accordance with the law of
armed conflict and the rules of engagement. (Refer to FM 6-27).
This publication implements or is in consonance with the following North Atlantic Treaty Organization
(NATO) Standardization Agreement (STANAG) and the standards of the American, British, Canadian,
Australian, and New Zealand (ABCANZ) Armies program. NATO STANAGs are accessible online at the
NATO Standardization Office (NSO) public website. ABCANZ standards are accessible online at the
Protected Internet eXchange (PiX) – ABCANZ product library website. All referenced websites are listed in
the rear matter of this publication.

## Introduction

FM 3-08 focuses on the essential aspects of CASEVAC within the military framework, aligning its content
with the principles and strategies outlined in FM 3-0 and FM 4-02. Beyond its role in medical care,
CASEVAC is a critical enabler of operational success, facilitating the rearward movement of casualties to
clear the battlefield and maintain the commander’s freedom of maneuver. This function is vital in preventing
unit culmination by ensuring the rapid evacuation and treatment of wounded, ill and injured (WII) Soldiers,
maximizing return to duty (RTD) rates, and conserving combat power. Just as sustainment efforts recover
and repair damaged equipment to sustain operations, CASEVAC preserves the fighting strength of the force
by minimizing preventable fatalities and medical complications. This publication modernizes and
standardizes evacuation procedures, ensuring they reflect the latest and most effective practices. By providing
a comprehensive, accessible guide, FM 3-08 serves as a critical resource for personnel engaged in casualty
care, battlefield evacuation, and enhances proficiency in these vital operations.
FM 3-08 serves as the keystone doctrine for CASEVAC, providing a topic overview, foundational discussion,
and guidance on planning at echelon. Readers should also refer to Army Techniques Publication
(ATP) 4-02.13 which focuses on the tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTP) to conduct CASEVAC
operations. While FM 3-08 establishes the overarching principles and planning framework, ATP 4-02.13
provides detailed execution guidance, complementing the content of this manual. Together, these documents
form a cohesive doctrinal foundation for Army CASEVAC operations.
CASEVAC is critical to the success of military maneuvers and overall mission accomplishment. It provides
swift and effective transportation of WII Soldiers from the battlefield to MTFs, helping preserve the unit’s
combat power and morale. The strategic use of CASEVAC reflects the Army’s commitment to Soldier
welfare and supports operational continuity. By reducing complications from injuries and preventable deaths,
effective casualty management directly supports the success and endurance of military operations.
The primary aim of FM 3-08 is to equip all Army personnel with the necessary knowledge and skills for
conducting efficient CASEVAC operations. This document is crafted to enhance the readiness and
competence of Soldiers in handling medical emergencies and ensuring personnel can perform evacuations
proficiently under diverse conditions. Through detailed guidelines and structured protocols, FM 3-08 makes
the complex procedures of CASEVAC comprehensible and manageable, enabling Soldiers to maintain
operational effectiveness while upholding the highest standards of care and safety for their fellow Soldiers.
FM 3-08 contains four chapters, and five appendixes as follows:
**Chapter 1**establishes the foundational role of CASEVAC in Army operations and shows how it strengthens
combat power, survivability, and endurance by supporting tasks across all levels of command.
**Chapter 2** shows how commanders incorporate CASEVAC into joint and multidomain operations to support
tempo and mission success. Describe how CASEVAC aligns with warfighting functions and adapts across
operational environments.
**Chapter 3**explains how units plan CASEVAC during the military decision-making process (MDMP) or
troop leading procedures (TLP). Highlight how planning roles vary by echelon and require coordination
across medical and operational staff.
**Chapter 4**shows how units conduct CASEVAC during operations by using mission triggers, command
authority, and coordination systems. It emphasizes the importance of real-time adjustments to keep
evacuation efforts timely and maintain operational momentum.
**Appendix A**describes how commanders tailor CASEVAC strategies to diverse environments like jungle,
mountain, desert, and urban terrain. Focus on terrain-specific challenges, equipment, and planning needed
for successful evacuation.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**vii**
**Introduction**
**Appendix B** explores the complexities of managing mass casualty (MASCAL) situations, emphasizing the
crucial role of CASEVAC in operations. It covers planning, coordination, and execution strategies, including
how to address chemical, biological, radiological, and nuclear (CBRN) hazards and incorporate technological
advancements, enabling military planners to optimize responses and improve outcomes in high-stress
environments.
**Appendix C**examines the capabilities and uses of CASEVAC in operations, highlighting how they support
and expand traditional MEDEVAC platforms. It addresses key operational considerations such as
establishing CASEVAC corridors, allocating resources, and emphasizing the value of training and
coordination. These efforts help create smooth transitions and continuous care during evacuation across a
range of environments.
**Appendix D**presents a comprehensive checklist for conducting CASEVAC operations, highlighting the need
for detailed planning, synchronized execution, and ongoing assessment. It outlines essential elements such
as resource identification, casualty collection point setup, and logistics coordination. These tools help leaders
provide effective medical support and achieve mission success across a range of operational environments.
**Appendix E**compares the evacuation request formats used by different countries and organizations,
including the U.S. military 9-Line, ABCANZ Nine-Line, and NATO MEDEVAC 9-Liner formats. It
identifies similarities and differences between these formats, offering recommendations for standardizing
them to improve interoperability and coordination in multinational operations, ultimately enhancing
evacuation effectiveness and patient care.
In accordance with doctrinal changes, terms for which FM 3-08 is the proponent have been included in this
publication. The glossary contains acronyms and defined terms with modified or rescinded Army terms
in introductory table-1.

### Chapter 1

## Foundations of Casualty Evacuation

The Army uses casualty evacuation (CASEVAC) to preserve combat power by rapidly moving
wounded, ill, and injured (WII) Soldiers from the battlefield to medical treatment. CASEVAC
provides immediate transport using available platforms, allowing units to sustain operations
while casualties receive care. In contrast, medical evacuation (MEDEVAC) involves the
regulated movement of patients on dedicated, protected medical platforms with en route care
provided by trained personnel. Together, CASEVAC and MEDEVAC enhance survivability
and contribute to operational endurance.
This chapter introduces CASEVAC as a critical element of operational success across different
environments. It explains how commanders and staffs plan and carry out. The chapter also
shows how CASEVAC functions across the theater strategic, operational, and tactical levels.
It highlights the key principles that guide CASEVAC planning, integration, execution, and
sustainment. Leaders will gain tools to incorporate CASEVAC into broader operational plans
and maintain readiness in dispersed, contested, and degraded conditions.

## INTRODUCTION OF CASUALTY EVACUATION AS AN

## INTEGRATED OPERATIONAL TASK ACROSS ECHELON

1-1. CASEVAC contributes to planned, resourced, and synchronized evacuation operations. It moves WII
personnel to a medical treatment facility (MTF). Commanders plan for CASEVAC at the strategic,
operational, and tactical levels of warfare to help sustain operations and preserve combat power. For
additional information on the levels of warfare, refer to FM 3-0. Combat power is the total means of
destructive and disruptive force that a military unit/formation can apply against an enemy at a given time
(Joint Publication [JP] 3-0). When built into the operational framework, CASEVAC improves unit
survivability by ensuring timely medical support. Survivability is a quality or capability of military forces
which permits them to avoid or withstand hostile actions or environmental conditions while retaining the
ability to fulfill their primary mission (Army Techniques Publication [ATP] 3-37.34).
1-2. CASEVAC operations support mission success by enabling timely casualty movement and reducing
disruption to combat operations. Commanders account for planning factors such as coordination, movement
timelines, and casualty flow to integrate CASEVAC into the operation. When applied, CASEVAC
strengthens unit endurance and allows forces to maintain effectiveness under battlefield conditions.
1-3. At the theater strategic level, commanders and staffs coordinate joint capabilities to support CASEVAC
as a mission enabler. These efforts help subordinate units conduct evacuation tasks, even in contested or
degraded environments. By planning CASEVAC as part of operational support at each level, leaders align
resources with mission needs and tie evacuation planning into the broader operation.
1-4. At operational and tactical levels, all leaders and planners develop CASEVAC plans that support the
mission, match the terrain, account for threats, and keep pace with the fight. These plans define how
evacuation platforms, medical assets, and the casualty collection point (CCP) fit within the scheme of
maneuver. CASEVAC execution starts at the lowest levels, with buddy teams moving casualties to platoon
and company CCPs. A casualty collection point is a location that may or may not be staffed, where casualties
are assembled for evacuation to a medical treatment facility (ATP 4-02.2). From the CCP the first sergeant
(1SG), medic, or designated CASEVAC platforms move casualties toward higher roles of care. Effective
plans anticipate likely obstacles, such as complex terrain, weather, or enemy contact, and include
contingencies to keep evacuation support continuous and reduce risk to the force.
1-5. Agility is essential in CASEVAC planning and execution. Commanders and planners track changes in
the operational environment and adjust routes, timing, and coordination procedures as needed. This demands
situational awareness, clear communication, and quick decision-making across all levels of command. By
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**1**
**Chapter 1**
refining evacuation strategies in real time and responding to new threats, units keep CASEVAC operations
moving and aligned with the maneuver plan.

## UNDERSTANDING CASUALTY EVACUATION AND MEDICAL

## EVACUATION IN OPERATIONS

1-6. CASEVAC and MEDEVAC both move WII personnel, but they differ in purpose, platform, and medical
support. **Casualty evacuation****is the movement of wounded, ill, and injured personnel aboard any**
**nonmedical platform; also called CASEVAC**. Medical evacuation is the timely and effective movement
of the wounded, injured, or ill to and between medical treatment facilities on dedicated and properly marked
medical platforms with en route care provided by medical personnel. Also called MEDEVAC (ATP 4-02.2).
A medical treatment facility is any facility established for the purpose of providing medical treatment. This
includes battalion aid stations, Role 2 facilities, dispensaries, clinics, and hospitals (Field Manual [FM] 4-02).
**Note.** Joint doctrine separates CASEVAC, MEDEVAC, and multi-modal patient movement based
on platform type and availability of en route care. CASEVAC, in joint doctrine, refers to
unregulated transport without medical personnel. MEDEVAC is the regulated use of dedicated,
marked platforms with standardized medical support. Multi-modal patient movement bridges
CASEVAC and MEDEVAC and involves both regulated and unregulated movement with en route
care on non-dedicated platforms.
1-7. These distinctions shape how evacuation is conducted during operations. CASEVAC is often more
flexible and may be used when MEDEVAC assets are unavailable due to threat conditions. However,
CASEVAC platforms generally lack the medical personnel, equipment, and legal protections granted under
the Geneva Conventions. Unless preplanned or coordinated in advance, CASEVAC typically does not
provide en route medical care. Despite these limitations, CASEVAC allows for faster access to treatment and
supports survival in high-risk or austere environments.
1-8. Commanders and planners assess both legal and operational considerations when choosing between
CASEVAC and MEDEVAC. CASEVAC may involve moving casualties who have not received care, as well
as patients who have. This distinction affects planning for triage, resource allocation, and compliance with
international law during transport and treatment.
1-9. A casualty is any person who is lost to the organization by having been declared dead, duty
status – whereabouts unknown, excused absence – whereabouts unknown, missing, ill, or injured (JP 4-02).
A patient is a wounded, ill, and injured person who receives medical care or treatment from medical personnel
(ATP 4-02.10). The Army Health System (AHS) uses these terms when coordinating patient movement and
determining evacuation priorities. Table 1-1 compares the categories of WII personnel to support consistent
identification and management within the patient movement system. CASEVAC serves as a critical link in
this system when medical platforms are unavailable or delayed.
**Table 1-1. Comparison between wounded, ill, and injured personnel**
**Category**
**Cause**
**Nature of Harm**
**Common Context**

Hostile action, combat.
External (from enemy
force).

zones.

environmental.

conditions.

psychological trauma,
distress or damage to the
body.
1-10. CASEVAC operations often occur under fire or in degraded conditions, particularly during large-scale
combat operations. These missions require thorough coordination between maneuver forces and supporting
units to minimize risk and support timely casualty movement. MEDEVAC platforms may be limited by air
**2**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Foundations of Casualty Evacuation**
defense threats or weather, making CASEVAC an essential option in contested areas. When units incorporate
CASEVAC into early planning, they improve alignment across movement assets, casualty tracking systems,
and operational timelines.
1-11. CASEVAC planning begins with a clear understanding of the environment, available resources, and
operational risk. CASEVAC is not a replacement for MEDEVAC but complements it by providing additional
flexibility in casualty movement. Both systems work toward the same goal, moving casualties to higher roles
of care. CASEVAC enhances survival, preserves unit strength, and enables commanders to continue
operations despite adversity.

## THE ROLE OF CASUALTY EVACUATION

1-12. CASEVAC supports the Army’s ability to fight, endure, and recover across the full range of military
operations. It contributes to combat power by reducing personnel losses and sustaining unit readiness. To
conduct CASEVAC, commanders and staffs view it not only as a medical action but as an implied task for
all operations. CASEVAC enables the rapid movement of WII personnel to medical care. The following
paragraphs describe how CASEVAC supports Army operations across the continuum from competition to
crisis to armed conflict.
1-13. CASEVAC reduces the human cost of war by providing WII personnel with timely medical care and,
when possible, returning them to duty. Return to Duty (RTD) a patient disposition which, after medical
evaluation and treatment when necessary, returns a Soldier for duty in his unit (FM 4-02). CASEVAC
remains adaptable to support casualty movement under threat, along contested routes, or through degraded
communications. CASEVAC strengthens morale, preserves cohesion, and helps maintain the Army’s
operational tempo.
1-14. During large-scale combat operations, casualty rates may exceed MEDEVAC capacity. Units
coordinate CASEVAC with maneuver, medical, and sustainment efforts to avoid delays and minimize
disruption. While rapid evacuation is essential, leaders also protect combat power by integrating CASEVAC
into the broader operational framework. This balance helps units sustain momentum even in high-casualty
environments.

## TENETS OF CASUALTY EVACUATION

1-15. The following tenets give commanders and staffs a foundation to plan and carry out CASEVAC in
many operational environments. These tenets describe how CASEVAC strengthens operational resilience by
preserving combat power, protecting mobility, and meeting the demands of dispersed, contested, or degraded
conditions. CASEVAC helps maintain continuity of operations by expanding evacuation options, supporting
survivability, and enabling maneuver over time. When commanders apply these tenets with mission variables
and operational tempo in mind, they help the force maintain readiness, synchronize support across domains,
and continue the fight after disruption.
1-16. Casualty evacuation is organized into four major tenets: planning and adaptation, operational
integration, execution and platforms, and preparedness and sustainment. Planning and adaptation helps units
prepare for changes using deliberate and flexible design. Operational integration aligns CASEVAC efforts
with the scheme of maneuver and commander’s intent. Execution and platforms focuses on using a range of
resources to maintain combat strength. Finally, preparedness and sustainment highlights the importance of
training, rehearsals, logistics and integrating with unified action partners to sustain evacuation operations.
Together, these tenets create a clear framework that helps commanders include CASEVAC in planning and
operations at every level.

### PLANNING ANDADAPTATION- HOWCASUALTYEVACUATIONSTARTS

1-17. CASEVAC begins with deliberate planning. This focuses on the ability to anticipate battlefield
changes, adjust to evolving threats, and maintain support across dispersed formations. Attributes like
proactive and adaptive planning, scalability and flexibility, redundancy and decentralization allow units to
respond to disruption. Together, they help commanders design evacuation systems that remain effective even
when conditions shift rapidly or support is degraded.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**3**
**Chapter 1**
**Proactive and Adaptive Planning**
1-18. Proactive planning enables units to forecast CASEVAC needs based on terrain, threat, tempo, and
available resources. Commanders identify challenges and build options for flexible execution. This attribute
reinforces scalability, redundancy, and synchronization, particularly during large-scale or rapidly evolving
operations.
**Scalability and Flexibility**
1-19. CASEVAC provides scalable and flexible capabilities that support forces across wide and contested
areas. These qualities allow units to adjust support in decentralized or isolated settings. This attribute
complements proactive planning by giving commanders options to adapt to changes in tempo, terrain, or
threat.
**Redundancy and Decentralization**
1-20. Redundancy and decentralization increase reliability when primary CASEVAC systems are
unavailable or compromised. Commanders develop alternate routes and platforms to maintain continuous
support under threat. This attribute aligns with platform versatility by expanding the range of available
options.

### OPERATIONALINTEGRATION– CASUALTYEVACUATION AS AMISSIONTASK IN THE

### BROADERFIGHT

1-21. CASEVAC stays connected to the flow of operations. Planners align casualty movement with
command structures, maneuver plans, and joint efforts. Integration across echelons and domains, combined
with synchronization with warfighting functions, helps CASEVAC support the broader mission, not disrupt
it. Disciplined mission command enables decentralized execution while keeping unity of effort.
**Integration Across Echelons and Domains**
1-22. CASEVAC planning connects capabilities across echelons and domains. Commanders match land, air,
and maritime assets to the situation to move casualties in a timely manner while maintaining protection during
movement. This integration supports joint operations and sustains operational reach and endurance under
fire.
**Synchronization with Warfighting Functions**
1-23. CASEVAC synchronizes with maneuver, fires, and sustainment to prevent delays and keep tempo.
Coordinated planning ties CASEVAC to the commander’s intent and links medical movement with
battlefield tasks. When CASEVAC is treated as a separate action, units risk losing momentum and exposing
evacuation assets to avoidable threats.
**Mission Command and Disciplined Initiative**
1-24. CASEVAC operations rely on mission command to function in complex or degraded environments.
Commanders clearly communicate their intent, not only for the operation, but for medical support. This
shared understanding allows CASEVAC teams to act with disciplined initiative, even when communications
are limited or conditions change. For example, a unit may need to move a casualty to an adjacent unit’s CCP
if it brings faster care. By linking CASEVAC decisions to the broader plan, commanders support
decentralized execution and keep operations moving forward.

### EXECUTION ANDPLATFORMS- WHATMOVESCASUALTIES

1-25. CASEVAC relies on the ability to move casualties using available resources. This tenet focuses on
employing a variety of platforms and improving efficiency in how forces evacuate and care for the WII.
Attributes such as platform versatility, resource optimization, and support to combat effectiveness help
CASEVAC efforts preserve combat power and use assets wisely. These practices keep units mobile,
responsive, and capable under pressure.
**4**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Foundations of Casualty Evacuation**
1-26. In large-scale combat operations, CASEVAC is often the primary method for moving casualties from
the point of injury (POI) to medical treatment. Units use whatever platforms are available, stretchers, litters,
cargo trucks, tactical vehicles, air assets, or unconventional means, based on terrain, threat, and urgency. This
flexibility helps preserve dedicated MEDEVAC for casualties requiring en route medical care. Platform
versatility is not about preference but survival. It supports decentralized execution and helps sustain casualty
movement when resources are limited, routes are contested, or operations cannot pause.
1-27. Partner nation platforms, ground, air, and maritime, often require adaptation to support CASEVAC.
For example, stretcher modifications, safety padding, and stocked medical supplies enhance casualty
movement. Historical precedents, including World War II hospital trains in Europe and naval evacuations
from Normandy, demonstrate how rail and maritime transport remain viable options today, particularly in
areas like the Indo-Pacific where vast distances separated operating forces.
1-28. Joint CASEVAC planning addresses technical and procedural differences between nations. This
includes aligning communications, vehicle standards, and casualty-handling protocols. Pre-arranged
agreements, such as those in North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO) and the Republic of Korea,
streamline transfers and improve interoperability. Training exercises and a shared standard operating
procedure (SOP) build familiarity and reduce friction during execution.

### PREPAREDNESS ANDSUSTAINMENT- WHATKEEPSCASUALTYEVACUATIONEFFECTIVE

1-29. CASEVAC is only effective when forces are trained, rehearsed, and properly supported. This tenet
emphasizes the importance of readiness and sustained support across units and coalition partners. Attributes
such as training and rehearsals, along with sustainment and multinational integration, help CASEVAC
systems perform under pressure, align with partners, and continue functioning during prolonged missions.
These efforts form the foundation for long-term operational endurance.
**Training and Rehearsals**
1-30. Training and rehearsals prepare CASEVAC personnel to confidently react under pressure. At the
tactical level, aid and litter teams, medic, platoon sergeant (PSG), and company 1SG often carry out the initial
evacuation. These teams practice movement, casualty handling, and coordination with higher medical assets.
At higher echelons, staff rehearse mission command tasks like route clearance, asset tracking, and handoffs
between units. Repeated practice improves communication and reduces friction during real operations by
aligning actions across all levels of command.
**Sustainment and Multinational Integration**
1-31. While MEDEVAC is a component of health service support (HSS) within the sustainment warfighting
function, CASEVAC synchronizes with both operations and sustainment. Commanders place CASEVAC
planning in Annex C (Operations) of the operation order (OPORD) to drive planning and resource allocation
as part of the scheme of maneuver. Coordination in Annex F (Sustainment) aligns CASEVAC with medical
evacuation, medical treatment, and other sustainment activities. Effective CASEVAC planning accounts for
routes, evacuation timelines, medical capability at transfer points, and integration with host-nation and allied
partners. Treating CASEVAC as an operational task, rather than an afterthought, improves readiness, reduces
delays, and maintains casualty movement even in combined or coalition environments.
1-32. CASEVAC supports freedom of action, protects combat power, and strengthens the Army’s ability to
operate under stress. It remains a key enabler with the sustainment warfighting function. The tenets of
CASEVAC help commanders and staffs incorporate CASEVAC into each phase of planning and execution.
Chapter 2 explains how CASEVAC fits within the Army’s operational framework and links to the
warfighting functions that shape mission success.

**FM 3-08**

## This page intentionally left blank.

### Chapter 2

## Casualty Evacuation During Operations

CASEVAC is a mission-focused task that is planned and executed across all levels of
command. This chapter explains how commanders and staffs integrate CASEVAC into
operations to preserve combat power and keep momentum. Effective integration requires
coordination across echelons, detailed planning, and flexibility in changing battlefield
conditions. CASEVAC supports broader mission goals and align with joint, multinational, and
interagency efforts. When viewed as a mission enabler rather than a separate function,
CASEVAC helps improve survivability, sustain tempo, and fulfill the Army’s responsibility to
care for WII personnel.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION INTEGRATION ACROSS ECHELON AND

## PLANNING CONSIDERATIONS

2-1. Commanders and staffs include CASEVAC in operations at all levels to help preserve combat power
and maintain momentum. Rather than treating it as a separate mission, leaders incorporate CASEVAC into
the plan, linking it with maneuver, command and control (C2), and sustainment. At each echelon, planners
assess how evacuation assets, CCPs, and routes support the broader scheme. They also consider
environmental threats, platform limits, and operational risks. Integrating CASEVAC early across warfighting
functions improves survivability, protects the force, and supports continuous operations. By embedding
CASEVAC into planning, commanders help move casualties from the POI to MTFs, aligning tactical actions
with broader mission goals.

### INTEGRATINGCASUALTYEVACUATIONACROSSECHELON

2-2. Theater-strategic leaders shape the policies, authorities, and force structure that support CASEVAC
planning and execution at lower echelons. While they do not plan individual evacuation missions, they create
the framework that enables subordinate commands to do so. This includes setting agreements with
interagency and multinational partners, authorizing access to host-nation medical infrastructure, and directing
theater-level medical posture. These efforts are especially important during joint and coalition operations,
where differences in medical capability, classification, or patient movement policies can delay or disrupt
casualty evacuation. Strategic-level support does not replace tactical planning but provides the foundation
for effective CASEVAC under complex or resource-constrained conditions.
2-3. At the operational level, planners shape the conditions that support CASEVAC across the theater. These
responsibilities include allocating medical assets, coordinating patient movement between Roles 2 and 3
facilities, and managing evacuation priorities when resources are limited. Operational planners do not manage
CCPs directly, but they understand how casualties move from the POI through the system. In large-scale
combat operations, they help develop routing plans, stage evacuation platforms, and prepare for mass casualty
(MASCAL) events that can overwhelm medical capabilities. These efforts connect with maneuver and
sustainment plans to maintain tempo, preserve combat power, and reduce operational risk. When CASEVAC
is treated as a supporting task, rather than an afterthought, units are better prepared to respond when
MEDEVAC is unavailable, or the volume of casualties exceeds capacity. A mass casualty is any number of
human casualties produced across a period of time that exceeds available medical support capabilities. Also
called MASCAL (JP 4-02).
2-4. At the tactical level, CASEVAC is often executed in dynamic and hostile conditions. Maneuver
commanders coordinate the use of available platforms, whether ground, air, or waterborne, to transport
casualties to CCPs or higher roles of care. Tactical CASEVAC responds to immediate needs and typically
lacks dedicated en route care, unless it has been preplanned or coordinated with medical assets. Its success
relies on the initiative of small units, situational awareness, and adaptability.
2-5. CASEVAC supports operational endurance by moving casualties from the POI to higher roles of care,
even in austere or contested environments. To remain effective, CASEVAC is deliberately incorporated into
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**7**
**Chapter 2**
operations and synchronized with C2, protection, and sustainment functions. This coordination clears the
area of casualties, preserves combat power, and reduces disruption to maneuver forces. While most
CASEVAC missions do not provide en route medical care, the act of evacuating WII Soldiers demonstrates
the Army’s commitment to its personnel. This reinforces morale and contributes to overall mission success.

### CASUALTYEVACUATIONPLANNINGCONSIDERATIONS

2-6. Effective CASEVAC operations begin with deliberate planning and risk assessment tailored to the
operational environment. Commanders and staffs evaluate enemy threats, air defense systems, and terrain
challenges that may influence evacuation routes, and platform selection. They also assess available assets,
including ground, air, and maritime transport, and coordinate with host-nation and coalition partners when
required. A well-developed CASEVAC plan increases survivability, supports operational tempo, and helps
casualties reach care without delaying the mission.
2-7. C2 is essential for effective CASEVAC. Commanders define roles, authorities, and communications
between maneuver elements, evacuation platforms, and medical treatment facilities to avoid confusion or
delays. Triage protocols are developed by medical personnel but directed through command authority. Triage
is the system of sorting and prioritizing casualties based on the tactical situation, mission, available resources,
and medical urgency (FM 4-02). Once a trained medical provider assumes responsibility for care, the casualty
becomes a patient and enters the patient movement system. This transition can happen at the POI, a CCP, or
any location where medical care is initiated. CASEVAC becomes part of the patient movement system when
used to move patients under medical direction, even if the platform is nonmedical. In MASCAL events,
commanders also coordinate with medical staff to estimate casualty volume, prioritize movement, and direct
public health activities under their C2 authority.
2-8. Contested environments present unique risks to CASEVAC operations that require deliberate planning
and mitigation. Peer threats such as Russia and China have demonstrated the ability to conduct
electromagnetic warfare, including Global Positioning System (GPS) spoofing, signal jamming and
interference, and cyberattacks against logistics and medical networks. They also use persistent surveillance
and long-range fires to target evacuation routes. To reduce these risks, planners develop alternate routes, use
deception and terrain masking, and include analog communications in CASEVAC plans. Coordination with
joint and multinational partners adds redundancy and increases resiliency. By planning for degraded
communications and enemy interference, commanders improve the continuity of CASEVAC in large-scale
combat operations.

### CASUALTYEVACUATION’SIMPACT ONCOMBATEFFECTIVENESS

2-9. CASEVAC directly affects unit readiness, morale, and the ability to maintain tempo in combat. Soldiers
who trust they will be treated and evacuated are more confident under fire and more willing to take necessary
risks. Rapid evacuation allows timely medical treatment, which reduces preventable deaths and helps
preserve combat strength. When CASEVAC is delayed or disrupted, casualties increase, stress builds, and
unit performance declines. Well-executed evacuation operations reassure troops, sustain morale, and help
commanders keep forces in the fight longer.
2-10. CASEVAC supports endurance in large-scale or prolonged combat operations. Removing WII Soldiers
from the fight allows commanders to reallocate resources, adjust task organization, and manage combat
power. When CASEVAC is built into the operational plan, it helps units recover from losses, avoid early
culmination, and remain effective despite fatigue or environmental stress. CASEVAC planning contributes
directly to force preservation, readiness, and survivability, making it a critical part of sustained operations.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION PLANNING AND EXECUTION

2-11. The operational environment significantly influences CASEVAC planning. Commanders assess
feasibility using mission variables:
•
Mission: Does CASEVAC support the operation’s purpose?
•
Enemy: Do threats impact evacuation timing or routes?
•
Terrain and Weather: Do conditions restrict movement?
•
Troops and Support Available: Are CASEVAC assets and medical personnel available?
•
Time Available: How quickly must the casualty be moved (evacuation precedence)?
**8**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation During Operations**
•
Civil Considerations: Are host-nation MTFs or civilian hospitals available?
•
Intelligence: What enemy activity may affect evacuation plans?
2-12. Applying mission variables improves adaptability and reduces risk. Commanders use this analysis to
refine CASEVAC timing, routing, and platform selection, supporting survivability and mission success.
2-13. CASEVAC planning begins with anticipating when and where casualties are likely to occur. Planners
assess available evacuation platforms and develop evacuation plans that support the scheme of maneuver
while accounting for tempo, terrain, and threat conditions. Integrating CASEVAC early improves the
delivery of care and enables timely movement of WII personnel.
2-14. Effective evacuation planning considers expected casualty rates, terrain challenges, and known threat
areas. Planners study how the mission might unfold and identify areas where CASEVAC will likely be
needed. This foresight helps shape a responsive and flexible system that adapts to changing battlefield
conditions.
2-15. Coordination among planners, executors, and support personnel helps CASEVAC tasks operate
smoothly under stress. Planners assign resources and define responsibilities. CASEVAC executors, such as
unit leaders, adapt those plans on the ground. Medical staff and support personnel assist during evacuation,
stabilizing casualties, and reducing delays during handoff.
2-16. CASEVAC is often a “be prepared to” task but may also be conducted as a deliberate mission in support
of AHS operations, such as clearing a MTF or sustaining evacuation during high-casualty events. Units plan
for CASEVAC by considering mission variables. The surgeon, exercising medical C2 on behalf of the
commander, synchronizes the movement of casualties and coordinates with operational elements to maintain
safe and timely evacuation. Close coordination between commanders, staff, and medical personnel reduces
confusion, enables patient tracking, and supports effective casualty movement under all conditions.
2-17. Unpredictable environments and communication breakdowns create operational friction that challenges
CASEVAC execution. However, CASEVAC teams should remain focused on planning, flexibility, and
coordination. Their quick adjustments improve patient outcomes and strengthen overall CASEVAC
resilience. Their role supports the broader goals of survivability and mission continuity.

### CASUALTYEVACUATION AS APLANNEDTASKWITHINMILITARYOPERATIONS

2-18. Casualty evacuation is planned as part of the larger operation, not handled as a last-minute task. When
CASEVAC is integrated into the operational framework, it helps commanders preserve freedom of maneuver
and maintain freedom of action. Freedom of maneuver allows forces to reposition or advance, while freedom
of action gives commanders choices about how to fight. In offensive operations, delays in evacuating
casualties can slow the advance and risk losing momentum. During defense, delays can isolate maneuver
units and leave them vulnerable to defeat in detail. Commanders account for terrain, enemy activity, and
mission objectives when linking CASEVAC tasks to maneuver and sustainment efforts.
2-19. Effective CASEVAC planning includes selecting routes, tasking evacuation assets, and coordinating
across echelons. These actions support casualty movement and reduce delays that can disrupt operations.
Tying CASEVAC to mission complexity and force structure helps units adapt to changing conditions during
the fight.
2-20. Using standardized procedures and common terms helps CASEVAC teams respond and maintain clear
communication with medical personnel. Training and rehearsals build muscle memory for events ranging
from high-risk evacuations under fire to large-scale casualty events.
2-21. A well-planned CASEVAC mission supports continuous operations by keeping Soldiers in the fight
and protecting unit readiness. CASEVAC planning does not end with the OPORD, it is refined as the mission
unfolds. When tied to mission goals, CASEVAC helps maintain tempo, reduce confusion, and increase
survivability.

### ADAPTABILITY INCASUALTYEVACUATIONOPERATIONS

2-22. CASEVAC effectiveness depends on a unit’s ability to anticipate and respond to changing battlefield
conditions. Visualization and preparation allow leaders to adjust procedures before conditions deteriorate.
This proactive approach supports continuity of care during unexpected events.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**9**
**Chapter 2**
2-23. Evacuation teams adjust to new threats, terrain, and conditions by changing evacuation routes, tactics,
platforms, or treatment priorities. Their ability to react in real time strengthens the connection between the
POI and higher levels of care.
2-24. Commanders maintain oversight by using real-time information to modify CASEVAC plans. These
adjustments may include shifting resources, rerouting platforms, or staging assets closer to expected high-
casualty areas. This foresight improves CASEVAC reliability in contested conditions.
2-25. Anticipation and adaptability work together to maintain readiness. CASEVAC leaders assess risks and
develop fallback options that allow continued operations when primary plans fail. This planning culture
supports both survivability and freedom of action.
2-26. When leaders visualize potential challenges, they better prepare personnel and resources for immediate
use. By acting early, they reduce delays and improve the effectiveness of limited evacuation options. This
approach blends discipline with flexibility.
2-27. CASEVAC depends on standard procedures, trained personnel, and accessible resources to bridge the
gap between planning and execution. These elements allow units to shift between deliberate evacuation and
real-time adjustment without losing tempo or cohesion.

### CONSIDERATIONS FORUNITEDSTATESARMYCONVENTIONAL ANDSPECIALOPERATIONS

### INTEGRATION

2-28. Army Special operations forces (ARSOF) often rely on CASEVAC support from conventional units.
Supporting ARSOF elements in deep or denied areas is especially challenging due to their small team size,
wide dispersion, and limited access to secure evacuation routes. These conditions increase the risk of
prolonged delays in casualty movement. Forward patient acquisition becomes even harder when enemy
activity or maneuver restrictions prevent conventional forces from reaching ARSOF elements. In such cases,
medical planners anticipate the need for extended care at the POI and consider forward positioning medical
personnel, such as a physician assistant or special operations medic, to provide care until evacuation becomes
possible.
2-29. CASEVAC planning for ARSOF requires close coordination between AHS planners, ARSOF medical
planners, and conventional force planners. This coordination integrates ARSOF requirements into both
special operations and conventional plans while aligning medical capabilities, evacuation timelines, and
command relationships. When medical units are not positioned forward, planners request specific AHS
capabilities, identify supporting platforms, and prepare for nonstandard casualty movement, deception
methods, or extended delays. Effective integration relies on shared graphics, mutual support agreements, and
clearly defined command relationships, especially during joint or coalition operations. CASEVAC planning
for ARSOF should include:
•
Integration into conventional and special OPORDs and graphics.
•
Coordination across command relationships and echelons.
•
Platform readiness, access to denied terrain, and the ability to conduct patient movement under
threat.
For more information on AHS support to ARSOF, refer to ATP 4-02.43. For more information on the ARSOF
patient evacuation system, refer to Training Circular (TC) 18-09.

## SPECIAL POPULATIONS AND ETHICAL CONSIDERATIONS

2-30. CASEVAC primarily supports the evacuation of United States (U.S.) and partner military personnel.
However, commanders plan for the movement of nonstandard casualties, including military working animal
(MWA), detainees, and civilians. Detainees and civilians are protected under the law of armed conflict
(LOAC) and present distinct ethical, medical, and logistical challenges. Planners account for these factors in
CASEVAC operations and follow unit policies and higher command guidance to uphold legal and humane
treatment at every level.

### MORTUARYAFFAIRS ANDCASUALTYEVACUATIONBOUNDARIES

2-31. CASEVAC and mortuary affairs are distinct missions with different responsibilities, personnel, and
procedures. Units must not confuse the two. According to ATP 4-46, mortuary affairs personnel recover,
**10**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation During Operations**
process, and evacuate human remains through a separate chain of custody. CASEVAC operations focus
solely on the evacuation of WII personnel to preserve life and maintain combat power. Planners integrate
mortuary affairs into operations to prevent confusion, maintain unit discipline, and uphold the respectful
treatment of the fallen.
2-32. Transporting deceased personnel on CASEVAC platforms may cause emotional harm and degrade unit
morale. When possible, commanders should use separate assets for the deceased. Sustainment and logistics
units coordinate these efforts, while religious affairs personnel support cultural or spiritual needs. Keeping
the missions distinct maintains the operational focus of CASEVAC teams and upholds the Army’s values.

### MILITARYWORKINGANIMALCASUALTYEVACUATIONCONSIDERATIONS

2-33. MWAs, such as dogs used for detection, patrol, or search and rescue, are a valuable force multiplier.
Their injury can degrade mission effectiveness, so CASEVAC teams plan for their stabilization and
evacuation. This may require special equipment such as muzzles, litters, or restraints. Commanders adapt
ground and air platforms to move MWAs safely without delaying the evacuation of personnel.
2-34. Veterinary support to MWA CASEVAC is coordinated in advance. Trained veterinary personnel or
handlers with approved canine tactical combat casualty care training provide care when available.
Commanders coordinate with supporting veterinary units to plan for triage, treatment, and evacuation of
MWAs as part of overall HSS.
2-35. Although human casualties always take priority, MWAs may be moved on a space-available basis.
Units with MWAs develop standard procedures for their emergency care and evacuation in coordination with
veterinary support.

## COMPLIANCE WITH THE LAW OF ARMED CONFLICT

2-36. CASEVAC operations must comply with the Geneva Conventions and the LOAC. Commanders must
understand that platforms used for CASEVAC do not receive protected status under international law unless
they meet strict criteria: being unarmed, clearly marked with a Red Cross, and used exclusively for medical
purposes. Most CASEVAC platforms, such as vehicles used by PSGs, 1SGs, or logistics personnel, do not
meet these standards and are not protected. Their use for casualty movement is lawful, but they may be legally
targeted under LOAC. Commanders should weigh the legal, operational, and ethical risks when selecting
CASEVAC platforms, and consult legal advisors when necessary.

### WARNING:

**The misuse of the distinctive emblem (Red Cross) is strictly**
**prohibited and could result in a war crime. The decision to**
**temporarily mark platforms used exclusively for medical**
**purposes with the Red Cross is the responsibility of the major**
**tactical commander, such as a brigade commander or higher.**

### MARKING OFPLATFORMS

2-37. The Red Cross emblem provides legal protection to medical platforms, but its use is strictly regulated
by international law. Misuse can result in war crimes and jeopardize missions. Only a brigade commander or
higher may authorize marking and must do so in consultation with legal counsel. To qualify for protection,
platforms must be dedicated solely to medical purposes, operated by non-combatants, and free of any
offensive weaponry, however, individual self-defense weapons are permitted. Platforms cannot carry troops,
war materiel, or engage in any activity supporting combat operations. Examples of permitted actions while
under the protected status of the Red Cross emblem are:
•
Transporting wounded Soldiers from a battlefield to a medical facility.
•
Operating a field hospital providing surgical care and medical treatment.
•
Providing medical supplies (such as bandages or medications) to a forward aid station.
•
Personnel providing direct medical care – triaging, administering aid, and performing surgery.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**11**
**Chapter 2**
•
Carrying medical personnel (doctors, nurses, medics) solely for medical duties.
•
Self-defense: A medic carrying a personal sidearm for protection while treating patients.
2-38. Utilizing the protection of the Red Cross emblem for other actions is illegal. Violations will result in
loss of protected status and can be a potential war crime. Examples of prohibited actions are:
•
Transporting ammunition or weapons alongside patients.
•
Using the platform to scout enemy positions or relay tactical information.
•
Carrying combat troops to a forward operating base.
•
Employing medical personnel in combat roles (such as a medic firing a weapon offensively).
•
Providing medical care as a pretext to gain access to enemy-held territory for intelligence
gathering.
•
Using a marked vehicle as a command post directing combat operations.
2-39. Ground and rotary-wing CASEVAC platforms are generally unmarked and lack legal protection. If
protected status is sought, strict adherence to the restrictions below is required, including disarmament.
Fixed-wing aircraft and maritime vessels face even stricter rules, advance agreement from belligerents is
typically needed for aircraft protection, and only specifically designated and permanently marked hospital
ships qualify for maritime protection. Specific restrictions for ground vehicles include:
•
No Offensive Mounts: Ground vehicles displaying the Red Cross cannot have any mounted
weapons systems (such as machine guns or grenade launchers). These must be removed before
marking.
•
Configuration: Vehicles should primarily be configured for medical use.
•
Cargo Restrictions: Cargo space must be dedicated to medical supplies and equipment. No non-
medical cargo (rations, fuel, and spare parts unrelated to medical maintenance) is permitted.
•
Route Adherence: While not a strict legal requirement, adhering to established and publicized
evacuation routes can reinforce the perception of neutrality and reduce risk.
•
No Escort: Marked medical vehicles should generally avoid traveling with armed escorts, as this
can undermine their protected status. If an escort is deemed necessary for security, it must maintain
a clear separation and not appear to be actively protecting the vehicle from attack.
•
Signage Visibility: Red Cross markings must be clearly visible from all angles and maintained in
good condition.
2-40. Commanders are responsible for understanding and enforcing these regulations. Oversight and
discipline are crucial to prevent misuse. Markings must be removed immediately upon mission completion.
Proper use of medical markings protects personnel, preserves legal protections, and upholds the Army’s
ethical standards. When in doubt, consult with a judge advocate.

### EVACUATIONROUTES

2-41. CASEVAC planners identify and deconflict movement routes to evacuate casualties safely from the
POI to an MTF. These routes are not labeled or controlled separately from other movement routes, but they
are coordinated to avoid conflict with maneuver operations, fires, and sustainment traffic. Coordination with
higher headquarters and joint partners supports timely movement, preserves operational tempo, and reduces
risk to evacuation platforms and patients.
2-42. Nonmedical platforms may be used when MEDEVAC is not available. Trucks or civilian vehicles can
transport casualties who do not require urgent medical care. However, these efforts are planned to avoid
interference with ongoing operations. Commanders synchronize these missions with sustainment and
maneuver plans to preserve tempo and safety.
2-43. In rare cases, commanders may coordinate temporary evacuation routes with belligerent forces during
negotiated pauses or humanitarian operations. If such routes are used, they are reserved strictly for medical
purposes. Using them to move troops, weapons, or supplies violates the LOAC, forfeits their protected status,
and endangers future humanitarian coordination. While uncommon in large-scale combat, these scenarios
may occur in stability operations or complex multinational environments and require close legal and
operational oversight.
**12**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation During Operations**
2-44. Using evacuation corridors for medical purposes reinforces compliance with international law. It also
protects medical personnel, promotes limited trust between opposing forces, and helps maintain access for
future humanitarian efforts. Clear command oversight preserves legal protections and supports the legitimacy
of the mission.

### DETAINEE ANDCIVILIANCASUALTYEVACUATIONCONSIDERATIONS

2-45. Effective CASEVAC planning accounts for detainees, including prisoners of war, and civilian
casualties. Under the Geneva Conventions and the LOAC, detainees and civilians are entitled to medical care
based on medical need, without adverse distinction. Commanders are responsible for ensuring that detainees
receive care equivalent to that provided to friendly forces. Likewise, civilians in the operational area must
have access to emergency care consistent with international law and Army values. For more information on
civilian considerations for medical treatment, refer to ATP 4-02.55.
2-46. Commanders balance ethical obligations with mission security. While detainees must receive necessary
medical support, evacuation operations must also prevent security risks such as escape or compromise of
operational security. This balance becomes more complex in joint or multinational operations, where
detainees may transit shared CASEVAC platforms or multinational treatment facilities. For more information
on medical support to detainee operations, refer to ATP 4-02.46.
2-47. In multinational operations, CASEVAC planners often coordinate with partner forces to share
evacuation assets and medical treatment capabilities. This includes aligning with partner-nation policies,
identifying shared medical treatment facilities, and preparing to treat civilians who may enter the area of
operations during combat. Coordination with local leaders and host-nation medical personnel helps maintain
safe zones and manage disruptions. These efforts support compliance with LOAC, strengthen ethical conduct,
and preserve mission legitimacy in complex environments.

### COORDINATION WITHENEMIES FORCASUALTYMOVEMENT

2-48. At times, commanders may coordinate with enemy forces to evacuate casualties. This may occur during
temporary ceasefires or be facilitated through third parties, such as the International Committee of the Red
Cross. These actions reflect the Army’s adherence to international law and its moral obligation to provide
humane care.
2-49. Although working with adversaries for medical purposes may seem unconventional, it demonstrates
professionalism and discipline. Saving lives and protecting dignity remain central to military values. These
actions show that the Army can uphold ethical standards even during combat.

## ARMY CASUALTY EVACUATION PLANNING IN JOINT AND

## MULTINATIONAL CONTEXTS

2-50. Army forces often operate as part of a joint or multinational force, and CASEVAC planning accounts
for coordination across components and nations. Commanders and staff ensure Army CASEVAC
requirements are integrated into higher-level sustainment and HSS plans, especially when relying on
nonorganic capabilities such as partner-nation MTFs or evacuation platforms. For joint-level planning
considerations on patient movement, including CASEVAC, refer to JP 4-02.
2-51. In coalition operations, Army CASEVAC planning aligns with host-nation or allied support systems.
This includes identifying available platforms, understanding treatment capabilities, and establishing linkages
with local medical systems. For example, in the Indo-Pacific and Europe, Army units may rely on host-nation
MTFs under bilateral agreements or NATO frameworks. For operational examples and planning
considerations, refer to ATP 4-02.55.
2-52. Nonstandard platforms, including partner-nation vehicles, civilian transports, watercraft, and aircraft,
may support Army CASEVAC when dedicated platforms are unavailable. Army commanders assess risks,
adapt platforms for safe movement, and coordinate approvals with higher headquarters when operating in a
joint environment.
2-53. Differences in platform design, communications systems, and medical protocols can complicate
multinational CASEVAC. Army units mitigate these challenges through early coordination, familiarization
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**13**
**Chapter 2**
training, and by following established joint and multinational procedures. Combined exercises give Army
CASEVAC teams critical understanding of partner capabilities and constraints, which improves integration
in operations.

## DISTINCTIONS BETWEEN ARMY, JOINT CASUALTY

## EVACUATION AND MULTI-MODAL PATIENT MOVEMENT

2-54. Army and joint doctrine use different terms and criteria for casualty evacuation. Recognizing the
distinctions helps avoid confusion and supports unified efforts across components. Commanders understand
these differences to ensure proper coordination during operations.
2-55. In Army doctrine, CASEVAC includes moving casualties on nonmedical platforms, which may or may
not include medical personnel or en route care. When medical support is present, but platforms remain
nonmedical, the operation remains CASEVAC. This flexibility supports rapid action when MEDEVAC is
not available.
2-56. Joint doctrine separates CASEVAC, MEDEVAC, and multi-modal patient movement (MMPM) based
on platform type and availability of en route care. CASEVAC refers to unregulated transport without medical
personnel. MMPM bridges CASEVAC and MEDEVAC, involving regulated or unregulated movement with
en route care on non-dedicated platforms. MEDEVAC remains the regulated use of dedicated, marked
platforms with standardized medical support.
2-57. The primary difference is that Army CASEVAC may include en route care, while joint CASEVAC
does not. MMPM fills this gap, allowing medical care to continue on improvised platforms. Using the correct
doctrinal term improves coordination and helps the patient receives the appropriate level of support.
2-58. MMPM involves diverse transportation methods, including aircraft, ships, rail, and ground convoys, to
move casualties when dedicated MEDEVAC is limited. These movements require coordination with
sustainment and protection elements but expand the options available to commanders in large-scale or
extended operations. Table 2-1 on page 15 compares Army and joint doctrinal approaches to CASEVAC and
MMPM, highlighting differences in platform use, medical support, and regulatory frameworks.
**Note.** Table 2-1 is for Army doctrinal awareness and reference only. Joint definitions are governed
by JP 4-02 and related publications.
