## CASUALTY EVACUATION IN MULTIDOMAIN AND CONTESTED

## ENVIRONMENTS

2-59. The Army plans CASEVAC operations differently depending on the operational context, even though
all operations occur across multiple domains. This section explains how CASEVAC supports operations in
contested, degraded, and large-scale combat conditions. It emphasizes the need for flexibility,
interoperability, and survivability by providing planning considerations for each domain.
2-60. Army and joint forces operate across air, land, maritime, space, and cyberspace to complete missions
and maintain combat power. CASEVAC facilitates this by transporting WII personnel to medical treatment,
allowing them to RTD or continue with the evacuation. CASEVAC supports these efforts by integrating
capabilities across domains to improve speed, security, flexibility, and accountability.
2-61. Each domain contributes to CASEVAC success. Air platforms move casualties over long distances.
Land forces secure ground routes, establish CCPs, and provide transport when air access is limited or
unavailable. Maritime forces, including joint and Army watercraft, support evacuation in coastal or river
environments. Space systems assist with navigation, communication, and tracking. Cyberspace provides
secure communications and supports mission command. CASEVAC planners address cyber threats to protect
casualty data and keep operations moving.
2-62. CASEVAC teams leverage strengths across domains to overcome complex threats and maintain
casualty movement even when the environment is contested. They plan across all domains to keep the force
ready, maintain care continuity, and preserve combat power, even under pressure or in dispersed areas.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**15**
**Chapter 2**

### CASUALTYEVACUATION ANDLARGE-SCALECOMBATOPERATIONS

2-63. CASEVAC strengthens morale and combat effectiveness during large-scale combat operations. Rapid
evacuation reduces strain on Soldiers and helps maintain tempo and maneuver when conditions allow. Even
when commanders cannot prioritize CASEVAC due to mission constraints, early planning and coordination
help reduce delays and manage expectations. Integrating CASEVAC into the operational plan demonstrates
a leader’s commitment to preserving the force and sustaining unit cohesion under fire.
2-64. CASEVAC also acts as a strategic signal. It demonstrates Army readiness, reassures partners, and
shows that operations can continue despite threats. In both active combat and stability efforts, well-executed
CASEVAC contributes to mission success and reinforces the Army’s ability to respond to threats across
domains.

### ADAPTINGCASUALTYEVACUATION TOCONTESTEDENVIRONMENTS

2-65. CASEVAC planners anticipate contested environments where joint capabilities are disrupted or denied.
Loss of air superiority, degraded communications, and blocked routes challenge traditional CASEVAC.
Teams build flexible plans that use alternate transport, decentralized support, and pre-coordinated procedures
to operate under fire or across long distances.
2-66. Leaders adapt CASEVAC by using the strengths of each domain. Ground forces may secure terrain or
use wheeled platforms when aircraft are not available. Maritime forces may use watercraft to evacuate from
island or river areas. Planners include backup command systems to handle cyber threats or jamming. Frequent
rehearsals and coordination allow CASEVAC to continue even when systems fail or the situation changes.

### THEROLE OFINFORMATIONCOLLECTION ANDTARGETING INCASUALTYEVACUATION

2-67. Information collection and targeting support CASEVAC planning by improving situational awareness.
While dedicated surveillance assets may not be prioritized for evacuation missions, commanders use
available intelligence to understand enemy threats, assess terrain, and select safer routes. When possible,
planners coordinate with staff to align CASEVAC timing with periods of reduced threat or increased
intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance coverage. Even limited access to predictive information helps
reduce risk and supports more effective casualty movement.
2-68. CASEVAC planners also link with targeting efforts. By coordinating fire support or suppression along
CASEVAC routes, units reduce risks to evacuation teams. Intelligence-led CASEVAC increases
survivability and allows medical support to continue in high-threat areas.

## THREAT-BASED CHALLENGES TO CASUALTY EVACUATION

## INTEGRATION

2-69. Enemy forces often target CASEVAC operations to delay care and disrupt force preservation. Long-
range fires, cyberattacks, and disinformation campaigns can strike evacuation routes, deceive friendly forces,
or damage medical networks. CASEVAC is planned as part of the protection warfighting function and
synchronized with maneuver, sustainment, and communications efforts. Planning includes incorporating
deception, route masking, redundant communication systems, and rapid route adaptation.
2-70. During large-scale combat operations, the wide dispersion of forces increases the time and distance
required to evacuate casualties. Reliance on joint assets such as air or reconnaissance platforms may be
disrupted by enemy anti-access capabilities. When external support is unavailable, dispersed formations
execute CASEVAC independently. Commanders plan CASEVAC as an organic task at every echelon, with
emergency procedures and ground evacuation routes rehearsed and validated in advance.
2-71. CASEVAC teams may be forced to operate in areas under fire or contaminated by chemical, biological,
radiological, and nuclear (CBRN) effects. Enemy use of massed fires or weapons of mass destruction can
overwhelm medical resources and temporarily block evacuation routes. Planners reduce this risk by
decentralizing CCPs, dispersing CASEVAC assets, using terrain for protection, and incorporate alternate
routes and force packages into the CASEVAC plan. CASEVAC remains viable, even under direct threat or
MASCAL conditions.
**16**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation During Operations**
2-72. Gaps and seams in fast-moving operations increases risk for CASEVAC. Bypassed enemy elements or
unsecured terrain may disrupt casualty flow. Follow-on forces are tasked to secure evacuation routes as part
of the operational framework. In strategic environments, adversaries may attack airfields, ports, or transit
points to disrupt the movement of CASEVAC platforms and medical personnel. CASEVAC planning spans
from the tactical edge to the strategic rear, accounting for both forward and rear-area threats.

## SYNCHRONIZING CASUALTY EVACUATION THROUGH THE

## WARFIGHTING FUNCTIONS

2-73. A warfighting function is a group of tasks and systems united by a common purpose that commanders
use to accomplish missions and training objectives (ADP 3-0). The Army organizes operations through six
warfighting functions, C2, movement and maneuver, intelligence, fires, sustainment, and protection.
CASEVAC does not stand alone as a separate function. Instead, it supports the mission through integration
with each of these areas. Each function helps plan, coordinate, and execute CASEVAC in a way that protects
combat power and keeps units ready to fight.

### COMMAND ANDCONTROL

2-74. The command and control warfighting function is the related tasks and a system that enable
commanders to exercise authority and direction to accomplish missions (ADP 3-0). C2 allows commanders
to direct and manage CASEVAC operations across all echelons. Through C2 systems, leaders issue
evacuation orders, set priorities, and maintain communication with evacuation teams, even under high-threat
conditions. A reliable C2 network connects CASEVAC actions to the commander’s intent and keeps
evacuation efforts aligned with mission goals. It also supports coordination between units and allows timely
adjustments to shifting conditions or threats.

### MOVEMENT ANDMANEUVER

2-75. The movement and maneuver warfighting function is the related tasks and systems that move and
employ forces to achieve a position of relative advantage with respect to the enemy (ADP 3-0). Movement
and maneuver functions position CASEVAC within the flow of operations. Commanders incorporate
evacuation routes and CCPs into maneuver plans to keep them accessible and responsive. CASEVAC teams
operate with or near combat elements so they can reach casualties without delay. By synchronizing
evacuation with tactical movement, units maintain casualty support as the mission tempo shifts or formations
reposition during battle.

### INTELLIGENCE

2-76. The intelligence warfighting function is the related tasks and systems that facilitate understanding the
enemy, terrain, weather, civil considerations, and other significant aspects of the operational environment
(ADP 3-0). Intelligence helps CASEVAC planners understand where, when, and how casualties may occur.
It identifies likely threat areas, predicts casualty-producing events, and monitors enemy activity that could
impact evacuation routes. Intelligence also provides real-time updates that help commanders adjust
CASEVAC plans as the battlefield evolves. By using reconnaissance and surveillance systems, units improve
route selection and reduce risks to CASEVAC teams.

### FIRES

2-77. The fires warfighting function is the related tasks and systems that create and converge effects in all

### domains against the threat to enable operations across the range of military operations (ADP 3-0). Fires

support CASEVAC by shaping the battlefield and suppressing enemy threats along evacuation routes.
Commanders use fires to create safe windows for air or ground movement by neutralizing or deterring enemy
interference. Fires also help CASEVAC teams reach contested areas and return safely. When coordinated
with CASEVAC movements, fires protect WII personnel and allow medical teams to operate under combat
conditions.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**17**
**Chapter 2**

### SUSTAINMENT

2-78. The sustainment warfighting function is the related tasks and systems that provide support and services
to enable freedom of action, extend operational reach, and prolong endurance (ADP 3-0). Sustainment
integrates CASEVAC platforms, personnel, and equipment into ongoing operations. Units prepare by pre-
positioning medical supplies, maintaining vehicles, and including CASEVAC in convoy and logistics
planning. Sustainment efforts also cover fuel, maintenance, and medical resupply for evacuation missions.
When commanders embed CASEVAC into sustainment planning, they extend operational reach and keep
the force ready during prolonged engagements.

### PROTECTION

2-79. The protection warfighting function is the related tasks, systems, and methods that prevent or mitigate
detection, threat effects, and hazards to preserve the force, deny the enemy freedom of action, and enable
commanders to apply combat power (ADP 3-0). Protection tasks defend CASEVAC teams, assets, and
evacuation routes from enemy threats. Units use route security, deception, cyber defense, and electromagnetic
discipline to reduce the risk of detection or attack. These measures help CASEVAC operations continue even
under fire. Commanders coordinate protection efforts to limit risk and keep evacuation missions moving
during combat.

## INTEGRATING CASUALTY EVACUATION SUPPORT INTO

## COMBAT OPERATIONS

2-80. CASEVAC supports the sustainment and protection warfighting functions by preserving the force and
reducing preventable deaths. Removing WII Soldiers from the battlefield helps maintain readiness and
combat effectiveness. CASEVAC also boosts morale, when troops trust that they will be evacuated, they
fight with more confidence and stay engaged longer. These effects contribute to overall combat power by
helping commanders maintain tempo and retain effective fighting strength across the formation.
2-81. Commanders should not treat CASEVAC as a stand-alone operation or isolated task. Instead, they
direct CASEVAC as a supporting task at echelon that integrates across all warfighting functions. Effective
CASEVAC planning strengthens tempo, survivability, and endurance. The following examples demonstrate
how CASEVAC contributes to each warfighting function:
•
Command and control: Leaders integrate CASEVAC into planning, mission orders, and risk
assessments.
•
Movement and maneuver: Units use CASEVAC routes and platforms to reposition casualties while
maintaining operational momentum.
•
Intelligence: Accurate casualty reporting supports decision-making and real-time coordination
across echelons.
•
Fires: Suppressive fires reduce threats during evacuation and protect casualty collection points.
•
Sustainment: CASEVAC links casualties to medical treatment and preserves the fighting strength
of the force.
•
Protection: Units safeguard medics, nonmedical platforms, and casualties through security and force
protection measures.
2-82. Commanders and staffs plan, coordinate, and execute CASEVAC as part of joint, multinational, and
multidomain operations. When leaders integrate CASEVAC across all warfighting functions, they help
preserve combat power, maintain tempo, and treat casualties with discipline and care. Effective CASEVAC
planning demonstrates readiness, builds trust, and reflects a commander’s commitment to the force.
2-83. Understanding how CASEVAC supports operations across domains and units prepares leaders for
deliberate planning. Because CASEVAC connects to tasks at every echelon, commanders align evacuation
efforts with mission goals, resources, and tempo. Chapter 3 builds on this foundation by describing how
leaders plan CASEVAC to meet tactical, operational, and strategic demands. It explains how coordination,
asset use, and mission variables shape CASEVAC planning in both routine and high-threat situations.
**18**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**

### Chapter 3

## Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations

CASEVAC is an integral part of mission planning and is coordinated alongside other
operational requirements at every level. Commanders and staffs include evacuation needs early
in the planning process by assigning responsibilities and coordinating available assets across
all warfighting functions. This chapter explains how units plan CASEVAC using the military
decision-making process (MDMP) or troop leading procedures (TLP), while considering
mission variables and medical infrastructure. It also demonstrates how joint, interagency, and
multinational factors influence planning and highlights the need to match CASEVAC with the
operation’s tempo, phases, and risk levels.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION PLANNING

3-1. Commanders and staffs plan CASEVAC to support operations across joint, multinational, and
interagency environments. Effective planning keeps evacuation efforts synchronized from the theater
strategic level to the tactical level. Joint and multinational operations require additional coordination to
overcome interoperability gaps. Coordination within the theater, with interagency partners such as the
Department of State, host-nation governments, and nongovernmental organizations helps secure access to
medical facilities, routes, and logistics support. Early integration allows forces to align resources, avoid
duplication, and improve casualty care at every level. CASEVAC planning is fully integrated into the scheme
of support and reflected in Annex C (Operations) and Annex F (Sustainment) of the operations order to ensure
visibility and unity of effort across all warfighting functions.
3-2. To keep CASEVAC planning aligned across echelons, theater strategic planners create the structure,
operational planners connect the systems, and tactical teams execute the mission. This alignment supports
the larger operation, protects the force, and keeps momentum during joint and multinational missions.
Planners include CASEVAC triggers, timing, and coordination measures in unit-level and combined arms
rehearsals, ensuring that all participants from the POI to the MTF understand their role in the evacuation
chain.

### THEATERSTRATEGICLEVELPLANNING

3-3. At the theater strategic level, geographic combatant commands, theater special operations commands,
and supporting Army Service component commands develop policies, allocate resources, and build the
foundation for joint and multinational CASEVAC support. These headquarters coordinate with national and
host-nation medical authorities, including Ministries of Health, nongovernmental organizations, and coalition
partners, to integrate medical support across the theater. CASEVAC planning at this level fits within broader
joint, interagency, intergovernmental, and multinational efforts to maintain unity of effort and freedom of
movement for patient evacuation.
3-4. Theater strategic planning aligns key resources, such as evacuation platforms, trained personnel, and
medical supplies, with mission demands. Planners also coordinate access to host-nation and partner MTFs.
When necessary, they identify alternative platforms like amphibious craft or naval vessels to expand
evacuation options and maintain operational flexibility. They also direct the establishment of a theater-wide
communications plan for CASEVAC that links strategic, operational, and tactical echelons, accounting for
potential degraded or denied communications environments.
3-5. Leaders at this level develop shared SOPs that guide medical priorities, casualty tracking, and
communication procedures. These SOPs improve interoperability and reduce delays caused by different
equipment, policies, or standards across partner forces.
3-6. Formal agreements with coalition partners allow units to share medical facilities, logistics networks, and
CASEVAC assets. These agreements are established before operations begin to support seamless integration
during execution.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**19**
**Chapter 3**
3-7. Civil affairs personnel support theater strategic planners by identifying local medical infrastructure,
assessing host-nation treatment capabilities, and advising on legal, cultural, and ethical considerations. Their
coordination with Ministries of Health, local hospitals, and international relief organizations informs the
command’s ability to integrate host-nation support into CASEVAC planning. This collaboration improves
situational awareness and supports the broader strategic medical architecture in coalition and multinational
environments.

### OPERATIONALLEVELPLANNING

3-8. Operational planning connects theater strategic goals to mission execution by developing CASEVAC
procedures suited to the specific theater. joint task forces, land component commands, or corps headquarters
serving in those roles synchronize CASEVAC across units, adapt to conditions on the ground, and position
resources to support maneuver and sustainment. When no field Army is present, the corps may serve as the
bridge between operational and tactical levels. In all cases, the division remains the Army’s primary tactical
formation.
3-9. Commanders and staffs at the operational level build shared understanding across echelons and assign
responsibilities for casualty movement. While CCPs are executed at the tactical level, operational planners
coordinate the flow of casualties from CCPs to ambulance exchange points (AXPs) and MTFs by aligning
transportation assets, route security, and handover timelines. This includes synchronizing ground and air
corridors, establishing medical regulating priorities, and ensuring communication interoperability between
units. They also establish reporting standards and account for joint and multinational procedural differences
to create a cohesive CASEVAC architecture. An ambulance exchange point is a location where a patient is
transferred from one ambulance to another en route to a medical treatment facility. Also called AXP
(ATP 4-02.2). Operational-level CASEVAC planning explicitly integrates protection tasks, including convoy
escort, route clearance, and air defense coverage, to mitigate risks to evacuation platforms in contested
environments.
3-10. Planners position Role 3 MTFs and medical staging areas to deliver timely care and support extended
operations. They do this by assessing anticipated casualty flow, terrain access, and evacuation timelines, then
selecting sites that balance proximity to supported units with security from threat activity. Planners oversee
patient tracking by integrating reporting systems, coordinate with regulating authorities to match patient
needs to available facilities; and forecast medical logistics requirements to sustain casualty movement. This
includes fuel planning, Class VIII distribution, and coordinating CASEVAC platform availability with
supporting medical units. They also coordinate with the medical regulating officer to align patient movement
control processes with operational priorities, ensuring that casualties are delivered to the appropriate facility
at the right time based on clinical urgency and available capacity.
3-11. A detailed understanding of terrain, weather, enemy activity, and infrastructure damage informs
operational CASEVAC planning. Planners incorporate current intelligence updates, engineer assessments,
and weather forecasts to refine route selection and evacuation timing. Risk assessments drive the
development of alternate routes, protected corridors, and backup medical sites, enabling units to adapt to
changing threats and maintain continuity of casualty movement.
3-12. Working with host-nation and interagency partners strengthens evacuation plans by expanding
available resources and access points. Civil affairs personnel and allied forces help identify available medical
resources facilities, staging areas, and transportation assets, and assist in deconflicting evacuation routes with
civilian and coalition activities. This coordination often involves liaison officers, joint planning sessions, and
combined rehearsals to improve access and increase care options for WII personnel.
3-13. Some operational environments require CASEVAC plans to include riverine or maritime capabilities.
Planners assess waterway routes for navigability, security, and connection to inland evacuation networks.
Amphibious craft, naval platforms, and riverboats are integrated into the CASEVAC plan through joint
coordination with naval or maritime components, ensuring seamless casualty movement between land and
water systems.

### TACTICALLEVELPLANNING: REDUNDANCY ANDRESILIENCE

3-14. CASEVAC is primarily a tactical-level task conducted by company- and battalion-level units. These
formations are responsible for moving casualties from the POI to the nearest MTF, often through CCPs.
**20**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
Above the battalion level, casualty movement typically involves MEDEVAC or regulated transport
coordinated by higher headquarters. In large-scale operations, higher echelons may support tactical
CASEVAC indirectly by providing additional nonmedical vehicles or coordinating transport between MTFs
when MEDEVAC capabilities are overwhelmed.
3-15. To plan CASEVAC at the tactical level, commanders and planners understand the difference between
redundancy and resilience. These terms get used interchangeably sometimes, but they’re distinctly different,
and understanding that difference impacts how we build a robust and survivable operation.
•
Redundancy is about having backups. It’s about duplicating critical systems or capabilities so that
if one fails, another can immediately take over. Think of it like having two identical radio systems
–if one goes down, you switch to the other. It’s a direct, one-to-one replacement. Redundancy
prevents mission failure from a single point of failure.
•
Resilience is about the ability to recover from disruption. It's about building a system that can absorb
shocks, adapt to changing circumstances, and continue functioning, even if degraded, in the face of
adversity. It’s not necessarily about having a direct replacement; it’s about having flexibility and
adaptability to work around the problem.
3-16. Redundancy in CASEVAC requires building multiple identical systems and depends on manpower,
funding, and logistical support. Some examples of redundancy in CASEVAC are:
•
Multiple Evacuation Platforms: Having both dedicated helicopters and ground assets available. If
the airspace is compromised, we can switch to ground transport. This is a direct one-to-one
replacement.
•
Alternate landing zone (LZ): Pre-planning and designating multiple LZs for each potential CCP. If
one LZ is compromised by enemy fire or terrain, we have others ready to go.
•
Backup Communication Systems: Having both primary and secondary radio frequencies, satellite
communication capabilities, and even pre-arranged signal plans (visual signals, runners) in case
electronic communications fail.
•
Duplicate Medical Kits: Ensuring each Soldier, combat lifesaver (CLS), medic and CCP has a fully
stocked medical kit with essential life-saving equipment.
•
Multiple Qualified Personnel: Having more than one qualified personnel at each CCP or on each
evacuation team. If one is incapacitated, another can immediately take over.
3-17. Resilience in CASEVAC, while still requiring investment, focuses on building adaptable systems,
empowering decentralized decision-making, and fostering a culture of innovation. Some examples of
resilience in CASEVAC are:
•
Decentralized Casualty Collection: Empowering unit leaders and medics at the POI to provide initial
stabilization and treatment, rather than relying solely on centralized CCPs. This reduces the strain
on the evacuation system and allows for quicker intervention.
•
Cross-Training: Ensuring personnel are cross-trained in multiple skills (such as a medic also trained
in radio operation, a vehicle crew member trained in tactical combat casualty care (TCCC). This
increases flexibility and allows personnel to fill gaps as needed.
•
Networked Information System with Offline Capability: Having a digital medical tracking system
while ensuring medics can record and transmit casualty information using paper-based systems if
the network is down.
•
Local Partnerships: Establishing relationships with local medical facilities, such as host-nation
hospitals, civilian clinics, to augment the evacuation capacity if needed. This requires pre-planning
and coordination.
•
Adaptive Routing: Developing the capability to dynamically reroute evacuation assets based on real-
time intelligence, avoiding known enemy positions, congested areas, or hazardous terrain. This
requires robust situational awareness and flexible C2.
•
Improvised Evacuation Techniques: Training personnel to utilize available assets, civilian vehicles,
local transportation, for emergency evacuation if traditional CASEVAC platforms are unavailable.
This requires risk assessment and clear guidelines.
3-18. Redundancy and resilience are most effective when used together in a layered approach. This creates a
more robust and survivable CASEVAC system capable of withstanding a wider range of disruptions. This
layered approach recognizes that a single point of failure is rarely the sole threat; rather, operations are often
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**21**
**Chapter 3**
impacted by a cascade of events. Tactical CASEVAC planning also identifies sustainment interdependencies,
including fuel, maintenance, and Class IX repair parts for evacuation platforms, and synchronizes them with
the unit’s distribution plan.
3-19. This combined approach shifts the focus from simply preventing failure (redundancy) to proactively
managing the consequences of failure (resilience) while simultaneously mitigating potential vulnerabilities.
It necessitates a shift in mindset, from a rigid adherence to pre-planned procedures to a more adaptable and
improvisational approach. Key factors of a combined approach are:
•
Prioritize Criticality: Identify the most critical elements of the CASEVAC system, such as qualified
medical personnel, communication networks. Focus redundancy and resilience efforts specifically
on these critical areas to enhance overall operational effectiveness.
•
Diversify Assets: Avoid relying on a single asset or method for any critical function. Diversification
reduces vulnerability to specific threats.
•
Empower Decentralized Decision-Making: Grant lower-echelon leaders the authority to adapt to
changing circumstances. Additionally, provide training to ensure they can make informed decisions
regarding CASEVAC operations.
•
Continuous Assessment and Adaptation: Regularly evaluate the effectiveness of CASEVAC
procedures. Use lessons learned and evolving threats to inform necessary adjustments and
improvements.
•
Integration with Overall Operational Planning: CASEVAC planning is integrated with overall
operational planning to maintain seamless coordination and support. This integration is essential for
providing effective support during operations.
3-20. In large-scale combat operations, even well-prepared patrols can face sudden disruptions that challenge
casualty evacuation efforts. The following scenario illustrates how an ambush against a squad-sized element
highlights the risks of relying on a single method of communication and evacuation. Employing a combined
approach, integrating redundancy, resilience, and coordination, can mean the difference between delayed
treatment with higher casualty rate and timely evacuation that preserves combat power.

### Scenario: A Squad-Sized Patrol is Ambushed Resulting in

### Multiple Casualties.

**Casualty Evacuation**
**(CASEVAC) without a**
**combined approach**
**CASEVAC with a combined approach**
Redundancy: The patrol carries both a primary and secondary radio and
has pre-designated alternate landing zone (LZ) identified. A secondary
team is on standby with a ground CASEVAC or MEDEVAC.
Resilience: The patrol leader, trained in tactical combat casualty care
(TCCC), immediately directs medics to provide life-saving interventions.
Recognizing the jammed radio frequency, the leader utilizes a pre-
arranged visual signal to alert a nearby observation post, which relays the
MEDEVAC request via a different communication channel.
Simultaneously, the leader assesses the terrain and prepares the alternate
LZ.
The patrol relies on a pre-
planned medical evacuation
(MEDEVAC) request using
a single radio frequency.
The frequency is jammed
by the enemy. The patrol
lacks alternate
communication methods
and is unable to request
assistance, resulting in
delayed evacuation and
increased casualties.
Combined Effect: Despite the disruption to primary communication, the
patrol successfully requests assistance, stabilizes casualties, and prepares
for evacuation. The MEDEVAC helicopter, guided by the observation post,
lands at the alternate LZ and evacuates the wounded. The standby ground
teams are prepared to support if airspace remains contested.
3-21. In the example above, redundancy provided alternate communication and LZ options. Resilience
supported through training, decentralized decision-making, and adaptability, enabled the patrol to overcome
the communication disruption and continue the evacuation process.
3-22. A truly effective CASEVAC system requires a holistic approach that builds both redundancy and
resilience. By using this approach, commanders can significantly improve the survivability of their forces

**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
and support the timely and effective evacuation of casualties, even in the most challenging operational
environments.

### CASUALTYEVACUATIONPLANNINGCONSIDERATIONS

3-23. Planning for CASEVAC is most effective when commanders and staffs link strategic, operational, and
tactical considerations into a unified plan that can adapt to battlefield realities. The considerations outlined
in table 3-1 build on the tenets discussed in chapter 1, providing planners with a quick-reference tool to
address terrain, threat, resources, coordination requirements, and contingency measures. These
considerations help synchronize CASEVAC with maneuver, sustainment, and protection functions while
integrating redundancy and resilience. Using the table during the MDMP and rehearsals supports unity of
effort, reduces friction between echelons, and improves the survivability of casualties and evacuation teams.

**Considerations**

Identify primary and alternate CASEVAC routes; integrate with maneuver and
sustainment overlays; plan for fording, route clearance, or other mobility support.

Transfer Points
Confirm CCPs, ALPs, AXPs, and LRPs are integrated with the ambulance shuttle
system when used.
Coordination with
Maneuver
Synchronize CASEVAC overlay with maneuver graphics and fire support plans;
confirm security measures for evacuation routes in contested areas.

Sustainment
Align CASEVAC with Class VIII resupply, refuel-on-the-move points, and support
area displacement plans.

Establish triggers for MASCAL activation; designate augmentation assets;
integrate MASCAL drills into rehearsals and battle drills.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION PLANNING WITHIN THE OPERATIONS

## PROCESS

3-24. CASEVAC planning occurs as part of every mission, beginning with mission analysis and extending
through orders production. Commanders and staffs identify CASEVAC requirements early and address them
through coordinated input across the warfighting functions, particularly protection, sustainment, and C2.
CASEVAC is not a stand-alone medical task, it is a shared staff responsibility that supports unit readiness
and operational tempo.
3-25. Commanders at all echelons are responsible for moving casualties to the point where regulated medical
treatment can occur. Company- and platoon-level elements typically conduct CASEVAC to designated CCP,
AXP, or MTF. When evacuation requirements exceed available evacuation capabilities, both CASEVAC and
MEDEVAC, higher-level commanders coordinate for additional CASEVAC support. MEDEVAC remains
the regulated movement of casualties by dedicated and medically staffed platforms. CASEVAC uses
nonmedical vehicles or aircraft, often under austere conditions, when MEDEVAC is unavailable, delayed, or
tactically unsuitable.
3-26. The mission variables directly shape CASEVAC planning. Planners assess terrain for route selection,
consider weather impacts on ground and air movement, and evaluate enemy threats to CASEVAC operations.
They should also identify and mitigate gaps in evacuation coverage. CASEVAC planning aligns with the
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**23**
**Chapter 3**
commander’s concept of operations and integrate redundancy, emergency procedures, and prioritization
schemes, especially in MASCAL scenarios or contested environments.

## OPERATIONAL ENABLERS FOR CASUALTY EVACUATION

## SUPPORT

3-27. CASEVAC depends on reliable movement, communication, and coordination. Terrain can delay or
block evacuation routes, while weather affects visibility, airlift availability, and ground mobility. Electronic
warfare, jamming, and degraded network conditions can disrupt C2 during evacuation. These operational
factors are assessed during planning to position CASEVAC assets, designate alternate routes, and maintain
communications across formations.
3-28. Cyberspace operations can disrupt or degrade CASEVAC efforts by targeting communication nodes,
patient tracking systems, or GPS-enabled navigation. Commanders and staffs plan for cyber and electronic
warfare threats by establishing redundant communication channels, using pre-coordinated signal plans, and
validating backup procedures. These protective measures help units sustain CASEVAC operations in
complex and contested environments.

## PLANNING CASUALTY EVACUATION AT ECHELON

3-29. Casualty evacuation is a task conducted at echelon to move WII personnel from the POI to the next
role of care. Commanders deliberately plan CASEVAC to align with their scheme of maneuver and expected
operational conditions. CASEVAC is often the primary method of evacuation when MEDEVAC is
unavailable, delayed, or tactically unsuitable. Planning includes selecting and securing CCPs, identifying
available evacuation platforms, determining equipment requirements such as litters and casualty handling
gear, and establishing triage, communication, and casualty reporting procedures. It also includes identifying
and meeting training requirements for CASEVAC crews and litter teams to ensure they can perform under
operational conditions. These actions are integrated into the unit’s OPORD and rehearsed before execution.
3-30. At the company level, commanders execute CASEVAC using internal assets and guidance from higher
headquarters. They designate CCPs, supervise casualty movement under fire, and rely on noncommissioned
officers and combat medics to adjust plans during contact. Incorporating CASEVAC into battle drills builds
repetition and speed. Company-level units report casualty information upward and provide accurate location
data to support movement decisions. This local execution is the foundation of successful casualty evacuation
across the formation.
3-31. The battalion level leads tactical planning for CASEVAC. Battalion commanders identify key CCPs,
synchronize CASEVAC routes with fire support and maneuver, and determine when CASEVAC is
appropriate based on mission variables. The staff tracks casualty flow, identifies potential delays, and
coordinates nonstandard platforms such as recovery vehicles or command mobility assets. Battalion-level
planning includes direct coordination with adjacent units and supporting medical elements to support both
organic and external CASEVAC efforts. The battalion serves as the first level at which CASEVAC decisions
are evaluated against MEDEVAC availability.
3-32. At the brigade level, planners integrate CASEVAC into the sustainment and protection plans. Brigade
staff coordinate evacuation support across battalions and confirm which assets are available and when. This
includes deconflicting CASEVAC with MEDEVAC, especially during MASCAL events or when air
platforms are limited by weather, terrain, or enemy activity. The battalion or brigade operations officer (S-3)
and battalion or brigade logistics staff officer (S-4) work together to confirm availability of vehicles, recovery
teams, and logistics support to sustain prolonged or large-scale casualty movement. Brigade planners may
also coordinate casualty hand-offs with Role 2 MTFs and supporting medical companies.
3-33. Division and higher headquarters provide oversight, resourcing, and policy direction to support
subordinate CASEVAC efforts. These echelons allocate medical support units, evacuation platforms, and
coordination authorities based on the operational design. Higher headquarters may also establish CASEVAC
coordination cells or patient movement control elements as part of the medical C2 structure. At this level,
leaders shape the operational environment by integrating host-nation, joint, and coalition medical support,
expanding treatment and transport options available to lower echelons. CASEVAC planning at these levels
supports the tempo and survivability of tactical forces.
**24**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
3-34. Commanders at all levels develop a commander’s critical information requirement (CCIR) that relate
to casualty evacuation. These CCIR may include triggers for when MEDEVAC is no longer viable due to
threat, weather, or asset diversion. For example, a commander may direct a report when MEDEVAC is
unavailable for more than 30 minutes or when CASEVAC is initiated without prior coordination. These
decision points allow commanders to anticipate risks and shift between CASEVAC and MEDEVAC
appropriately. By defining CASEVAC-related CCIR during planning, leaders can respond under pressure
and maintain control over casualty movement.
3-35. CASEVAC planning begins during mission analysis and is fully integrated into the MDMP.
Commanders and their medical planners identify how CASEVAC supports the concept of operations, assess
asset availability, and define priorities of movement. The CASEVAC plan is developed alongside the HSS
plan and synchronized with logistics, fires, and maneuver. When CASEVAC is built into the operation from
the start, it becomes a deliberate supporting task, not an improvised reaction to battlefield conditions.
3-36. Staff officers across multiple warfighting functions contribute to the CASEVAC plan. The assistant
chief of staff, personnel (G-1) or the battalion or brigade personnel staff officer (S-1) track casualty estimates
and reports, providing input on casualty flow and projected evacuation needs. The assistant chief of staff,
operations (G-3) or the battalion or brigade S-3 aligns CASEVAC with the commander’s intent, scheme of
maneuver, and operational phasing. The assistant chief of staff, logistics (G-4) or the battalion or brigade S-4
links CASEVAC to logistics planning, ensuring platforms, fuel, and maintenance support are available. Staff
with technical expertise, such as air defense, engineer, or CBRN officers, assess environmental and threat
conditions that affect evacuation. Coordinated staff input helps commanders develop CASEVAC plans that
are grounded in mission realities.
3-37. CASEVAC planning also supports mission command by providing subordinate leaders with a clear
understanding of how casualty movement will occur. Plans include execution criteria, command
relationships, coordination measures, and reporting procedures. These details give junior leaders confidence
in how to act when casualties occur. In contested environments or high-casualty scenarios, the ability to
execute CASEVAC affects tempo, morale, and survivability. Commanders who make CASEVAC a visible
and rehearsed part of the operation strengthen the unit’s resilience in combat.
3-38. CASEVAC is not a substitute for MEDEVAC but complements it. Commanders understand when each
option is appropriate and build flexible plans that account for both. CASEVAC provides a critical gap-filler
when MEDEVAC is unavailable or unsuitable due to enemy activity, weather, or platform constraints.
Effective CASEVAC plans include clearly defined routes, task-organized platforms, and coordination with
receiving medical treatment facilities. These plans also identify conditions under which CASEVAC
transitions to MEDEVAC or vice versa.
3-39. CASEVAC in support of special operations presents unique challenges. These missions often cover
long distances, take place in denied terrain, and require immediate casualty movement under high risk.
Because these operations evolve quickly, conventional staffs coordinate early with special operations
planners to understand timelines, constraints, and required support. Commanders may refer to ATP 4-02.43
and TC 18-09 for detailed considerations. By planning early and clearly defining responsibilities, leaders can
support special operations forces with tailored CASEVAC options even under complex or high-threat
conditions.

### CASUALTYESTIMATES

3-40. Commanders and staff create casualty estimates early in planning to determine how WII personnel may
affect operations. These estimates guide CASEVAC planning by identifying likely evacuation demands and
helping leaders match resources to potential needs. At the Army Service Combat Command level, the G-1
leads the preparation and refinement of the casualty estimate in coordination with the surgeon and other staff
sections, then provides it to the G-3 for integration into operational planning. Surgeon sections at echelons
above brigade derive or refine medical support requirements based on updated casualty estimates. Operations
and sustainment staff use the estimate to shape timelines, movement plans, and supply requirements. As the
mission evolves, they revise the estimate and share updates across sections. This approach helps the unit
adjust CASEVAC plans, align support with expected conditions, and prepare for the medical and logistical
challenges that come with sustaining combat power.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**25**
**Chapter 3**
3-41. The staff analyzes operational and mission variables to create an accurate casualty estimate. By
assessing these factors, they can forecast the likelihood of casualties and identify challenges that may arise
during the operation. This estimate serves as the foundation for CASEVAC planning, including the placement
of evacuation resources, coordination between staff sections, and the allocation of medical supplies. A
proactive estimate helps the unit prepare to manage casualties without delay and maintain readiness
throughout the mission.
3-42. The operations staff uses the casualty estimate to determine the level of support needed for evacuating
and treating WII personnel. They assess the number of expected casualties, the types of likely injuries, and
the evacuation platforms and medical personnel required. The sustainment staff uses the same estimate to
plan for medical supply needs. They calculate the types and amounts of supplies needed for both immediate
treatment and follow-on care. By coordinating closely with the operations staff, the sustainment team
positions resources where they are most effective to support timely and continuous CASEVAC.
3-43. The staff updates the casualty estimate throughout the operation to keep it accurate and relevant. These
updates help maintain situational awareness and allow commanders and staff to adjust to changing conditions
on the ground. Keeping the estimate current informs all sections, particularly operations and sustainment, of
the impact on the force, and guides their planning. This continuous flow of information allows leaders to
make timely decisions, allocate resources, and improve coordination across the formation. Accurate estimates
also reduce confusion and help the unit respond to changing casualty conditions.
3-44. Military planners use tools such as the Joint Medical Planning Tool and the Medical Planners’ Toolkit
to develop realistic casualty estimates and align medical efforts with operational planning. These Department
of War (DOW) tools are taught through training programs like the Joint Medical Operations Course-Basic
and the Joint Medical Planning Tool Course. These courses build skills for planning CASEVAC and other
medical tasks during operations. For multinational operations, the NATO Joint Medical Planner Course
provides similar instruction tailored to joint and allied environments. These resources help planners refine
medical estimates, coordinate evacuation requirements, and prepare for medical challenges across various
missions. For more information about these tools and resources, refer to the following websites in the
reference area of this publication:
•
Joint Medical Planning Tool.
•
Defense Medical Readiness Training Institute.
•
NATO Joint Medical Planner Course.
•
Joint Medical Operations Course.

### INTEGRATINGCASUALTYEVACUATION IN THEMILITARYDECISION-MAKINGPROCESS

3-45. Casualty evacuation is not a separate mission; it is a supporting task that commanders integrate into
their operation from the start. Like sustainment and protection, CASEVAC requires layered planning at
echelon to match maneuver and maintain combat power. The MDMP offers a structured method to do this.
Commanders and staffs address CASEVAC during MDMP so that evacuation plans support tempo,
survivability, and mission execution, not just medical care.
3-46. CASEVAC integration begins during mission analysis. The assistant chief of staff, intelligence (G-2)
or battalion or brigade intelligence staff officer (S-2) and G/S-3 assess terrain, weather, enemy activity, and
friendly force structure to estimate casualty risks. The G/S-1 uses this input to project casualty numbers,
while the G/S-4 and medical planner assess evacuation platform requirements. Planners identify potential
CCP locations, evaluate route conditions, and assess how CASEVAC might affect maneuver timelines. They
also consider nonstandard or coalition platforms that could supplement organic capability. CASEVAC is
integrated with the unit’s tactical movement plan to support, not disrupt, ongoing operations. This early
analysis allows the commander to shape guidance, prioritize resources, and define CCIR related to casualty
movement.
3-47. During course of action (COA) development, the staff builds CASEVAC directly into each COA. The
G/S-3 leads this effort, working with the medical planner to align CASEVAC with tactical phases, casualty
flow estimates, and platform availability. The G/S-4 confirms which vehicles, personnel, and sustainment
resources can support evacuation tasks. The staff identifies likely CCPs and links them to both organic and
external treatment capabilities. By embedding CASEVAC in the COA, commanders can test its feasibility,
**26**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
adjust support requirements, and confirm that evacuation does not degrade maneuver or tempo. This step
prevents CASEVAC from becoming an afterthought during execution.
3-48. During COA analysis and war-gaming, the staff evaluates how each COA performs under stress,
including during MASCAL events, denied environments, or degraded communications. War-gaming tests
CASEVAC routes, time-distance factors, and casualty load capacity. War-gaming identifies terrain
chokepoints, airspace conflicts, or gaps in platform coverage. These insights inform risk mitigation, decision
support templates, and commander guidance. If CASEVAC cannot meet operational demands under a given
COA, planners revise the concept or develop branch plans to compensate.
3-49. In COA comparison, the staff evaluates each option’s ability to provide fast, secure, and sustainable
evacuation. CASEVAC is assessed alongside other sustainment and protection tasks. The recommended
COA should reflect the most responsive and executable CASEVAC plan available. If gaps remain, the
commander may direct resource shifts, adjust priorities, or refine the scheme of support before approval.
3-50. During orders production, the staff includes CASEVAC planning throughout the OPORD. Key
instructions include CCP locations, movement timelines, platform tasking, unit responsibilities, and
coordination measures with medical and nonmedical partners. CASEVAC planning also appears in the
sustainment annex and supporting tabs. These details give subordinate units the information they need to
plan, rehearse, and execute CASEVAC without delay. By integrating CASEVAC into MDMP, commanders
link casualty movement to operational success and demonstrate commitment to protecting the force.
3-51. Table 3-2 lists key MDMP products, planning actions, and staff responsibilities for CASEVAC
integration. CASEVAC should receive the same deliberate attention as fires, logistics, or communications.
Staffs treat casualty evacuation as a mission-critical task, planned early, resourced properly, and
synchronized across warfighting functions.

### CASUALTYEVACUATION ANDTROOPLEADINGPROCEDURES

3-52. Company-level units and below employ TLPs to plan and prepare missions. CASEVAC is embedded
throughout this process. Units designate primary and alternate aid and litter teams, identify evacuation
platforms, and assign casualty flow responsibilities. Leaders reinforce these responsibilities through task
organization, clear guidance, and prioritized rehearsals that include CASEVAC scenarios.
3-53. Upon receipt of the mission, leaders begin considering CASEVAC requirements based on mission
objectives and anticipated threats. This includes identifying tentative CCP locations, understanding what
evacuation support may be available, and determining any special equipment needed, such as fording gear,
tools for negotiating rough terrain, or other mission-specific requirements. At this stage, the commander may
assign responsibility for CASEVAC planning and rehearsals. Typically, platoon medics and PSGs help
identify platoon-level CCPs, while the 1SG and senior medic identify the company CCP based on a map
recon and, when possible, a physical leader's reconnaissance.
3-54. During the warning order, leaders communicate early CASEVAC expectations, including aid and litter
team readiness, casualty flow routes, and equipment checks. Rehearsal priorities, especially for casualty
movement, should be identified in the warning order. This helps shape expectations and guides subordinate
leaders’ preparation.
3-55. As leaders make a tentative plan, they assess terrain, enemy activity, and movement timelines to refine
CASEVAC options. Leaders tentatively identify CCP locations, assess casualty flow against potential threats,
and coordinate with supporting elements. At this point, CASEVAC planning remains flexible but is detailed
enough to guide initial preparations.
3-56. Initiating movement includes checking CASEVAC equipment, positioning evacuation platforms, and
confirming support relationships. Leaders may also task subordinate units with reconnoitering CCP sites or
alternate routes. Equipment checks include vehicle readiness, litter availability, lashing, and communication
tools.
3-57. During reconnaissance, leaders verify CASEVAC routes and CCP locations identified during course
of action development. If reconnaissance confirms or invalidates initial assumptions, leaders adjust the
CASEVAC plan accordingly. When time permits, leaders validate access to CCPs, terrain masking, or
recovery feasibility.
3-58. As leaders complete the plan, they finalize CCP locations, designate CASEVAC responsibilities, and
confirm signals or communication protocols. The CASEVAC plan is refined to align with the scheme of
maneuver and is integrated into the unit OPORD.
3-59. When issuing the order, leaders brief CASEVAC responsibilities clearly. The OPORD should include
CCP locations, movement routes, signals, and supporting units. Every Soldier understands the expected
casualty evacuation process, including how and where to move casualties during execution.
3-60. Supervise and refine includes rehearsals that incorporate CASEVAC tasks. Leaders evaluate aid and
litter drills, validate CCP placement, and ensure subordinate leaders can execute the evacuation plan.
Adjustments are made based on rehearsal outcomes, changing terrain, or updated intelligence.
3-61. Incorporating CASEVAC into each step of the TLP builds unit readiness and reinforces responsibility
for caring for the wounded. Early planning, clear roles, and realistic rehearsals help units maintain momentum
during combat and preserve the force. Table 3-3 on page 29 illustrates how CASEVAC fits into each step of
the TLP. This early and deliberate planning strengthens the operation and shows the unit’s commitment to
protecting its Soldiers.
