### CHALLENGES ANDMITIGATION

3-62. CASEVAC operations rely on nonmedical vehicles that were not designed to carry or care for
casualties. Unlike MEDEVAC platforms, which include medical equipment and trained crews, CASEVAC
platforms often lack basic features like litter mounts, stabilization equipment, and protective interiors. This
limitation creates significant risks during movement and places the burden of adaptation on unit leaders and
Soldiers.
3-63. Most standard Army vehicles do not have dedicated CASEVAC capability, forcing Soldiers to
improvise under stress. Units often use cargo trucks, utility vehicles, or rotary-wing aircraft without medical
interiors to move wounded personnel. When leaders fail to plan, these efforts may result in preventable
deaths, delayed care, or further additional injuries during transport.
3-64. Commanders cannot assume they will have time or flexibility to create CASEVAC solutions during
contact. They assess available vehicles early in mission planning to see if they can safely carry casualties.
Planners should ask:
•
Can a NATO-standard litter fit inside?
•
Can the litter be secured?
•
If not, can the casualty be laid down and transported without risk of harm?
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**29**
**Chapter 3**
3-65. If the answer to any of those questions is no, commanders develop alternatives before operations begin.
Units should identify CASEVAC-capable platforms, modify them to support casualty movement, and remove
obstacles like troop seats or gear racks. Even when rotary-wing aircraft lack medical interiors, planners
should include them in CASEVAC load plans to expand lift options.
3-66. Failing to plan for CASEVAC increases the risk of losing Soldiers who could otherwise survive. Delays
or confusion during movement can make injuries worse or cause casualties to die before reaching medical
care. Commanders protect their formation and fulfill their duty to the wounded by addressing CASEVAC
early and deliberately.
3-67. Tactical leaders plan for casualties to receive medical care as far forward as the mission allows. Early
care improves survival and helps stabilize Soldiers during CASEVAC. When needed, this may involve
coordinating forward damage control resuscitative or surgical support on a temporary basis. These
capabilities align with the mission and be coordinated with higher medical authorities to avoid creating
unnecessary risk.
3-68. Commanders take full ownership of CASEVAC planning and include it during mission analysis.
Treating CASEVAC as a last-minute problem invites loss of life and weakens combat power. CASEVAC is
a tactical and operational responsibility that supports the unit’s ability to fight, recover, and continue the
mission.
3-69. CASEVAC platforms lack the equipment and personnel needed to treat injuries during transport.
Commanders should equip designated CASEVAC vehicles with basic medical kits and train vehicle
operators in TCCC. These simple steps prepare units to respond when MEDEVAC is delayed or unavailable.
3-70. CASEVAC also competes with other transportation needs during high-tempo operations. Moving
casualties requires planners to balance medical needs with other mobility tasks like resupply or maneuver.
By prioritizing CASEVAC in advance and coordinating lift requirements, units can avoid delays and reduce
operational friction.
3-71. The psychological effects of CASEVAC should not be ignored. Watching a wounded teammate
evacuated under uncertain conditions can hurt morale. Leaders should speak clearly to their formations,
explain the plan, and reinforce the Army’s commitment to care for every WII Soldier. Honest communication
builds trust and helps maintain unit cohesion during stress.
3-72. When CASEVAC is planned and executed well, it keeps the force in the fight. It gives wounded
Soldiers a better chance to survive, clears the battlefield for follow-on operations, and strengthens combat
readiness. Effective CASEVAC demonstrates that Army leaders are prepared to act under pressure and meet
their responsibility to protect the force, even in contested or resource-limited environments.

## RISK MITIGATION AND SURVIVABILITY IN CASUALTY

## EVACUATION

3-73. CASEVAC operations face multiple risks that can disrupt evacuation and endanger both casualties and
evacuation teams. These threats include direct and indirect fire, jamming or loss of communication systems,
cyberattacks, and terrain that limits movement. Commanders and planners assess these threats early and
develop mitigation strategies, such as identifying alternate evacuation routes, building communication
redundancies, and coordinating with maneuver units for secure movement. Integrating these measures into
the CASEVAC plan reduces the risk of mission failure and improves the chances of safely moving casualties.
3-74. In denied or degraded environments, CASEVAC may require covered and concealed movement,
deception, or terrain masking to avoid detection and enemy threats. Commanders prepare for situations where
MEDEVAC is not available by identifying nonmedical platforms and training personnel in basic casualty
handling techniques. Since CASEVAC typically lacks en route medical care, unless specifically arranged,
units focus on triage, patient stabilization, and coordination with higher medical echelons. A well-developed
CASEVAC plan improves survivability by allowing evacuation to continue even when conditions worsen or
adversaries attempt to interfere.
3-75. CASEVAC helps preserve combat power and supports continuous operations. It is incorporated into
key warfighting functions, such as maneuver, protection, and sustainment. Commanders and staffs use the
**30**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
MDMP to integrate CASEVAC at every echelon, ensuring it supports the broader concept of operations. By
synchronizing CASEVAC with operational enablers and addressing threats through early planning, units
maintain tempo, care for the wounded, and improve mission success.

## ENVIRONMENTAL PLANNING FACTORS

3-76. Conducting CASEVAC in diverse environments, such as urban areas, subterranean spaces,
mountainous terrain, cold weather regions, jungles, deserts, arctic zones, maritime settings, and CBRN
conditions, requires terrain-specific planning. Each environment has its own unique challenges and
mitigation measures. Commanders adjust CASEVAC procedures to account for these factors and keep
evacuation efforts reliable. By tailoring operations to the environment, commanders strengthen maneuver
efforts and maintain evacuation readiness across all terrain types. This flexibility helps units remain effective,
even in the most demanding operational conditions.
3-77. Appendix A provides important insights into improving the Army’s evacuation capabilities to meet the
varied demands of CASEVAC across different terrains. Whether evacuating casualties from the arid expanses
of a desert or the dense foliage of a jungle, each environment requires specific equipment and strategy
adjustments. For example, in extreme cold, units address hypothermia risks during evacuation, while urban
operations may require detailed route reconnaissance to navigate complex infrastructure. These adaptations
help deliver timely and effective medical care, preserve Soldier health and morale, and reinforce overall
operational readiness.
3-78. Commanders adjust CASEVAC strategies to address the demands of specific environments. In
mountainous terrain, limited LZs may force reliance on ground evacuation or rope extractions. In jungle
settings, thick vegetation may require clearing for rotary-wing aircraft or using smaller platforms with short
landing capability. Urban operations often call for alternate routes and additional security due to blocked
roads or enemy threats. Commanders plan for these factors by modifying equipment, adjusting training, and
coordinating CASEVAC tasks to meet mission requirements in each environment.
3-79. CASEVAC planners also account for environmental hazards and seasonal conditions that may affect
evacuation, such as typhoons, monsoons, flash flooding, sandstorms, or extreme heat and cold. These hazards
can limit or delay evacuation operations by closing LZs, degrading visibility, or impeding ground movement.
Planning should identify platform-specific capabilities, such as hoists, rescue baskets, or water-landing gear,
and confirm whether supporting aviation or maritime assets have the necessary equipment for the operating
environment. Early identification of these factors allows units to select alternate platforms, adjust timelines,
or pre-position equipment to maintain evacuation readiness.
3-80. Well-developed CASEVAC plans in difficult environments help units deliver medical care and keep
maneuver elements mission capable. Rehearsals and training tailored to the terrain sharpen coordination and
improve response times. These efforts help maintain unit strength and support successful operations in
complex settings. By incorporating terrain-specific CASEVAC planning into the broader scheme of
maneuver, commanders strengthen survivability and operational reach. For additional planning factors or
considerations, see Appendix A.

### URBAN ANDSUBTERRANEANENVIRONMENTS

3-81. Planning for CASEVAC in urban and subterranean environments requires a comprehensive approach
across theater strategic, operational, and tactical levels. These environments pose unique challenges,
including dense infrastructure, limited access points, civilian presence, and degraded communications. Each
echelon contributes to a synchronized evacuation effort that supports uninterrupted care and movement of
casualties.
3-82. At the theater strategic level, leaders prioritize resource allocation, such as prepositioning medical
supplies and identifying potential triage and treatment sites in or near safe zones. Collaboration with allied
forces, host-nation partners, and nongovernmental organizations expands access to supplies, facilities, and
transportation assets that may not otherwise be available to the joint force. These partnerships also support
shared situational awareness and promote unified messaging in densely populated areas.
3-83. Operational planning in urban and subterranean environments focuses on identifying routes, assessing
risk, and coordinating movement between CCPs and evacuation nodes. These operations often include both
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**31**
**Chapter 3**
surface and subterranean objectives. For this reason, planners should position CCPs near identified portals
of entry to support breaching and extraction. As maneuver units progress underground, CASEVAC plans
include aid-and-litter team shuttle systems that can operate within confined spaces and follow the assault
force. Staff also evaluate structural integrity, detect explosive or chemical threats, and establish AXPs to
transfer casualties to evacuation platforms. Intelligence, engineer, and protection staff support the
identification of viable routes, chokepoints, and hazards that affect CASEVAC movement.
3-84. Tactical planning centers on safe and rapid casualty extraction from confined, obstructed, or collapsed
spaces. Units establish and manage CCPs close to the POI and train CASEVAC teams to use equipment such
as litters, pulleys, breaching tools, and protective gear. Platform hardening, noise and light discipline, and
redundant communications reduce risk during movement. In subterranean terrain, signal interference may
prevent radio communication, so units plan for alternate methods to maintain contact with higher
headquarters and medical elements.

### MOUNTAIN ANDCOLDWEATHERENVIRONMENTS

3-85. CASEVAC planning in mountain and cold weather environments requires a deliberate approach across
theater strategic, operational, and tactical levels. These environments pose serious logistical and
environmental challenges, including high altitudes, extreme temperatures, and difficult terrain. Each echelon
adapts its planning to account for limited mobility, weather delays, and increased risk to both casualties and
evacuation teams.
3-86. Theater strategic level planning focuses on allocating and positioning resources before operations
begin. Cold-weather clothing, equipment for treating frostbite and hypothermia, and snow-capable vehicles
are prepositioned to sustain casualty movement. Collaboration with allied forces or host-nation units
improves resource availability by giving planners access to existing supply caches, terrain-specific transport
platforms, and experienced personnel. These partnerships can reduce the need to deploy additional assets
from outside the area of operations.
3-87. At the operational level, staffs plan and synchronize casualty flow across multiple units and locations.
They identify viable evacuation routes, staging areas, and AXPs, while tracking weather patterns and
snowpack stability. Although they do not control or directly monitor patient movement from company level
CCPs, they maintain awareness of subordinate unit status, casualty trends, and operational conditions to
forecast when brigade or division level CASEVAC resources may be required. Role 2 capabilities placed
near assembly areas can shorten evacuation distances and improve survival rates.
3-88. Tactical planning focuses on casualty care at the POI and movement to the CCP. Units operating in
cold or mountainous terrain establish CCPs close to fighting positions and manage them until casualties can
be evacuated. CASEVAC teams are trained and equipped to operate in extreme cold, maintain
communication under harsh conditions, and adapt to delays caused by snow, terrain, or enemy activity. When
isolated, CASEVAC teams may need to use survival skills such as building shelters, melting snow for water,
or managing hypothermia until evacuation resumes.

### JUNGLEENVIRONMENT

3-89. Planning for CASEVAC in the jungle environment requires consideration at theater strategic,
operational, and tactical levels. Each level addresses the challenges of dense terrain, heavy rainfall, and
limited infrastructure to support successful CASEVAC operations.
3-90. Theater strategic planning focuses on resource prepositioning, including medical supplies and durable
equipment resistant to humidity and corrosion. Establishing MTFs close to operational areas reduces
evacuation times. Collaboration with allied forces or host-nation partners familiar with jungle operations
enhances resource sharing and operational effectiveness.
3-91. Operational planning prioritizes route selection, identification of ambulance loading points or AXPs,
and integration of aerial assets for evacuation. Planners assess the terrain's impact on movement and identify
areas that can support rotary-wing aircraft landings, hoist operations, or waterborne extractions.
3-92. Tactical planning emphasizes immediate casualty care and flexible extraction methods. Teams prepare
for extended delays due to difficult terrain and weather, ensuring they carry sufficient medical supplies for
**32**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
prolonged care and patient stabilization. Communication plans are critical, as the jungle often disrupts radio
signals, requiring alternative signaling or relay methods.

### DESERTENVIRONMENT

3-93. Effective CASEVAC planning in desert environments requires a multi-level approach that integrates
theater strategic, operational, and tactical considerations. While the core principles of CASEVAC remain
consistent across all operational settings, each environment presents distinct challenges that require tailored
solutions to support timely evacuation, efficient resource use, and effective risk mitigation.
3-94. Theater strategic planning in desert environments prioritizes the availability and distribution of
CASEVAC assets across expansive and often inhospitable terrain. Pre-positioning essential resources, such
as water, medical supplies, and CASEVAC platforms near high-risk areas, enhances response time and
survivability. Close collaboration with coalition partners facilitates resource sharing and extends operational
reach, mitigating the logistical burdens imposed by vast distances and harsh environmental conditions.
3-95. Operational planning focuses on mapping evacuation routes, establishing staging areas, and ensuring
logistical sustainability. While CCPs remain a tactical level responsibility, operational planners maintain
awareness of casualty movement from CCPs and coordinate AXPs to facilitate timely transfer to Role 2 or
Role 3 MTFs. Key considerations include:
•
Identifying terrain features that provide natural cover or influence movement.
•
Ensuring fuel resupply points along extended routes to sustain CASEVAC mobility.
•
Integrating robust communication networks to maintain coordination despite environmental
disruptions.
•
Coordinating with information collection assets to monitor threat levels and adjust operations
accordingly.
3-96. Tactical planning emphasizes the execution of CASEVAC missions under austere and potentially
hostile conditions. Units are responsible for establishing CCPs close to the POI, where casualties are triaged
and stabilized for further evacuation. CASEVAC teams are prepared for:
•
Casualty care techniques adapted to heat-related injuries and dehydration risks.
•
Platform hardening and mobility to counter threats such as ambushes and explosive hazards on open
terrain.
•
Route selection and contingency planning to account for platform breakdowns, dust storms, and
extended response times.

### ARCTIC ANDEXTREMECOLDENVIRONMENTS

3-97. Planning for CASEVAC in arctic and extreme cold environments requires theater strategic, operational,
and tactical considerations to address the unique challenges of arctic and extreme cold-weather operations.
Each level of planning accounts for terrain, weather, and limited resources.
3-98.  Theater strategic planners focus on positioning cold-weather medical supplies, shelters, and vehicles
in key locations before operations begin. These vehicles should be equipped to move over snow and ice.
Planners build strong logistics networks and coordinate with allied forces experienced in cold-weather
operations. They confirm that aerial evacuation assets are prepared to operate when snow and ice make
ground routes unusable.
3-99. At the operational level, planners identify evacuation routes that remain open and safe during freezing
conditions. They also establish warming stations or shelters along those routes to reduce cold-weather
exposure. Regardless of the environment, planners include CCPs, AXPs, and ambulance loading points to
support timely transfer of casualties. Coordination with intelligence teams helps monitor enemy activity and
forecast changes in weather that could disrupt evacuation timelines. MTFs are prepared to manage cold-
related injuries such as frostbite, trench foot, and hypothermia.
3-100. Tactical teams focus on stabilizing casualties and using gear made for cold-weather environments.
They conduct detailed route checks to avoid delays. Since bad weather can stop evacuation, teams carry
survival kits to protect both casualties and themselves during long prolonged holding periods.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**33**
**Chapter 3**

### MARITIME ANDLITTORALENVIRONMENTS

3-101. Planning for CASEVAC in maritime and littoral environments requires theater strategic, operational,
and tactical considerations to address the challenges of waterborne operations and coastal terrain. Maritime
environments refer to open water and deep-sea operations conducted from naval vessels or other large
watercraft. Littoral environments are coastal areas where land and sea meet, including harbors, estuaries, and
near-shore waters where operations involve both land and maritime forces. Effective planning supports the
safe and timely evacuation of casualties under these conditions.
3-102. Theater strategic planning focuses on allocating specialized CASEVAC assets, such as amphibious
vehicles, naval vessels, and rotary-wing aircraft capable of operating in maritime environments.
Prepositioning medical supplies on ships or forward operating bases in coastal regions enhances
responsiveness. Collaboration with naval and coalition forces improves resource availability and operational
reach.
3-103. Operational planning emphasizes identifying safe extraction points, such as docks, beaches, or
designated rotary-wing aircraft LZs. Planners account for tidal patterns, weather conditions, and enemy
presence in these areas. Establishing staging areas and integrating intelligence about the maritime domain
are critical for ensuring effective CASEVAC operations.
3-104. Tactical planning prioritizes casualty care and secure movement to extraction points. CASEVAC
teams develop contingency plans for evacuating casualties during adverse weather or enemy engagements.
Rapid-reaction watercraft and small rotary-wing aircraft provide critical capability for navigating confined
littoral zones and responding to emergencies.

### CHEMICAL, BIOLOGICAL, RADIOLOGICAL,ANDNUCLEARENVIRONMENTS

3-105. Planning for CASEVAC in a CBRN environment requires a multi-tiered approach, addressing theater
strategic, operational, and tactical considerations. The commander, CBRN staff officer, and unit leaders
provide CBRN threat and hazard information during the deliberate planning process. The medical officer
provides threat information, including endemic disease and other occupational environmental hazards present
in the assigned area. Planning includes CBRN vulnerability assessments and casualty estimates into decision
briefings and orders. Effective planning facilitates the accomplishment of the mission under hazardous
conditions while minimizing casualties. For more information on CBRN planning, refer to ATP 3-11.36.
3-106. Several factors influence CASEVAC in a CBRN environment. These include DOW policies,
agreements with host-nations, and theater strategic level planning. Public perception and enemy propaganda
may also influence available COA involving contaminated personnel or equipment.
3-107. CASEVAC from CBRN-contaminated areas often requires theater strategic level coordination. U.S.
and host-nation policies may restrict the handling, movement and transport of contaminated personnel and
equipment. Strategic air evacuation is coordinated with medical authorities, the Combatant Commander, and
U.S. Transportation Command. Final approval for evacuation rests with the Secretary of War.
3-108. To execute a coordinated response, commanders establish clear communication protocols for
reporting contamination mission-oriented protective posture (MOPP) levels and casualties. Collaboration
with allied forces and host-nation authorities enhances resource sharing and strengthens overall response
efforts. For additional guidance on CBRN planning at the theater strategic level, refer to JP 3-11.
3-109. Planning for operations in a CBRN environment focuses on enabling commanders to fight and win.
This includes the movement of personnel and equipment within and out of theater. Planning enables the
accomplishment of mission-essential tasks under CBRN conditions. This involves vulnerability analysis,
determining CBRN threat status, and ensuring the availability and employment of CBRN protective
equipment and assets. The CBRN annex on an OPORD addresses critical considerations such as operational
exposure guidelines, radiation exposure status, and identifying MTF capable of advanced treatment of
CBRN-contaminated casualties. Incorporating this information into operational planning for CASEVAC is
essential. Synchronizing CBRN, medical, and CASEVAC consolidates, and aligns resources to support the
operational mission.
3-110. Tactical CBRN planning should address threats and hazards identified through intelligence and
deliberate mission planning. At the tactical level, this includes conducting vulnerability assessments,
**34**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
establishing automatic masking criteria, analyzing MOPP and heat stress risks, and identifying linkup points
for decontamination. The CBRN Warning and Reporting System provides the primary means for alerting
units to actual or anticipated hazards and supports the development of the common operational picture. Units
use this system to plan CBRN operations such as route decontamination, reconnaissance, contamination
crossings, and personnel warnings. CASEVAC teams must have access to this system to avoid contaminated
areas and adjust evacuation routes accordingly. Teams prepare contingency plans for delayed or disrupted
evacuations, including unreported or secondary contamination. Redundant communication systems help
sustain CASEVAC operations in degraded or denied environments. For additional guidance, refer to
Technical Manual (TM) 3-11.32.

### ROUGHTERRAIN

3-111. Planning for CASEVAC in rough terrain requires a comprehensive, multi-echelon approach that
integrates theater strategic, operational, and tactical considerations. Each level of planning accounts for
terrain constraints, limited mobility, and the potential for prolonged evacuation times.
**Theater Strategic Planning**
3-112. Theater strategic planning supports CASEVAC by shaping the operational environment, not by
directing tactical execution. Strategic planners focus on coordinating resources, building access to partner
capabilities, and aligning medical support across joint and multinational formations. This plan includes the
forward positioning of medical materiel, mobility platforms, and CASEVAC-enabling equipment to reduce
response times in high-risk areas. Planners also coordinate with other Services, interagency partners, and
coalition forces to improve interoperability. Because CASEVAC is a tactical task, theater-level efforts focus
on setting the conditions that allow subordinate commanders to execute CASEVAC under diverse operational
conditions.
3-113. Planners at this level consider variations in terrain, threat, and force composition across the theater.
They may need to support operations in jungle, desert, mountain, or littoral environments, all with different
implications for evacuation. To address this, strategic planners establish policy, identify resource
requirements, and coordinate theater-wide training opportunities. Early issue of specialized CASEVAC
related equipment, such as casualty restraints or mountaineering litters, gives tactical units time to train before
deployment. These efforts follow joint doctrine and support the broader HSS architecture, including access
to intertheater evacuation platforms and joint patient movement systems. Strategic-level coordination
enhances the ability of subordinate formations to execute CASEVAC as part of their mission.
**Operational Planning**
3-114. At the operational level, planners focus on establishing the conditions necessary for subordinate units
to conduct effective casualty evacuation. This includes identifying suitable terrain for CCPs, evaluating the
viability of ground and air evacuation routes, and integrating evacuation into the scheme of support.
Operational staffs coordinate closely with joint and multinational medical partners to ensure rotary-wing or
fixed-wing platforms are positioned to support the mission. They also help define where MTFs should be
staged to meet evacuation timelines and patient flow demands. CASEVAC remains a tactical task, but
operational planners provide the resources, policy guidance, and situational awareness that allow subordinate
units to execute it.
3-115. Key staff sections contribute to this planning effort. The G/S-3 (Operations) aligns medical evacuation
support with the maneuver plan and establishes control measures for CCPs and LZs. The G/S-4 (Logistics)
supports medical resupply, fuel planning, and platform maintenance. The G/S-3 Air coordinates lift assets,
while the G/S-2 (Intelligence) monitors enemy activity, terrain changes, and environmental conditions. These
staff sections do not manage the movement of casualties but instead support the positioning of evacuation
assets, sustainment enablers, and communications systems that make CASEVAC and MEDEVAC feasible
at the tactical level.
3-116. Operational staffs reassess evacuation conditions continuously. Flooded routes, damaged trails, or
enemy interdiction can delay or deny evacuation, placing wounded personnel at increased risk. Intelligence
updates are timely, accurate, and relevant to support rapid adjustments. This includes reporting new enemy
threats along planned routes, changes in terrain accessibility, and the availability of alternate extraction sites.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**35**
**Chapter 3**
When operational planners integrate this information into the common operating picture, they improve
support to subordinate formations, reduce evacuation delays, and strengthen the alignment of medical
capabilities with the overall operation.
**Tactical Planning**
3-117. Tactical-level planning prepares CASEVAC teams to operate in challenging environments. Teams
are equipped with lightweight, portable medical kits, stretchers, climbing gear, and rope systems for casualty
extractions in difficult terrain. They also require training that matches the demands of the operational
environment.
3-118. Weather is a key consideration in planning, as rain, snow, or extreme temperatures can affect both
evacuation routes and the endurance of litter teams. CASEVAC teams should have:
•
Contingency plans for delayed evacuations, including alternate routes and patient sustainment
measures.
•
Pre-identified LZs along dismounted routes to support rapid air evacuation when aviation assets
become available.
•
Personnel trained in advanced litter techniques and high-angle rescue methods to move casualties
safely through restrictive terrain.

## CASUALTY MANAGEMENT

3-119. Casualty management during CASEVAC operations requires coordination, judgment, and trained
personnel at every level. Medical personnel conduct medical regulating to determine the best destination for
each casualty based on injury severity, the number of patients, and the capacity of nearby MTFs. Medical
regulating is the coordination for the movement of patients to match patients with an appropriate medical
treatment facility (JP 4-02).
3-120. If en route care is not available, leaders consider how long a casualty can survive without their
condition worsening. While medical personnel are preferred for triage, all personnel involved in CASEVAC
are trained to perform basic triage tasks. Nonmedical personnel are often the first to reach WII Soldiers, and
their ability to assess, secure, and move casualties can save lives. Training these personnel to identify patient
categories and provide basic care during transport improves the effectiveness and survivability of CASEVAC
missions.
3-121. Casualties should be transported to the nearest CCP, AXP, MTF, or other sites where medical
personnel are located. However, CASEVAC may not always follow a linear or rearward direction. In
dynamic operational environments, casualties may need to be moved forward toward the forward line of
troops (known as FLOT) or laterally to adjacent units. These movements can occur when MTFs are bounding
forward to maintain proximity to maneuver forces or when relocation is required to adapt to combat
operations. This non-linear approach helps maintain timely access to care despite changing battlefield
conditions.
3-122. To manage these complexities, casualty movement and prioritization are coordinated. Command
surgeons, working closely with the G/S-1 and G/S-4, play a key role in identifying effective evacuation routes
and destinations. Although this is not a formal medical regulating process, well-coordinated casualty
movement supports efficient resource allocation, prioritization based on medical urgency, and real-time
awareness of available MTFs. This coordination improves the use of evacuation assets, avoids bottlenecks,
and increases the survival and recovery of casualties.
3-123. Effective casualty management requires a combination of clear guidelines, flexible planning, and
strong command oversight. Commanders and medical personnel establish standardized procedures for
determining the severity of injuries and patient categories, ensuring consistency and accuracy in triage
decisions. This proactive approach enables personnel to make informed decisions, even in high-stress
situations, and helps to prevent errors that can have serious consequences.
3-124. Accurate prioritization of casualties, also known as triage, is crucial to ensuring that WII personnel
receive the appropriate level of care as quickly as possible. Errors in triage can have serious consequences:
**36**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
•
Over-prioritization, treating minor injuries as urgent, can overload medical resources, diverting
attention from critically WII personnel.
•
Under-prioritization, failing to recognize life-threatening injuries, can delay urgent care, leading to
preventable deaths or worsened medical outcomes.
3-125. These mistakes can result in poor care during transport, delays in patient movement, and misallocation
of evacuation resources. To prevent these issues, commanders and medical personnel train their units on the
principles of triage, the significance of accurate casualty assessment, and the risks of misclassification.
Training should emphasize:
•
Rapid injury assessment techniques to correctly determine severity.
•
Proper assignment of priority levels to match casualties with the right care.
•
Seeking medical expertise whenever possible to enhance decision-making under pressure.
3-126. By reinforcing these skills, units can improve decision-making in critical situations, ensuring
casualties receive the care they need while maintaining the overall effectiveness of evacuation operations.
Proper triage and prioritization not only enhance survival rates but also optimize the use of available medical
and evacuation resources, preventing unnecessary strain on the system.
3-127. Regular review and assessment of CASEVAC operations are essential for identifying areas of
improvement and optimizing the use of evacuation assets. Commanders and their teams should conduct
thorough after action reviews (AARs), analyzing what worked, what did not, and how procedures can be
improved. They should also gather feedback from medical personnel and other stakeholders to inform future
planning. By fostering a culture of continuous evaluation and adaptation, units can keep CASEVAC
operations responsive to the changing demands of the mission.
**The following paragraph is in consonance with**
**Standardization Agreement (STANAG) 3204**
3-128. The most severe casualties, those categorized as URGENT or URGENT-SURG, should be evacuated
before those classified as PRIORITY or ROUTINE. However, leaders monitor lower-priority casualties
closely and evacuate them before their condition worsens to the point of requiring an upgrade in precedence.
URGENT and URGENT-SURG casualties should be transported by ambulances that can provide en route
medical care. If a nonmedical asset is used to move one of these patients, that casualty should be transferred
to a ground or air ambulance at the first opportunity or delivered directly to a supporting medical treatment
facility.
3-129. Planners should incorporate the use of nonmedical air assets and dedicated ground ambulances for
moving PRIORITY casualties when medical evacuation platforms are limited. Similarly, nonmedical ground
vehicles may be used to transport ROUTINE precedence patients if no dedicated medical assets are available.
When CASEVAC platforms are used, every effort should be made to assign medical personnel to accompany
the patient, even for ROUTINE cases. See table 3-4 on page 38 for categories of evacuation precedence.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**37**
**Chapter 3**
**Table 3-4. Categories of evacuation precedence**
**Priority I, URGENT**
**(movement within 1 hour)**
Is assigned to emergency cases that should be evacuated as soon
as possible and within a maximum of one hour to save life, limb, or
eyesight and to prevent complications of serious illness and to avoid
permanent disability.
**Priority IA, URGENT-SURG****1**
**(movement within 1 hour)**
Is assigned to patients that should be evacuated as soon as
possible and within a maximum of one hour, who must receive far
forward surgical intervention to save life, limb, or eyesight, and to
stabilize for further evacuation.
**Priority II, PRIORITY**
**(movement within 4 hours)**
Is assigned to sick and wounded personnel requiring prompt
medical care. This precedence is used when the individual should
be evacuated within four hours, or if their medical condition could
deteriorate to such a degree that they will become an URGENT
precedence, or whose requirements for special treatment are not
available locally, or who will suffer unnecessary pain or disability.
**Priority III, ROUTINE**
**(movement within 24 hour)**
Is assigned to sick and wounded personnel requiring evacuation but
whose condition is not expected to deteriorate significantly. The sick
and wounded in this category should be evacuated within 24 hours.
**Priority IV, CONVENIENCE****1**
Is assigned to patients for whom evacuation by medical platform is
a matter of medical convenience rather than a necessity.
**(movement within no time limit)**
**Note.** The NATO STANAG 3204 has deleted the categories of Priority IA (URGENT SURG) and Priority IV
(CONVENIENCE); however, these categories are still included in the United States Army evacuation priorities as there
is a requirement for it in an operational environment.

### RESPONSIBILITIES

3-130. The Service component commander is responsible for coordinating and executing CASEVAC. While
not equipped with MEDEVAC capabilities, nonmedical platforms such as rotary-wing and fixed-wing
aircraft can be used to evacuate casualties under appropriate conditions. These platforms are often provided
by the Army, Air Force, or Navy, depending on operational needs, and are prepared to adapt to different
CASEVAC scenarios.
3-131. CASEVAC relies on a combination of military assets, including tactical rotary-wing aircraft, transport
aircraft, and other platforms that may be pressed into Service during evacuation operations. The Army may
provide ground and rotary-wing transport, often leveraging its combat aircraft for tactical CASEVAC in
support of joint and allied forces. For fixed-wing CASEVAC, the Air Force or Navy may assume
responsibility depending on the location and available platforms.
3-132. Coordination between Services is essential for effective CASEVAC operations, especially when
adapting to changing battlefield conditions, enemy threats, or logistical challenges. Nonmedical aircraft,
such as the CH-47 Chinook or C-130 Hercules, are often repurposed for CASEVAC when medical platforms
are unavailable or overwhelmed. Integrating CASEVAC into evacuation plans adds flexibility and enables
rapid response, but it requires thorough planning, pre-mission coordination, and training to reduce risk and
support the safe transport of casualties.

### UTILIZINGEVACUATIONPRECEDENCE FOREVACUATIONDETERMINATION

3-133. In combat and operational environments, evacuating casualties is critical to preserving life and
combat effectiveness. The evacuation precedence system helps medical personnel allocate resources based
on the severity of each patient’s condition. However, MEDEVAC may not always be available due to
mission priorities, limited resources, or hostile conditions. In these situations, CASEVAC provides an
alternative to prevent delays in life-saving treatment.
3-134. Commanders, medical teams, and unit leaders decide whether to use MEDEVAC or CASEVAC
based on several factors:
•
Evacuation Precedence:

Priority I (URGENT) and Priority IA (URGENT-SURG) cases require MEDEVAC whenever
possible due to the need for rapid transport and en route care. However, if MEDEVAC assets
**38**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
are unavailable, CASEVAC should be immediately coordinated using the fastest available
transportation.

Priority II (PRIORITY) cases may be evacuated using either MEDEVAC or CASEVAC,
depending on the availability of medical transport and the risk of deterioration.

Priority III (ROUTINE) and Priority IV (CONVENIENCE) cases are ideal for CASEVAC to
preserve MEDEVAC resources for critical cases.
•
Availability of MEDEVAC Assets:

If task organized MEDEVAC platforms (air or ground) are available, MEDEVAC should be
the first choice for any patient with life-threatening injuries.

When MEDEVAC assets are unavailable, delayed, or grounded due to enemy threats,
commanders should immediately coordinate CASEVAC using ground combat vehicles, rotary-
wing aircraft, or logistics convoys.
•
Tactical and Environmental Factors:

Hostile environments, contested airspace, or logistical constraints may prevent the use of
MEDEVAC. In such cases, CASEVAC is implemented using available transportation.

Nonmedical ground or air assets (such as troop carriers, supply trucks, or cargo rotary-wing
aircraft) may be adapted for emergency transport if MEDEVAC is not an option.
3-135. To support seamless casualty movement, medical planners should:
•
Pre-designate CASEVAC routes using nonmedical platforms as a contingency for MEDEVAC
delays.
•
Train combat units on basic casualty care techniques so they can sustain patients during CASEVAC.
•
Develop MEDEVAC-to-CASEVAC transition protocols so that urgent patients do not wait for
unavailable MEDEVAC assets.
•
Equip nonmedical platforms with basic medical supplies (such as litters, and first-aid kits) for
emergency use during CASEVAC.
•
Identify additional equipment casualties and patients may require in extreme environments such as
extra blankets or weather-specific protective gear.
•
Ensure CASEVAC teams understand and can apply standard triage categories, casualty markings,
and patient documentation requirements in accordance with unit SOPs and applicable joint or
multinational procedures.
3-136. Effective casualty movement requires flexibility and rapid decision-making. While ground
MEDEVAC remains the primary method with air MEDEVAC as the preferred method for critically WII
personnel. When MEDEVAC is unavailable, CASEVAC provides an alternate means of evacuation,
reducing unnecessary delays in care. By integrating evacuation precedence into planning and establishing
CASEVAC as a viable backup, commanders support timely and effective patient movement in any
operational environment.

### BEHAVIORALHEALTHCASUALTIES

3-137. Behavioral health casualties, such as those resulting from combat and operational stress, can
significantly impact readiness and command effectiveness. Commanders should coordinate with behavioral
health elements to manage these cases early and close to the POI, when possible, to facilitate rapid RTD.
However, evacuation may be necessary when operational demands or medical evaluations warrant removal
from the combat zone. CASEVAC assets may be used to evacuate behavioral health casualties when required.
Balancing the preservation of combat power with the imperative to unburden commanders is critical to
sustaining long-term operational effectiveness. For detailed guidance on procedures, planning considerations,
and treatment for behavioral health casualties, commanders and staff should, refer to—
•
ATP 4-02.8 for combat operational stress control team structure, behavioral health triage, and
treatment and return-to-duty protocols.
•
ATP 4-02.13 for planning and execution of CASEVAC, including behavioral health casualty
considerations.

### PHYSIOLOGICALCONSIDERATIONS INCASUALTYEVACUATION

3-138. CASEVAC planning often emphasizes logistical and tactical factors such as vehicle speed, route
selection, and resource availability. However, the anatomical and physiological constraints of casualties play
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**39**
**Chapter 3**
an equally critical role in determining the urgency, method, and feasibility of evacuation. Several factors
affect whether a WII Soldier survives and recovers. These include how quickly they get treatment, how much
blood they lose, how well they can breathe, whether their spine is stable, and how they can handle movement,
changes in altitude, and the environment. Transporting a casualty is not simply a function of distance and
speed, it accounts for individual physiological responses to evacuation stressors.
3-139. For example, casualties in hemorrhagic shock (shock from severe blood loss) deteriorate rapidly
without immediate resuscitation and require the fastest evacuation method available. In contrast, a casualty
with a minor fracture may not require immediate transport and can wait for optimized CASEVAC conditions.
Spinal cord injuries demand risk management, which means minimal movement and body stabilization, to
prevent further neurological damage, requiring specialized transport techniques. Similarly, air CASEVAC
introduces considerations such as barometric pressure changes, which can worsen conditions like
pneumothorax (collapsed lung) and require decompression procedures before flight.
3-140. Effective CASEVAC planning requires understanding how the human body responds to trauma, blood
loss, and shock. Anatomical principles help leaders recognize which injuries are most life-threatening, such
as those involving the chest, head, or major arteries. Physiological principles guide decisions about how
quickly the body can deteriorate without treatment, especially when dealing with breathing problems, blood
circulation, or changes in consciousness. By applying these principles, leaders prioritize the movement of
casualties who need urgent care first. This approach supports both medical and operational priorities by
helping units evacuate the right casualty at the right time, preserving life, and maintaining combat power.

### RESTRICTION OFMOVEMENT, ISOLATION,ANDQUARANTINE

3-141. To prevent the spread of an infectious disease or contagious illness, public health authorities use
different strategies. Three of these strategies are: restriction of movement, isolation, and quarantine. These
are common practices in public health and aim to prevent and control exposure to unexposed and uninfected
individuals to potentially infected or infectious persons. These measures may be voluntarily implemented or
be a directive by public health authorities, or by military commanders.
3-142. Infectious disease outbreaks, such as Ebola or other high-risk pathogens, require coordination at the
theater strategic level so that CASEVAC operations comply with international, national, and military health
regulations. When moving casualties across international or state borders, restrictions from host-nations,
coalition partners, or local governments may delay or block evacuation routes, access to definitive care, or
patient transfers. Theater strategic planning addresses legal, logistical, and diplomatic factors when
conducting CASEVAC during a public health emergency.
3-143. In the event of an outbreak, commanders and medical planners at the corps, theater Army, or
equivalent joint or multinational headquarters engage with:
•
The Department of State to secure diplomatic clearances for the evacuation of WII across
international borders.
•
The Centers for Disease Control and Prevention and Host-Nation Health Authorities to comply with
quarantine requirements and public health protocols.
•
Relevant international health organizations and regulatory frameworks (such as the World Health
Organization or International Health Regulations) to support coordination with host-nations and
multinational partners.
•
Military and civilian air transport authorities to arrange for specialized evacuation (such as
aeromedical isolation units for highly infectious patients).
3-144. Special considerations for high-risk disease CASEVAC include:
•
Designating regional quarantine and treatment facilities before an outbreak occurs.
•
Pre-positioning personal protective equipment (PPE) and isolation transport capabilities in high-risk
theaters of operation.
•
Training military medical personnel in infectious disease containment and aeromedical isolation
procedures.
**40**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
•
Using dedicated biocontainment assets (such as Airborne Biological Containment Systems used in
past Ebola responses).
•
Coordinating with host-nation and coalition forces so that cross-border patient movement aligns
with international travel restrictions, entry and exit requirements, and public health protocols.
3-145. During a declared public health emergency, a commander, in consultation with the public health
emergency officer, may exercise special powers relating to people necessary to prevent the spread of
communicable diseases. To the extent necessary for protecting or securing military property or places and
associated military personnel, such special powers may also include people other than military personnel
who are present on a DOW installation or other area under DOW control. For more information, refer to
Department of Defense 5200.08-R.
**Restriction of Movement**
3-146. Restriction of movement is the limitation of movement for individuals who may have been exposed
to a communicable disease to prevent its spread. This measure may apply to military personnel and civilians
to reduce the risk of transmission in military settings. For military personnel, restriction of movement may
include isolation, quarantine, or other necessary measures to prevent or limit disease transmission. For non-
military personnel, restriction of movement may involve limiting access to or from military installations to
control potential exposure.
**Isolation**
3-147. Isolation is the separation of individuals who have a specific infectious illness from healthy
populations to prevent the spread of disease. This measure allows targeted medical care for infected
individuals while ensuring that uninfected personnel remain protected. Isolated individuals may receive care
in their homes, hospitals, or designated MTFs. Protective sequestration, a form of reverse isolation, separates
uninfected Service members from infected populations or contaminated environments to preserve force
readiness as a tactical or strategic reserve.
**Quarantine**
3-148. Quarantine is the separation and restriction of movement of individuals who have been exposed to an
infectious agent but have not yet developed symptoms. It involves confinement and active health surveillance
to monitor for signs of infection and prevent potential disease transmission. Quarantine is a medically
effective measure to protect unexposed personnel and limit the spread of infectious diseases.
3-149. In most situations, it is recommended that necessary medical resources be moved to the event location
(treat in place) rather than moving a contagious patient. However, CASEVAC will continue during restriction
of movement, isolation, and quarantine, requiring additional protective measures, specialized transport, and
coordination at multiple levels of command.
3-150. Strategic-level considerations for infectious disease CASEVAC include securing diplomatic
clearances for cross-border patient movement, complying with International Health Regulations, and
coordinating with host-nation governments to avoid disruptions to evacuation routes. Commanders plan for:
•
Isolation transport for confirmed cases to prevent further transmission during CASEVAC.
•
Contingency evacuation routes that reflect international and state travel restrictions.
•
Additional protective equipment and decontamination methods for medical personnel, transport
platforms, and receiving medical treatment facilities.
•
Collaboration with international health authorities and military medical planners so that CASEVAC
procedures align with national and global public health measures.
3-151. For more information, refer to ATP 4-02.84, the DOW Global Health Engagement policies, guidance
from the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention, and the World Health Organization on infectious
disease response. These references provide detailed guidance for coordinating health protection measures
during joint, interagency, and multinational operations.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**41**
**Chapter 3**

### ADDRESSINGMEDICAL, TACTICAL,ANDDECONTAMINATIONCONSIDERATIONS

3-152. CASEVAC helps maintain combat effectiveness by providing timely evacuation when MEDEVAC
assets are unavailable. Repurposing nonmedical vehicles for CASEVAC allows casualties to receive early
care and are removed from danger. This approach supports unit cohesion, limits battlefield exposure, and
preserves operational capability by keeping forces intact and mobile.
3-153. Planning for CASEVAC accounts for available resources and real-world operational constraints.
Strategically, leaders prioritize the allocation of supplies and CASEVAC vehicles to optimize casualty flow.
Tactically, commanders should identify evacuation assets in advance, train personnel to handle casualties,
and integrate CASEVAC procedures into mission rehearsals. This improves responsiveness and helps
prevent unnecessary loss of life.
3-154. CASEVAC planning addresses the risks associated with transporting patients who may have
infectious diseases such as tuberculosis or influenza. Commanders should consult with unit medical
personnel to apply appropriate restriction of movement, quarantine procedures, and PPE requirements. These
measures reduce the risk of disease spread among vehicle crews and follow-on casualties.
3-155. Decontamination of CASEVAC vehicles is essential after transporting casualties. Blood, body fluids,
and biological contaminants pose ongoing risks to personnel. Commanders ensure that decontamination
procedures are established and executed. These include utilizing approved military disinfectants, PPE for
cleaning crews, and proper disposal of contaminated materials. For more information, refer to ATP 3-11.33
and ATP 4-02.7.
3-156. Theater strategic, operational and tactical units integrate CASEVAC decontamination protocols into
their planning. Theater strategic level leaders allocate sanitation resources and issue guidance on
contamination control, while tactical level units execute cleaning procedures after each CASEVAC mission.
These measures support readiness and reduce the risk of further casualties due to infection.
3-157. Units should assess CASEVAC vehicles, equipment, and personnel for contamination at logical points
in the mission, such as after exposure to known biological hazards, before re-entry into clean areas, or upon
completion of operations. While full decontamination after every movement between CCPs, AXPs, or MTFs
may not be feasible, crews remain alert to contamination risks during high-exposure missions. Company-
level leaders coordinate basic cleaning efforts, such as wiping surfaces and removing waste, while battalion
and brigade medical elements provide support for more thorough cleaning when needed. Reusable
equipment, like litters and straps, should be cleaned in accordance with exposure levels and standard
operating procedures before returning to service.
3-158. In CBRN environments, CASEVAC decontamination efforts are coordinated with broader personnel
and equipment decontamination operations. Collocating patient decontamination sites with other
decontamination assets helps conserve resources and prevent the spread of contamination. Refer to
ATP 3-11.33 and ATP 4-02.7 for detailed guidance.

## COMMAND ROLES AND RESPONSIBILITIES IN CASUALTY

## EVACUATION

3-159. CASEVAC planning is a responsibility shared across all echelons, from Army and theater commands
to platoon-level units. It supports timely medical care for WII Soldiers while preserving mobility, sustaining
morale, and reinforcing force health protection measures. For more information on force health protection,
refer to FM 4-02.
3-160. At the strategic level, CASEVAC planning includes force projection, coordination with joint and
multinational partners, and pre-positioning medical resources to support sustained operations. Strategic
leaders also secure evacuation clearances and establish theater-wide networks that connect air and ground
platforms.
3-161. At the operational level, theater and corps commands allocate CASEVAC resources across divisions
and align Role 2 and Role 3 MTFs with maneuver plans. This coordination supports continuity of care and
helps keeps evacuation operations feasible, even during high-casualty conditions.
**42**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
3-162. Tactical CASEVAC planning occurs at all echelons from brigade to platoon. Leaders identify routes,
assign platforms, and train personnel to execute casualty movement under fire. Since most units lack
dedicated CASEVAC equipment in their table of organization and equipment, commanders adapt existing
vehicles without diverting MEDEVAC assets or degrading MTF operations.

### COMMANDCONSIDERATIONS FORCASUALTYEVACUATIONPLANNING

3-163. Commanders are responsible for integrating CASEVAC planning into the overall mission rather than
treating it as a separate function. Effective CASEVAC supports survivability, morale, and readiness across
the force. Commanders prioritize CASEVAC assets based on anticipated casualty flow, risk exposure, and
the specific requirements of each mission. Assets should be positioned and task-organized to support these
requirements without unnecessarily diverting combat power.
3-164. CASEVAC is a combat multiplier when it is responsive and aligned with operational tempo. Planners
account for enemy threats, platform survivability, and resource limitations. Commanders should anticipate
MASCAL events, establish clear C2 roles, and ensure that CASEVAC plans are rehearsed. In austere or
contested environments, commanders may need to rely on alternate transport options and integrate
CASEVAC into the overall protection and sustainment framework. This approach helps units maintain
momentum despite battlefield challenges.

### THEATER ANDCORPSLEVEL

3-165. At the theater and corps levels, evacuation planning focuses on aligning evacuation capabilities,
including CASEVAC and MEDEVAC, with medical infrastructure, sustainment operations, and mission
timelines. The theater medical command and medical brigade coordinate with supported division, corps, joint
task force, and other joint task force planners to assess medical capacity, patient flow, and evacuation
requirements. While CASEVAC is executed at the tactical level, these higher headquarters synchronize it
within the broader patient movement function to ensure unity of effort across the joint force. Their planning
ensures subordinate units can move casualties out of CCPs and MTFs using available platforms when
MEDEVAC is not accessible or sufficient.
3-166. Once casualties enter the AHS, regulated medical evacuation follows joint patient movement policies
and MEDEVAC procedures. However, when regulated movement is delayed or unavailable, regulated
CASEVAC may be required to clear Role 2 and Role 3 MTFs or to support repositioning of those facilities.
At the theater and, to a degree, corps levels, CASEVAC planning also includes contingency planning and
standardized procedures for deliberate and regulated CASEVAC in support of large-scale MEDEVAC
operations. The theater medical command and medical brigade plan for these conditions in coordination with
the theater sustainment command and the expeditionary sustainment command. This coordination allows
planners to integrate nonmedical evacuation assets into the patient movement architecture, sustain casualty
movement in austere or high-threat areas, and sustain patient flow across the theater.
3-167. Evacuation planning at this level requires integrated effort across medical, sustainment, protection,
maneuver, and joint staff sections. CASEVAC is addressed alongside MEDEVAC to create a unified
evacuation capability that adapts to operational demands. Medical and sustainment planners work together
to match platform availability with medical movement requirements, identify gaps in regulated evacuation,
and build options for casualty movement under varying threat and terrain conditions. This coordination
supports MTF survivability, patient outcomes, and the commander’s ability to maintain operational tempo.
**Command Group**
3-168. The command group plays a critical role in providing overall guidance, intent, and priorities for
CASEVAC operations. They direct staff coordination to support a seamless and efficient process aligned
with operational requirements. The command group also communicates the commander’s intent for
CASEVAC to all staff sections and oversees resource allocation and decision-making during execution. This
oversight is essential for conducting effective and efficient CASEVAC operations.
3-169. In addition to setting overall direction, the command group ensures that all staff sections work together
to support CASEVAC operations. This includes coordinating with the Theater Medical Command, Medical
Brigade, Expeditionary Sustainment Command, and Combat Sustainment Support Battalion to align medical
logistics and supply chain management with operational medical needs. The command group also
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**43**
**Chapter 3**
collaborates closely with the primary staff to confirm that CASEVAC operations are fully planned, well-
integrated, and properly synchronized.
**Primary Staff Sections**
3-170. The primary staff sections play a critical role in supporting CASEVAC operations. The primary staff
sections include:
•
G-1 (Personnel): Manages casualty operations, replacement operations, and personnel readiness.
•
G-2 (Intelligence): Assesses enemy threats and terrain considerations that impact CASEVAC routes.
•
G-3 (Operations): Coordinates mission planning, assists with casualty and equipment evacuation
operations, and synchronization with combat operations.
•
G-4 (Logistics): Oversees medical supply, equipment, and evacuation assets.
•
G-5 (Plans): Develops long-term operational and strategic CASEVAC plans.
•
G-6 (Communications): Maintains secure communication and tracking of CASEVAC operations.
•
G-7 (Training and Exercises): Oversees CASEVAC-related training and readiness assessments.
•
G-8 (Finance and Resource Management): Manages CASEVAC funding and resource allocation.
•
G-9 (Civil Affairs Operations): Manages the Commander’s military engagement priorities and
objectives with regards to unified action partners.
**Special Staff Officers**
3-171. Special staff officers play a critical role in planning, coordinating, and executing effective CASEVAC
operations. Working closely with the command group and staff sections, these officers apply functional and
technical expertise throughout all phases of the operation. Their responsibilities include:
•
Surgeon (Medical Operations) who —

Advises the commander and staff on CASEVAC planning and medical integration across all
phases of the operation.

Coordinates with the G-3 to align medical capabilities with the operational plan and
synchronize medical asset placement within the area of operations.

Advises the G-2 on medical risks tied to environmental conditions, including terrain, weather,
altitude, and CBRN threats.

Works with the G-1, Patient Administration Division, and Medical Regulating Officer to
manage casualty reporting and guide patient movement decisions.

Coordinates with the G-4 and assistant chief of staff, signal (G-6) to prepare CASEVAC
platforms with the right medical supplies and communication systems.

Advises the G-1 and G-4 on the handling and tracking of deceased personnel in coordination
with Mortuary Affairs.

Supports the assistant chief of staff, training (G-7) in planning CASEVAC-related training and
readiness assessments.

Works closely with MEDEVAC planners to synchronize evacuation efforts and prevent
duplication.

Tracks medical resource use and compiles lessons learned to improve future CASEVAC
operations and sustainment planning.
•
Provost Marshal (Security Operations): Develops and executes security plans for CASEVAC routes
and platforms. Coordinates military police escorts when needed; and collaborates with G-2 and G-3
to assess threats and protect casualty movement.
•
Public Affairs Officer (Strategic Communication): Manages internal and external messaging about
CASEVAC operations. Maintains operational security in communications, provides casualty
updates to families as directed, and works with the G-1 to deliver timely, accurate, and respectful
information.
•
Engineer (Mobility and Infrastructure): Assesses and improves CASEVAC routes, including road
repair and bridging operations. Advises on terrain effects that may impact CASEVAC mobility and
works with the G-3 and G-4 to keep evacuation routes open and reliable.
