**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
**Integrating Medical Regulating and Casualty Evacuation Prioritization into Operations**
3-172. Medical regulating is a critical function that supports the effective use of CASEVAC and MEDEVAC
assets across all echelons. It requires close coordination between medical and nonmedical staff, including
operations, logistics, and personnel sections. Medical regulating links patients to the right MTF based on the
severity of injury, urgency of care, available transportation, and the status of MTF capacity.
3-173. An effective medical regulating system directs casualty flow to the appropriate medical treatment
facility at the right time. It prevents the overuse of specific MTFs while others remain underutilized. This
regulation helps keep MEDEVAC platforms available for follow-on missions and supports timely delivery
of care based on the severity and needs of each casualty. Medical regulating also improves the use of theater-
level strategic evacuation assets, including both air and ground MEDEVAC capabilities.
3-174. The process begins with the Patient Administration Division, the Medical Regulating Officer, and the
Surgeon (Medical Operations) who collect real-time information on patient status, treatment needs, and the
availability of MTFs. These individuals coordinate closely with the command surgeon, G-1, G-3, and G-4
staff to prioritize patients, assess platform availability, and recommend destinations. When MEDEVAC
aircraft are limited, the medical regulating process helps determine which casualties require urgent movement
and which can be moved by CASEVAC or delayed without risk to life or limb.
3-175. Strategic asset prioritization considers several factors, including the number and severity of casualties,
the urgency of operations, and the availability of routes, staging areas, or LZs. Medical regulating supports
this process by identifying which patients require surgical intervention, specialty care, or long-term
hospitalization. This prioritization helps prevent treatment backlogs and improves the overall flow of patients
throughout the operational area.
3-176. In addition to managing MEDEVAC, medical regulating also supports the use of CASEVAC
platforms. Trucks, tactical vehicles, and aircraft without onboard medical support may be used when
MEDEVAC is unavailable. These platforms are coordinated through the regulating process to move lower-
precedence casualties or to clear collection points during MASCAL events. While CASEVAC platforms may
not offer en route medical care, they remain essential for keeping casualty flow moving.
3-177. By incorporating CASEVAC into the broader evacuation plan, commanders help preserve
MEDEVAC capacity for the most critical patients. CASEVAC platforms can transport stabilized personnel
to AXPs or rear-area MTFs, particularly during large-scale operations. This tiered use of evacuation
platforms improves system efficiency and reduces delays in care. To be effective, units rehearse transitions
between MEDEVAC and CASEVAC and clearly define roles and responsibilities in their plans.
3-178. The success of medical regulating and CASEVAC prioritization depends on shared understanding and
integrated planning. Commanders, medical planners, and sustainment staff work together to synchronize
evacuation priorities, route security, patient tracking, and platform management. Real-time communication,
accurate reporting, and trained personnel are necessary to make timely decisions under stress.
3-179. Medical regulating helps the command allocate limited resources while maintaining combat power
and operational momentum. By balancing the demand for MEDEVAC and CASEVAC, and directing
casualties to the right location, medical regulating ensures that the force can sustain itself even during
large-scale, resource-constrained operations. For additional guidance on medical regulating, refer to
ATP 4-02.2 and ATP 4-02.55.
**Casualty Evacuation Support to Intratheater and Intertheater Evacuation**
3-180. CASEVAC is a vital part of military operations. It allows for the quick and safe transportation of
wounded Soldiers from the battlefield to MTF. CASEVAC plays a crucial role in both intratheater and
intertheater evacuations.
3-181. Intratheater evacuation refers to the movement of patients within a single theater of operation, for
example, from a forward operating base to a regional MTF. This type of evacuation is typically done using
tactical aircraft, like rotary-wing aircraft or planes, that are equipped with medical personnel and equipment
to provide care during transport.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**45**
**Chapter 3**
3-182. Intertheater evacuation involves moving patients between different theaters of operation, for example,
from a combat zone in one region to a MTF in another region. This type of evacuation is usually done using
strategic aircraft, like cargo planes or tanker planes, that are equipped with advanced medical equipment and
personnel.
3-183. CASEVAC supports both initial casualty movement and, when required, transport between MTFs. It
provides a flexible option when MEDEVAC is unavailable or when commanders need to move casualties to
the next role of care. In some situations, such as during company-level operations, a PSG may move a
casualty directly to a Role 1 battalion aid station if it is nearby and accessible. However, longer distances or
higher casualty volumes typically require a tiered evacuation process using designated AXPs and
MEDEVAC platforms. CASEVAC complements this system by bridging gaps and maintaining casualty flow
under challenging conditions.
3-184. CASEVAC operations can be challenging due to various factors such as distance, terrain, weather,
and patient acuity. The distance and terrain between the POI and the MTF can pose significant challenges,
particularly in harsh or hostile environments. Weather conditions like extreme temperatures, dust, or fog can
also impact the safety and effectiveness of CASEVAC operations. Additionally, the severity of the patient's
condition and the level of care required can affect the type of aircraft and medical personnel needed for
transport. To overcome these challenges, it is essential to have effective communication and coordination
between medical personnel, aircrew, and other stakeholders.
3-185. Safe and effective patient transport depends on developing standardized CASEVAC procedures and
conducting regular training and exercises. Standardized procedures promote consistency and safety across
different operational environments. Regular training prepares CASEVAC and MEDEVAC teams for both
intratheater and intertheater movements. When possible, CASEVAC operations should use medical
equipment and supplies authorized through existing procurement and sustainment processes, such as those
outlined in Common Tables of Allowances 8-100. This approach supports compatibility, reduces training
and maintenance gaps, and strengthens long-term readiness.
3-186. CASEVAC plays a vital role in both intratheater and intertheater patient movement. To reduce the
risk of further injury or complications, military medical personnel understand the unique challenges of
CASEVAC operations and apply best practices during patient transport. Effective CASEVAC requires close
coordination and clear communication between medical personnel, aircrews, and other supporting elements
to maintain continuity of care for WII personnel. By integrating CASEVAC platforms into both intratheater
and intertheater evacuation systems, the force can expand its ability to deliver care across wide operational
areas, preserving combat effectiveness, and saving lives.
**Liaison Officers**
3-187. A liaison officer (LNO) plays a critical role in facilitating communication and coordination between
units and allied forces for effective CASEVAC operations. These officers serve as direct links between units,
ensuring that CASEVAC protocols are followed and that resources are utilized efficiently. By coordinating
CASEVAC efforts, LNOs help reduce confusion, prevent overlapping of resources, and maintain a steady
flow of information that supports the operational effectiveness, and adaptability of the Army. LNOs enhance
inter-unit cooperation and streamline CASEVAC procedures, allowing for a seamless response to casualty
needs on the battlefield.
**Deconfliction**
3-188. Deconfliction in CASEVAC planning helps prevent delays and confusion by avoiding overextension
or duplication of resources. This process includes coordinating the timing, routing, and prioritization of
CASEVAC operations to reduce congestion and eliminate competition between units for the same medical
assets. Deconfliction supports the synchronization principles outlined in FM 3-0 by promoting an organized
and efficient flow of CASEVAC operations, minimizing interference, and improving the use of available
resources.
**46**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**

### CORPS, DIVISION, BRIGADE,ANDBATTALIONLEVEL

3-189. The success of CASEVAC operations depends on coordinated planning and execution across all
echelons. Corps provides operational oversight and links to joint and theater-level patient movement.
Division integrates corps guidance with brigade requirements and synchronizes evacuation across
subordinate formations. Brigade translates division direction into actionable plans for subordinate battalions.
Battalion plans and executes CASEVAC at the POI and initial handoff. While responsibilities differ by
echelon, planning and execution remain synchronized to preserve combat power and maintain operational
tempo.
3-190. The scale of combat operations influences which headquarters lead CASEVAC. When multiple corps
operate in a theater, the theater Army or joint force land component command coordinates evacuation across
the area of responsibility. Corps staffs synchronize CASEVAC in their assigned area, division staffs integrate
efforts between brigades, and brigade and battalion staffs conduct real-time execution.
3-191. Across all echelons, CASEVAC planning includes integrating into the OPORD, rehearsing evacuation
tasks, and maintaining flexibility to adjust under combat conditions. This coordinated approach ensures each
level supports the next, creating a seamless evacuation system from POI to higher roles of care. Although
each echelon shares the same goal, to evacuate casualties safely and efficiently, the scope, complexity, and
priorities differ.
**Corps Level**
3-192. Corps staffs integrate theater guidance, joint and multinational coordination, and division support
requirements into a unified evacuation plan. They manage policy, resource allocation, and operational-level
evacuation routes. Corps develops contingency options for regulated CASEVAC when MEDEVAC is
unavailable or insufficient, allocates nonmedical platforms as required, and sets evacuation priorities for
subordinate divisions.
**Division Staff**
3-193. The division is the Army’s primary tactical formation and serves as the link between operational level
and tactical level and is the link between corps direction and brigade execution. Division staffs manage
CASEVAC planning across brigades, allocate resources, and deconflict routes. They maintain situational
awareness of casualty flow to forecast when division-level CASEVAC resources are needed. Division staffs
also oversee training and readiness for CASEVAC platforms and coordinate with corps to request additional
support when required.
**Brigade Level**
3-194. Brigades translate division guidance into detailed CASEVAC plans that support battalion and
company operations. Brigade staffs monitor CCPs and AXPs, and coordinate with MTFs to move casualties
to higher roles of care. They allocate CASEVAC-capable platforms to battalions, monitor platform readiness,
and synchronize evacuation timelines with maneuver and fires to reduce risk to evacuation assets. Brigade
CASEVAC planning links to battalion execution through detailed tasking, rehearsals, and communications
architecture.
**Battalion Level**
3-195. Battalion staffs integrate CASEVAC into the MDMP to align with tactical operations. They assign
CASEVAC teams, identify evacuation routes, and ensure companies have the training, equipment, and
communications needed for execution. Battalion CASEVAC execution links company-level casualty
acquisition to brigade evacuation plans. CCPs, AXPs and the ambulance shuttle system provide a structured
method for continuous casualty movement and is typically managed at this level.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**47**
**Chapter 3**
**Medical Treatment Facilities**
3-196. Role 1 and Role 2 MTFs within brigade and battalion areas provide immediate care and prepare
patients for further evacuation. Continuous coordination between MTFs maintain patient flow and ensures
evacuation remains synchronized with operational tempo.
**Orders and Military Decision-Making Process**
3-197. Commanders and staff integrate CASEVAC planning into the MDMP to align it with the operation.
As the OPORD is developed, the staff incorporates CASEVAC tasks into the scheme of maneuver,
sustainment overlays, and synchronization matrices. These tasks include identifying evacuation routes,
selecting locations for CCPs, and assigning CASEVAC-capable platforms. Battalion orders reflect this
planning by assigning roles and responsibilities and providing the guidance subordinate units need to carry
out CASEVAC under expected conditions. Integrating CASEVAC throughout the MDMP helps support
timely and coordinated evacuation that protects combat power and keeps the mission on track.
3-198. Integrating CASEVAC into the MDMP also helps align evacuation tasks with the overall concept of
operations. During COA analysis and comparison, planners assess the feasibility of CASEVAC alongside
maneuver, sustainment, and protection considerations. CASEVAC details, such as evacuation routes, CCP
locations, evacuation triggers, and task assignments by echelon, are included in the OPORD. Rehearsals and
confirmation briefs allow units to validate the plan, improve coordination with supporting elements, and build
shared understanding across formations. This deliberate approach enables rapid and effective CASEVAC
under combat conditions, preserving combat power, and supporting mission continuity.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION PLANNING CONSIDERATIONS

3-199. CASEVAC in complex operational environments requires detailed planning and attention to unique
factors that affect both safety and effectiveness. In CBRN environments, contamination risks complicate
evacuation and demand strict mitigation procedures to protect casualties and personnel. Units train and equip
evacuation teams to operate in contaminated areas, reduce exposure, and prevent further harm. Mortuary
affairs must also be considered. The respectful handling, transport, and disposition of remains can be
particularly difficult in contested or resource-limited areas. Despite operational demands, units must treat the
deceased with dignity while maintaining mission continuity.
3-200. Multinational casualties introduce additional complexity to CASEVAC operations. Coordination with
allied and unified action partners is required to address differences in medical practices, languages, and
cultural expectations for casualty treatment. Clear, standardized protocols should be developed to support the
treatment and evacuation of multinational personnel while respecting operational and cultural differences.
Veterinary considerations also play a key role in operations involving MWAs. These animals contribute to
detection, security, and force protection missions. Maintaining their health and readiness is vital for mission
success and troop morale. These planning considerations, CBRN threats, mortuary affairs, multinational
coordination, and veterinary support, highlight the multifaceted nature of CASEVAC and the need for
adaptable, well-integrated strategies to support effective operations in diverse environments.

### CASUALTYEVACUATION IN ACHEMICAL, BIOLOGICAL, RADIOLOGICAL,ANDNUCLEAR

### ENVIRONMENT

3-201. Commanders conducting CASEVAC in CBRN environments protect their mission by evacuating
casualties without spreading contamination to personnel or critical assets. A well-developed CASEVAC plan
that accounts for CBRN hazards help maintain operational continuity and protects the health of the force.
Effective planning also conserves medical and operational resources by reducing the risk of cross-
contamination, which can lead to additional casualties and mission degradation. Commanders depend on
trained CASEVAC teams to execute evacuation tasks safely and efficiently, ensuring that contaminated or
exposed personnel are handled properly and receive the care they need without endangering others.
3-202. Key considerations for CASEVAC in a CBRN environment include establishing decontamination
sites close to, but outside of, contaminated areas, equipping CASEVAC personnel with PPE, and pre-
identifying evacuation routes that avoid contamination hazards. CASEVAC teams should also be trained to
recognize signs of CBRN exposure, as early detection of symptoms is critical to preserving life and
**48**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
preventing the spread of contamination. Additionally, MTFs receiving patients from contaminated areas
establish a patient decontamination site to prevent contamination from entering the collectively protected
environment of the MTF.

### MULTINATIONALCASUALTIES

3-203. Handling multinational casualties during CASEVAC operations requires early coordination and
continuous communication with allied and partner forces. Each force may follow different medical protocols,
casualty reporting procedures, and evacuation practices. FM 3-0 highlights the need for interoperability in
joint and coalition operations, and CASEVAC is no exception. CASEVAC teams are ready to work alongside
foreign medical personnel, even when those partners use different methods or type of care. Language barriers,
cultural norms, and differences in medical training may create challenges. To overcome these, CASEVAC
teams adapt their communication and coordination strategies to match the operational approach of allied
forces. This cooperation helps ensure that all coalition casualties receive timely treatment, maintain
continuity of care, and are evacuated with respect and professionalism.
3-204. Supporting multinational CASEVAC operations is critical to the commander’s mission as it fosters
trust and cooperation with coalition partners, enhancing overall mission, cohesion, and effectiveness.
Commanders understand that taking care of allied forces’ WII personnel strengthens alliances and builds
goodwill among partner nations. Properly coordinated CASEVAC for multinational casualties reflects the
command’s commitment to protecting all personnel under its responsibility, regardless of nationality, thereby
enhancing the operational unity of the coalition force and reinforcing mutual support on the battlefield.
3-205. In multinational operations, regulated CASEVAC may be used alongside or in place of MEDEVAC
when coordinated patient movement across borders or between coalition facilities is required. This occurs
when nonmedical platforms are deliberately tasked, resourced, and integrated into the patient movement
system to support large-scale or extended operations.
3-206. Planning for multinational CASEVAC in this regulated context includes:
•
Establishing shared communication protocols and medical reporting formats that are interoperable
with partner forces.
•
Developing pre-arranged agreements for cross-border evacuation, including diplomatic clearances
and customs procedures.
•
Ensuring that casualty tracking systems are compatible for joint use.
3-207. When regulated CASEVAC involves transfer between coalition medical facilities, planners account
for compatibility of patient movement items, litter systems, and essential medical devices. Incompatible
equipment can delay care or require unnecessary transfers.
3-208. CASEVAC teams operating in coalition environments should also be briefed on cultural
considerations that may affect treatment preferences, patient handling, or disposition of deceased personnel.
These cultural factors should be incorporated into rehearsals and mission planning to prevent
misunderstandings and maintain trust with partner forces and local populations.
3-209. These measures apply primarily to deliberate, pre-planned CASEVAC missions conducted in support
of regulated patient movement. Most tactical CASEVAC missions, moving casualties from the POI to the
nearest MTF, will not require such multinational equipment standardization but may still require basic
interoperability in communications and casualty reporting.

### VETERINARYCONSIDERATIONS

3-210. In environments where MWAs are used, such as military working dogs, veterinary considerations
become a significant part of CASEVAC planning. These animals are often critical to mission success; they
provide capabilities in detection, tracking, and security. FM 3-0 acknowledges the importance of supporting
all assets that contribute to mission objectives, and veterinary support in CASEVAC operations aligns with
this principle. Veterinary considerations include planning for the safe evacuation of WII animals, as well as
coordinating with veterinary medical personnel to provide immediate care. Just as with human casualties,
timely evacuation and treatment of working animals help maintain operational capabilities.
3-211. Veterinary CASEVAC supports the commander’s mission by preserving the operational effectiveness
of specialized capabilities provided by MWAs. The loss of a trained MWA can significantly reduce a unit’s
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**49**
**Chapter 3**
ability to detect explosives, conduct patrols, track personnel, or perform search-and-rescue operations. A
deliberate CASEVAC plan for WII animals demonstrates the commander’s commitment to sustaining these
capabilities and protecting critical resources. Safeguarding animal assets ensures the unit can continue
executing specialized tasks that directly support the mission.
3-212. CASEVAC teams tasked with supporting MWAs require specialized training and equipment to
provide safe, effective evacuation. These teams learn basic animal first aid and carry veterinary supplies to
stabilize WII MWAs before they reach a forward veterinary facility. Facilities such as Role 2 Veterinary
Service Support Team or Role 3 Veterinary Medical Treatment Station provide advanced care after initial
stabilization. Because MWAs have unique needs, CASEVAC platforms may need modifications or
containment systems to protect the animal and reduce stress or further injury. While the overall purpose of
CASEVAC remains the same, evacuating MWAs demands additional planning, specialized equipment, and
tailored training to achieve the best outcome.
3-213. Commanders allocate CASEVAC assets for MWAs based on mission priorities and the urgency of
the animal’s condition. When MEDEVAC platforms are not available, other vehicles may be used according
to standard operating procedures. In these cases, a handler or trained escort should accompany the animal
whenever possible to maintain continuity of care and reduce stress. This support reflects the Army’s broader
commitment to preserving operational capability and maintaining the welfare of critical force assets. For
more information on military working dogs and related procedures, refer to ATP 3-39.34.

### CASUALTYEVACUATIONUSINGCONTRACTEDAGENCIES

3-214. Commanders may use contracted agencies to support CASEVAC when military resources are limited
or unavailable. These agencies can provide ground, air, or maritime platforms that help fill critical gaps in
evacuation capability. However, integrating contracted CASEVAC into operations requires planning and
coordination. Planners evaluate the agency’s capabilities, understand any operational limits, and ensure the
service fits within the mission timeline. CASEVAC remains a military responsibility, and contracted support
is treated as a supporting task that aligns with the operation’s requirements, not as a stand-alone solution.
3-215. When using contracted CASEVAC platforms, commanders and staff assess several key factors. These
include the agency’s response time, platform reliability, and any restrictions on movement. For instance,
some contractors may not operate at night or during bad weather or may face limits on payload or range.
Planners address cross-border clearance requirements, which may delay or prevent evacuation. Finally,
funding and authorization is in place before a mission begins. Staff identify the approving authority, the
method of payment, and any sustainment or accountability procedures to support the use of contracted
CASEVAC platforms.
3-216. Cultural and ethnic considerations are also critical when working with contracted agencies, especially
in regions where local customs or sensitivities may influence operations. For example, contracted personnel
may need cultural training to align with host-nation practices or military protocols. Furthermore, the potential
need for "tail-to-tail" transfers, where patients are moved between contracted and military aircraft, requires
logistical planning, particularly for long-distance evacuations. Contracts should explicitly define whether the
agency will transport casualties directly to MTFs or local civilian hospitals.
3-217. Contracted platforms may support casualty movement when military assets are unavailable, but they
often come with unique limitations. Unlike MEDEVAC or dedicated CASEVAC operations, contracted
transport may lack onboard medical personnel or essential equipment. While this does not change the
classification of the movement, if no en-route care is provided, it remains CASEVAC, it does introduce
planning risks that differ from standard Army platforms. For example, compatibility issues between military
medical gear and contracted vehicles can delay care. Commanders and planners assess these risks ahead of
time and develop mitigation strategies, such as attaching military medical teams to contracted platforms or
preloading essential equipment to reduce response time and preserve patient care standards.
3-218. Coordinating with contracted agencies for CASEVAC requires a hands-on approach and early
engagement. In urgent situations, planners should meet directly with agency representatives to confirm
timelines, clarify responsibilities, and identify any gaps in capability. These meetings help build trust and
improve shared understanding. Commanders and staff treat contracted CASEVAC as part of the mission, not
**50**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Planning Casualty Evacuation Operations**
as a separate or optional task. Early coordination ensures these assets are ready to support the operation and
aligned with unit timelines, threat conditions, and evacuation priorities.
3-219. Contracted CASEVAC support aligns with the Army’s operational framework, including guidance
from FM 3-0, FM 4-0, and FM 6-0. Planners integrate these platforms within the scheme of maneuver,
sustainment planning, and medical support operations. CASEVAC conducted by contracted assets remains a
supporting task and requires deliberate synchronization across all echelons. To do this, planners coordinate
with operational contract support personnel and follow the procedures outlined in ATP 4-10. This
coordination ensures that contracted CASEVAC contributes to operational reach, freedom of action, and
continuity of care. When integrated properly, contracted platforms can help preserve combat power and
expand the Army’s ability to move casualties across contested or resource-limited environments.

### Chapter 4

## Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon

Executing CASEVAC is a dynamic task that supports ongoing operations and responds to
battlefield conditions in real time. Once a plan is in place, leaders at all echelons coordinate
and control the movement of casualties under pressure, often in contested environments and
with limited resources. This chapter focuses on how units execute CASEVAC as part of the
larger fight, including how they initiate evacuation based on triggers, manage communication
and reporting, and adjust to operational changes. It also explains the roles of mission and launch
authorities, the importance of rehearsals, and the actions required to maintain tempo and
preserve life during execution.

## INTEGRATING CASUALTY EVACUATION STRATEGIES INTO

## MILITARY OPERATIONS

4-1. CASEVAC planning is a deliberate commander’s task that sustains operational endurance and preserves
combat power. Planning links directly to the OPORD and synchronizes CASEVAC with MEDEVAC. It
accounts for mission variables, expected casualty load, evacuation timelines, and operational constraints such
as mobility restrictions, degraded communications, or threats along lines of communication. CASEVAC
planning also anticipates MASCAL events, identify critical decision points, and link evacuation requirements
to the scheme of maneuver to prevent disruption of combat operations.
4-2. Brigade and battalion staffs translate higher-level CASEVAC priorities into actionable plans that align
collection points, AXPs, and evacuation routes with maneuver graphics and sustainment overlays. Brigade
staffs integrate CASEVAC into supports rehearsals alongside other key actions such as detainee collection,
refuel-on-the-move points, and support area displacement. Battalions focus on POI collection and movement
into the ambulance shuttle system, using organic and nonmedical assets when required. Both echelons plan
for mobility support, such as engineer route clearance or fording equipment, to enable CASEVAC in complex
terrain.
4-3. In joint and multinational operations, CASEVAC planning requires early agreement on communications,
reporting formats, and patient movement procedures. Commanders identify platform compatibility issues and
resolve them before operations begin, especially for patient movement items and en route care equipment.
Planning should include contingencies for when MEDEVAC assets are insufficient or unavailable, and
CASEVAC bridges the gap. Coordination with coalition partners should designate specific CCPs, AXPs, and
medical treatment facilities by name to reduce confusion caused by language or doctrinal differences.
4-4. Execution in contested or high-threat environments requires deliberate route security, close coordination
with maneuver elements, and synchronization with operational timelines. CASEVAC accounts for possible
relocation of receiving MTFs during mobile defense or rapid advances, and units should plan alternate
destinations in advance. Both ground and air CASEVAC may be required to support distributed operations,
with nonmedical platforms configured for casualty movement when dedicated ambulances are not available.
CASEVAC should be integrated into battle drills for mass casualty response, ensuring rapid triage,
evacuation, and coordination with medical regulating channels.
4-5. After each mission, evacuation platforms undergo immediate reconstitution to maintain readiness. This
includes inspecting, cleaning, and refitting first aid and triage supplies, and verifying communications and
mobility systems. Commanders should incorporate AARs into this process to capture lessons learned, refine
SOPs, and improve interoperability with supporting units and coalition partners. Continuous readiness of
CASEVAC assets ensures the formation can respond to future casualty incidents without operational delay.

## EXECUTION OF ARMY CASUALTY EVACUATION

4-6. Army commanders and leaders recognize CASEVAC as more than just a means of saving lives, it is
essential for maintaining operational continuity, preserving combat effectiveness, and sustaining the will to
fight. The rapid evacuation of casualties reassures Soldiers that they will receive care if they are wounded,
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**53**
**Chapter 4**
reinforcing unit cohesion, morale, and resilience under fire. By ensuring that forces remain confident in their
evacuation capabilities, CASEVAC contributes directly to a unit’s ability to continue fighting and accomplish
its mission.

### PLATOONLEVEL

**Initiate Evacuation and Protect the**
4-7. At the platoon level, CASEVAC execution is immediate
and hands-on. The platoon leader and PSG ensure that all
Soldiers are trained in TCCC and understand basic evacuation
procedures. These leaders oversee the rapid assessment of
casualties, the application of lifesaving care, and the
movement of wounded Soldiers to secure pickup points. They
also coordinate with company-level assets for evacuation
support. The speed and effectiveness of CASEVAC at this
level reflect the unit’s training, discipline, and ability to act
under pressure. Well-trained Soldiers who understand their
roles in the CASEVAC process help maintain combat power
and reduce preventable deaths on the battlefield.
**Wounded**
Platoon leaders and PSGs manage
CASEVAC at the POI. They lead the
rapid assessment, lifesaving care, and
movement of wounded Soldiers to the
platoon CCP. They coordinate pickup
with company-level assets and keep
communications active. By acting
quickly and keeping the platoon
informed, they preserve life and combat
power under fire.
4-8. A clearly written platoon-level SOP for CASEVAC is
essential. It standardizes responsibilities across squads and teams, helping each Soldier understand their role
during an evacuation. The SOP identifies critical capabilities that are preserved, such as crew-served weapons
teams and radio operators, while outlining how other personnel or resources can support casualty movement
when needed. This structured approach allows the platoon to adapt without compromising mission readiness.
A reliable SOP also improves coordination during rehearsals and live operations, strengthening the platoon’s
ability to respond when casualties occur. For formation-specific SOP templates and tactical implementation
guidance, refer to applicable formation type doctrine.
**Preparation and Training**
4-9. Effective CASEVAC operations require deliberate preparation and regular, realistic training. Platoon
and squad leaders train Soldiers in manual carries, improvised litter construction, multihinged folding litter
deployment, standard litter carry techniques and vehicle loading procedures. Soldiers need to understand the
unit’s CASEVAC SOP, be familiar with likely casualty scenarios, and conduct rehearsals under conditions
that reflect the operational environment. These preparations reduce the risk of further injury during movement
and speed transfer to higher roles of care. Leaders plan evacuation routes, test communications plans, and
incorporate CASEVAC rehearsals into routine training to maintain readiness.
4-10. In addition to individual training, litter teams and vehicle drivers are familiar with the locations of
CCPs, ambulance loading points, AXPs, and MTFs. Each platform designated for CASEVAC should have
at least one CLS-trained Soldier, with CLS bag, as part of its crew. Timely communication is also critical for
minimizing delays and reducing risks. Drivers and litter teams are kept updated on any changes to the
locations of CCPs, ambulance loading points, AXPs, and MTFs throughout the mission. This level of
preparation and training enables CASEVAC operations to be executed efficiently, reducing exposure to
hazards, while ensuring prompt medical support to WII personnel.
**Physical Demands and Operational Flexibility**
4-11. CASEVAC is physically demanding, and leaders recognize these challenges when planning evacuation
operations. They should rotate litter team members as needed to sustain the effectiveness of CASEVAC
support and prevent fatigue. In some situations, manual carrying may be necessary due to tactical constraints,
limited litter availability, casualty conditions, or difficult terrain such as dense jungle, mountainous regions,
or urban environments.
4-12. Additionally, operational flexibility is critical, as CASEVAC methods adapt to terrain, threat levels,
and available evacuation assets. Leaders continuously assess mission conditions and select the most effective
evacuation approach, ensuring that casualties receive timely medical care while maintaining operations
security and mobility.
**54**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
**Considerations at Casualty Collection Points**
4-13. Litter teams should be well trained in medical tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTP) specific to
operating within CCPs. This includes managing different sections for killed in action, contaminated
casualties requiring decontamination, WII enemy detainees, and civilians, as well as having a security plan
for handling enemy casualties and equipment. Security and accountability are paramount; sensitive items
such as weapons, maps, and communication equipment from casualties are secured and managed responsibly.
**Coordination and Communication**
4-14. CASEVAC remains a vital supporting task at the platoon level and is deliberately planned and regularly
rehearsed. Developing a platoon-level CASEVAC SOP requires coordination with the unit’s medical
personnel to align procedures with tactical requirements. This coordination helps ensure that Soldiers know
how to safely move casualties, load them onto available evacuation platforms, and carry out their roles during
movement. Medical personnel also provide critical input on configuring the CCP. Their guidance shapes
decisions about security, triage layout, aid and litter team positions, drop-off procedures for sensitive items,
and medical resupply staging. These elements support a functional and responsive CCP that operates under
pressure and remains synchronized with the platoon’s scheme of maneuver.
4-15. While the SOP sets the foundation, CASEVAC procedures are refined through mission-specific
planning. Communication methods, including radio frequencies, call signs, message formats, and digital tools
such as Joint Battle Command–Platform, are addressed in the unit’s signal operating instructions and updated
with each OPORD. Visual signals, such as colored smoke or infrared strobes, may be addressed in SOPs but
are confirmed or modified during planning and rehearsals. Planners should identify CCPs, loading points,
AXPs, and supported MTFs during mission planning and update these details during key tactical transitions,
such as crossing phase lines or executing a passage of lines. CASEVAC and MEDEVAC planning is
integrated into the overall scheme of maneuver and sustainment operations and should be rehearsed and
refined through the MDMP or TLP processes at every echelon.

### COMPANYLEVEL

**Organize the CCP and Lead Tactical**
4-16. At the company level, CASEVAC plans are refined,
through TLPs, to address the specific operational needs and
resources available within the company’s assigned area.
Company commanders focus on ensuring that every Soldier is
familiar with casualty handling protocols and knows the
locations of the nearest CCPs. This level of CASEVAC
planning requires meticulous attention to detail, particularly
in tracking personnel, logistical assets, and medical supplies
(such as CLS bags, Warrior Aid Litter Kit [WALK] or
individual first aid kits), evacuation platforms, and essential
equipment like litters and straps.
**Movement**
Company commanders and 1SGs turn
the battalion CASEVAC plan into
action. They establish company-level
CCPs, organize litter teams, and assign
unit vehicles to CASEVAC tasks. They
manage medical supply readiness and
lead rehearsals. Their leadership
ensures the unit can move casualties
quickly while continuing to fight.
4-17. Commanders define the logistical requirements for
CASEVAC to prevent gaps in medical support, especially during large-scale combat operations or in
environments where communication breaks down. They lead by enforcing a disciplined approach that
prepares every Soldier to perform essential CASEVAC tasks under pressure. This clarity and structure allow
units to maintain continuous support for wounded personnel, even when conditions are difficult. A well-
organized CASEVAC plan directly supports the unit’s larger operational goals by preserving combat power
and reinforcing force health protection.
4-18. The unit’s CASEVAC plan is essential to successful MEDEVAC operations. It aligns all levels of
command and prepares them to carry out evacuation tasks while staying focused on mission objectives. A
clear and well-communicated plan allows company commanders to help subordinate units coordinate and
support one another during medical emergencies. The plan ties CASEVAC efforts into the broader operation,
linking evacuation procedures with mission needs, and improving the unit's ability to respond to battlefield
casualties. A strong CASEVAC plan promotes cohesion and contributes to both mission success and troop
morale.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**55**
**Chapter 4**
4-19. The company level plays a critical role in the execution of CASEVAC operations, ensuring timely and
effective CASEVAC within its assigned area. However, CASEVAC planning and coordination occur at
multiple echelons, with key differences in roles, responsibilities, and focus between the company and higher
headquarters (battalion, brigade, division, and corps staffs).
4-20. The key differences are:
•
Scope of responsibility: The company level is responsible for planning and executing CASEVAC
operations within their assigned area. In contrast, higher echelons manage broader efforts
encompassing multiple companies, battalions, or brigades.
•
Level of detail: The company level focuses on the detailed execution of CASEVAC operations,
including resource allocation, coordination with subordinate units, and real-time execution. In
contrast, higher echelons focus on the bigger picture, including policy development, strategic
resource allocation, and multi-echelon coordination.
•
Decision-making authority: The company level has limited authority and focuses mainly on
executing plans and adapting to mission requirements. Battalion, brigade, division, and corps staffs
have greater decision-making power to allocate resources and shape CASEVAC priorities at
operational and strategic levels.
•
Resource allocation: The company level has limited CASEVAC resources, relying on organic
medical personnel, unit vehicles, and available assets. Higher echelons have broader access to
personnel, vehicles, aircraft, and MTF, allowing for more comprehensive CASEVAC coordination.
•
Coordination and integration: At the company level, leaders coordinate with adjacent units and the
battalion staff to carry out CASEVAC operations without delays or confusion. Higher echelons,
brigade, division, and corps, coordinate across levels to build a fully integrated evacuation system
that supports the entire operation. This coordination helps move casualties through the chain of care,
even in complex or fast-moving missions.
•
Mission impact assessment: Company commanders identify the point at which CASEVAC supports
risks degrading their unit’s ability to accomplish its primary mission. CASEVAC operations should
be carefully balanced against tactical objectives to prevent mission failure or excessive strain on
combat power.
•
Training and rehearsals: While higher echelons establish a tactical standing operating procedure,
the company level is responsible for ensuring CASEVAC teams are properly trained, and that the
unit rehearses CASEVAC procedures. Regular drills enhance coordination, response times, and
overall CASEVAC effectiveness in combat scenarios.
4-21. The company level is focused on the tactical execution of CASEVAC operations, including:
•
Allocating resources: The company commander allocates resources to support CASEVAC
operations. This includes nonmedical platforms, personnel, and available medical assets, to support
CASEVAC operations.
•
Coordinating with subordinate units: The company commander works closely with platoon and
squad leaders to carry out CASEVAC operations in a smooth and timely way. This coordination
ensures that casualty movement stays aligned with the battalion’s overall evacuation plan and
supports the mission’s medical priorities. By maintaining clear communication and shared
understanding, the company keeps CASEVAC efforts effective under changing battlefield
conditions.
•
Executing plans: The company commander executes plans and adjusts operations based on higher-
echelon directives. They must also adapt to changing battlefield conditions as necessary.
•
Providing situational awareness: The company commander maintains constant communication with
higher echelons to provide situational awareness. This includes reporting the status of CASEVAC
operations and identifying challenges that may require battalion-level intervention.
•
Ensuring training and rehearsals: The company commander makes sure that CASEVAC teams are
properly trained and that all Soldiers rehearse the evacuation plan. These rehearsals align the
company with the battalion’s priorities and improve coordination, timing, and execution. Regular
training prepares the unit to carry out CASEVAC tasks, even under stress or in complex combat
conditions.
**56**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
4-22. The scope of responsibility, level of detail, and decision-making authority differ at each echelon of
command:
•
Scope of Responsibility and Available Resources:

Company Level: Oversees CASEVAC operations in a defined, localized area, focusing on
immediate CASEVAC and tactical medical care. Resources are limited to organic medical
personnel (combat medics), CLS, unit vehicles, and litter teams.

Battalion Level: Manages CASEVAC across multiple companies, ensuring synchronization of
efforts and prioritization of evacuation assets. Additional medical resources (such as battalion
aid stations, ground ambulances) allow for more structured evacuation.

Brigade Level: Encompasses multiple battalions and coordinates CASEVAC at an operational
level, ensuring medical planning and resource allocation. Brigades have access to echelons
above brigade capabilities, including higher-level treatment facilities, aerial CASEVAC assets,
and specialized medical units that extend beyond battalion-level resources.
•
Level of Detail and Decision-Making Authority:

Company Level: Focuses on the immediate execution of CASEVAC procedures. It carries out
orders within its localized operational environment.

Battalion Level: Takes a broader approach to CASEVAC efforts, ensuring synchronization
across multiple companies. It is responsible for allocating medical resources and adjusting
evacuation routes based on mission conditions.

Brigade Level: Directs CASEVAC efforts across multiple battalions, integrating higher medical
echelons, and managing CASEVAC in conjunction with air and ground MEDEVAC assets.
Brigades also liaise with joint or coalition partners, MTF, and echelons above brigade resources
to sustain operations over prolonged engagements.
4-23. Company and battalion levels focus on the tactical execution of CASEVAC operations. At the company
level, leaders establish CCPs, move casualties from the POI, and request evacuation support from higher
headquarters. Battalion leaders provide C2 over multiple companies, synchronize CASEVAC efforts across
the unit’s area of operations, and adjust plans in real time based on the fight. Brigade and division staffs
remain tactically engaged by directing evacuation priorities, coordinating with supporting medical assets,
and managing resources across subordinate formations. Corps and higher echelons address broader
operational or strategic considerations, such as long-range evacuation, theater-level policy, and sustainment
alignment. This tiered approach builds a responsive CASEVAC system that links tactical execution with
operational reach.
**Integration and Synchronization**
4-24. At the company level, CASEVAC integration begins with aligning the unit’s evacuation plan to higher
headquarters’ concept of support, ensuring it is nested in Annex C (Operations) and Annex F (Sustainment)
and reflected in company-level OPORDs. The company commander, first sergeant, and the senior combat
medic jointly develop CASEVAC TTPs that account for assigned personnel, mission profile, and available
assets. This includes identifying primary and alternate CCPs, designating evacuation platforms, and
rehearsing handover procedures with supported and supporting units. Company-level CASEVAC plans
incorporate the commander’s communications plan, security measures for evacuation platforms, and risk
controls identified in the unit’s deliberate risk assessment.
4-25. Squad and platoon leaders are trained to execute decentralized CASEVAC in degraded conditions,
using pre-identified routes, visual signals, and alternate casualty collection techniques if communications are
disrupted. Leaders rehearse CASEVAC as part of the support rehearsals, integrating with maneuver and fires
to maintain operational tempo during casualty movement. Platoon-level rehearsals confirm that load plans,
litter teams, and casualty tracking procedures are synchronized with the company scheme of maneuver and
higher headquarters’ reporting standards.
4-26. The company conducts CASEVAC training to build redundancy and resilience, ensuring backup
platforms, alternate CCP locations, and cross-trained personnel are available, while fostering adaptability to
adjust routes and procedures in real time. Integration with attached or habitually supporting enablers, such as
engineers, military police, or host-nation security, improves route security and expedites movement to the
battalion CCP or ambulance exchange points. Following each mission, the company conducts an AAR to
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**57**
**Chapter 4**
capture lessons, update SOPs, and refine CASEVAC rehearsals, sustaining readiness for subsequent
operations.
**Standard Operating Procedures**
4-27. SOPs for CASEVAC at the company level provide a clear and organized framework for managing
casualties. These SOPs are designed to be flexible, allowing them to adapt to different mission plans and
environmental conditions. They outline specific TTP that streamline the CASEVAC process, ensuring that
actions can be executed with precision under challenging circumstances.
4-28. Well-rehearsed and clearly communicated SOPs allow units to adjust CASEVAC operations as
battlefield conditions change. These SOPs give Soldiers the confidence to act immediately, even when the
situation is uncertain or resources are limited. A structured and flexible approach reduces confusion, supports
timely medical care, and helps the unit maintain operational effectiveness. By adapting SOPs to each mission
and environment, commanders make CASEVAC a reliable supporting task that reinforces the unit’s ability
to fight and win.
4-29. Small-unit CASEVAC SOPs align with higher command SOPs and support the overall HSS plan in
the OPORD. These SOPs link company- and platoon-level efforts with the medical elements at battalion and
brigade levels, such as the maneuver battalion medical platoon and the brigade support medical company.
By doing so, they help maintain a smooth flow of casualty care across all roles. The SOPs also outline each
step of care, beginning with self-aid and buddy aid, then progressing through CLS support, and finally to
care by trained combat medics. This progression reflects the principles of TCCC and ensures casualties
receive the right care at each stage. For more information on HSS planning, refer to FM 4-0 and ATP 4-02.55.
4-30. SOPs also prepare units for various scenarios, including MASCAL events, by outlining the progression
of care and evacuation from the POI to more advanced medical support as the situation evolves. By
addressing a wide range of potential situations, these SOPs provide a structured and adaptable framework for
CASEVAC operations, enabling units to respond to medical needs in a coordinated and systematic manner.
**Security and Tactical Considerations**
4-31. Security plays a vital role in every CASEVAC mission. It affects the safety of casualties, medical
personnel, and escort teams. The most dangerous phase often occurs when moving casualties from the POI
to the platoon CCP. During this phase, aid and litter teams operate in exposed conditions and may come
under fire. Commanders assess enemy activity, terrain, and the posture of friendly forces to decide how to
protect these routes and whether to assign escort elements. Even a small number of casualties can make a
squad or platoon combat ineffective. According to FM 1-02.2, units that fall below 85 percent personnel
strength are no longer fully operational. A squad reduced by just two Soldiers may drop below this threshold.
CASEVAC plans recognize how these losses affect combat power and build evacuation strategies that
prevent further degradation.
4-32. At every echelon, commanders weigh the urgency of evacuation against the tactical risk. This means
balancing the need to move wounded Soldiers with the danger posed by enemy fire, degraded terrain, or
limited visibility. CASEVAC plans should aim to protect evacuation teams while maintaining operational
momentum. Survivable and secure routes enable medical personnel to reach the wounded, begin treatment,
and sustain the casualty flow. Leaders treat CASEVAC as a mission-critical support task, not a separate
effort. By doing so, they preserve combat power, keep units in the fight, and improve the chances of a rapid
RTD. Timely evacuation reduces the likelihood of medical complications and helps prevent culmination due
to personnel shortfalls.
**Leader Role in Casualty Evacuation**
4-33. The 1SG manages CASEVAC operations at the company level, just as the PSG does at the platoon
level. While both noncommissioned officers traditionally handle logistics, such as resupply and vehicle
movement, their responsibilities also include establishing and managing CCPs and coordinating evacuation.
When platoons operate forward of the company headquarters, the PSG establishes the platoon CCP and
oversees casualty movement to the company CCP. These duties support the timely evacuation of WII
personnel and help maintain the unit’s combat power during sustained operations. Together, the 1SG and
**58**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
PSG create a coordinated CASEVAC system that functions across all elements of the company. For further
detail on CASEVAC tasks by echelon and formation type, readers should consult the appropriate company-
or platoon-level doctrine
4-34. To support this role, 1SGs lead regular CASEVAC training and rehearsals as part of the unit’s warrior
tasks and battle drills. These events integrate casualty care and evacuation with other core Soldier skills,
shoot, move, communicate, and survive, reflecting the Army’s recognition that protecting and caring for
wounded personnel is a deliberate task, not an afterthought. Soldiers act immediately to provide care and
begin movement toward a CCP, rather than waiting for medical teams to arrive. By leading rehearsals that
include casualty movement, CCP setup, and security measures, the 1SG builds readiness across the formation
and strengthens the unit’s ability to maintain combat power under fire.
4-35. Through strong leadership and active involvement in casualty planning, the 1SG builds a unit culture
focused on readiness, accountability, and rapid response. By making CASEVAC part of every operation,
they help safeguard the unit’s most valuable asset, its people. This integrated approach strengthens the
company’s ability to fight and recover, ensuring that both the mission and the welfare of Soldiers remain a
top priority.
**Support and Compliance with Standard Operating Procedure**
4-36. Company leaders ensure that CASEVAC plans at the platoon level align with the company’s SOPs.
They are responsible for preparing their units to conduct CASEVAC operations in a variety of environments,
including those that are harsh or unpredictable. This preparation includes checking that enough CLS–
qualified personnel are assigned, that medical supply shortages are identified and corrected, and that vehicles
set aside for CASEVAC are properly marked in line with Geneva Convention standards. These steps help
ensure the unit is ready to respond when casualties occur.
4-37. Leaders embed CASEVAC procedures into the unit’s regular training cycle to build consistency,
confidence, and skill across the formation. This consistent exposure helps every Soldier understand their role
in the evacuation process, from casualty identification to movement to the CCP. Repeated practice not only
improves the speed and accuracy of the response but also reinforces the unit’s ability to follow established
protocols under stress. By building CASEVAC into the training culture, leaders improve mission readiness
and strengthen the unit’s ability to care for its wounded during combat operations.
**Operational Readiness and the Unit Casualty Evacuation Plan**
4-38. The company’s CASEVAC plan is essential to maintaining operational readiness. It gives the unit a
clear method for providing timely medical care while continuing to meet mission objectives. The plan
depends on coordination across all echelons, as well as adaptability during execution and thorough
preparation before operations begin. When leaders at every level understand their roles and rehearse the plan
regularly, the company can carry out CASEVAC operations without disrupting the larger mission.
4-39. A well-structured CASEVAC plan enables the company to maintain mission continuity, even in the
face of casualties, by seamlessly integrating medical support into operational activities. Comprehensive
planning not only supports mission success but also reinforces the Army’s commitment to safeguarding the
health and welfare of its Soldiers, ensuring that operational goals are met while prioritizing the care and
well-being of its personnel.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**

### BATTALIONLEVEL

**Synchronize Resources and Maintain**
**the Flow**
Battalion commanders and staff control
CASEVAC operations across the
company formations. They position
CASEVAC assets, track casualty flow,
and coordinate with adjacent units and
MEDEVAC. They also monitor supply

coordination keeps evacuation moving
and avoids bottlenecks that could
overwhelm lower echelons.
4-40. Battalion-level
leaders
execute
the
brigade’s
CASEVAC plan by converting it into detailed actions that
achieve the brigade commander’s intent and align with the
battalion’s tactical mission. The battalion S-3 leads
integration of casualty movement into the scheme of

manages planning timelines to keep the plan synchronized
with brigade operations. The S-4 identifies, stages, and
sustains CASEVAC platforms, such as repurposed tactical

The S-1 maintains casualty tracking, projects evacuation
needs, and manages accountability throughout the operation.
4-41. The battalion medical officer or physician assistant
provides clinical guidance, recommends CCP locations, and ensures CASEVAC planning supports the
overall evacuation plan. The medical officer also trains unit personnel on casualty handling, equipment use,
and standardized triage marking procedures, and advises when organic MEDEVAC capability may be
exceeded and requires augmentation. Where necessary, the medical officer coordinates the integration of
CASEVAC into the brigade’s ambulance shuttle system to maintain continuous patient flow.
4-42. By synchronizing across staff sections and aligning with brigade-level planning, the battalion builds a
CASEVAC plan that supports the operational framework and moves casualties from the POI to the next role
of care. This synchronization ensures battalion-level actions directly reinforce higher-echelon priorities,
maintain operational momentum, and preserve combat power throughout the mission.
4-43. Each battalion operates as part of a larger brigade plan, which may involve shaping operations, decisive
attacks, or support tasks across a dispersed battlefield. While higher echelons, such as corps, division, and
brigade, focus on setting priorities and allocating resources, battalion staffs focus on the details of tactical
execution. This includes selecting CCPs, prepositioning CASEVAC assets, coordinating with company
elements, and managing casualty flow during operations. Battalion staffs adjust plans as battlefield conditions
change, responding to new threats, route closures, or surges in casualty numbers. These adjustments stay
aligned with the brigade’s overall effort to preserve combat power and maintain momentum.
4-44. CASEVAC at the battalion level is a deliberate, rehearsed task, not an improvised response. The
battalion staff allocates CASEVAC platforms, assigns personnel, and ensures all units understand their roles
in casualty collection and movement. Coordination across subordinate companies helps reduce delays and
prevents CCP congestion. Battalion planners also maintain communication with adjacent units and higher
headquarters to keep CASEVAC routes open and share situational updates. During execution, the staff
reports key information, such as the number of casualties evacuated, current platform status, and delays, to
brigade headquarters. These updates inform decision-making without replacing formal medical reporting
channels.
4-45. While brigade and division staffs provide planning guidance and resources, battalion staffs operate
closer to the POI and manage the fight as it unfolds. Their actions connect company-level execution with the
operational framework developed by higher echelons. By managing CASEVAC as a tactical task, battalion
leaders help prevent bottlenecks, maintain evacuation momentum, and support timely medical treatment.
This coordination keeps CASEVAC synchronized with maneuver, supports survivability, and contributes
directly to mission success during large-scale combat operations.
**Key Staff, Functions, and Teams**
4-46. CASEVAC planning and execution rely on the combined efforts of leaders and staff across the
battalion. Medical officers oversee CCPs and coordinate with evacuation platforms to manage patient
movement. Platoon leaders translate the battalion’s CASEVAC plan into tactical actions, making sure
Soldiers understand how to execute evacuation under fire. Logistics personnel keep evacuation platforms
fueled and supplied while distributing medical materiel to forward positions. These roles align with each

**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
unit’s position in the chain of command and support the broader CASEVAC effort across echelons. When
leaders at each level synchronize their actions, they create a seamless evacuation process that moves
casualties from the POI to higher roles of care without delay. This integration reflects the commander's
operational priorities and reinforces the Army’s focus on unity of effort during large-scale combat operations.
**Casualty Evacuation Flow**
4-47. Battalion-level CASEVAC operations are managed by the command post and key staff leaders under
the commander’s intent. The battalion S-3, in coordination with the S-1, medical platoon leader, and senior
medic, monitors casualty flow from the POI to the CCP and onward to higher roles of care. These leaders
track routes, adjust timelines, and coordinate with MEDEVAC assets or adjacent units based on changes in
the tactical situation. They also update subordinate units on any reroutes or delays to avoid congestion and
maintain momentum. This staff-driven process allows the commander to focus on the fight while ensuring
that CASEVAC operations remain responsive and synchronized with the overall mission.
4-48. Managing casualty flow helps the battalion maintain its combat power while meeting the medical needs
of its Soldiers. To do this well, units conduct regular rehearsals, keep communication open across all
echelons, and stay flexible as conditions change. A well-structured CASEVAC plan gives commanders the
tools to respond and keep operations on track.

**Integrate CASEVAC into Maneuver**
4-49. At the brigade staff level, planners focus on meeting
immediate tactical needs for CASEVAC across the brigade's
assigned area. They identify available nonmedical platforms,
such as trucks or utility vehicles, and assign them to support
CASEVAC when dedicated medical assets are unavailable or
committed elsewhere. Brigade staff develop short-term
CASEVAC plans that address unit-specific requirements,
adjust to shifting priorities, and align with the overall
maneuver plan.
**and Sustainment**

the larger scheme of maneuver. They
assign nonmedical platforms, direct
support between battalions, and manage
CASEVAC priorities during high-
casualty events. The brigade
coordinates with higher and lower

current fight. By overseeing

the unit’s ability to save lives and
maintain pressure on the enemy.
4-50. The brigade staff synchronizes CASEVAC operations
by coordinating with battalion-level leaders and medical

CCP locations, and setting standard reporting formats.
Brigade medical planners help deconflict evacuation
timelines, assess overflow risk at Role 1 MTFs, and redirect casualties when needed. They also coordinate
handoff to higher roles of care, including Role 2 MTFs, and provide updates to the brigade surgeon and
operations officer. When evacuation routes are blocked or platforms become unavailable, the brigade staff
helps redirect movement, assign alternate resources, or prioritize evacuation based on medical urgency and
mission requirements. This oversight helps maintain flow from the POI to Role 2 while minimizing delays
and reducing confusion under pressure.
4-51. By focusing on coordination, flexibility, and effective use of resources, the brigade staff supports
CASEVAC as a critical task that preserves combat power. Integrating nonmedical platforms and personnel
into evacuation planning extends the brigade’s ability to respond during high casualty events or limited
resource conditions. This approach allows medical assets to concentrate on urgent cases, while still ensuring
that all casualties receive timely evacuation and follow-on care.
**Orders**
4-52. Division and brigade staffs communicate CASEVAC guidance through the OPORD, placing primary
planning in Annex C (Operations) to direct execution, with supporting coordination in Annex F
(Sustainment). These documents outline evacuation procedures, CCPs, routes, and responsibilities.
CASEVAC planning aligns with higher-echelon guidance while addressing the specific terrain, threats, and
resources available to the division or brigade. During the sustainment rehearsal, staff refine CASEVAC
coordination, clarify task assignments, and address potential friction points. Orders also include marking
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**61**
**Chapter 4**
guidance for CASEVAC platforms in accordance with the Geneva Conventions. Publishing clear CASEVAC
instructions and rehearsing them with subordinate units strengthens coordination, reduces confusion, and
supports timely casualty movement in high-threat environments.
4-53. Division and brigade leaders ensure subordinate units fully understand and rehearse the CASEVAC
plan before operations begin. Briefings, walkthroughs, and field rehearsals help Soldiers learn their roles,
routes, and responsibilities in the evacuation process. These preparations give leaders a chance to fix
problems, confirm coordination, and adjust routes or plans to fit changing battlefield conditions. By
prioritizing readiness, training, and communication, division and brigade leaders reinforce CASEVAC as a
supporting task at echelon, one that protects Soldiers, preserves combat power, and supports the overall
mission.
**Deconfliction**
4-54. Deconfliction is a critical task for division and brigade staffs when planning and executing CASEVAC.
It prevents units from using the same routes or evacuation platforms at the same time, which can create traffic
congestion, resource shortages, or delays. Staffs coordinate CASEVAC timing, routing, and airspace use
through movement tables, such as the Road Movement Table in Exhibit 3 to Appendix 1 (Logistics) of Annex
F (Sustainment), and the Air Movement Table, when applicable. These tools help align CASEVAC with
maneuver operations, minimize disruption, and protect evacuation personnel. By synchronizing CASEVAC
with the overall scheme of maneuver, commanders uphold the principles of disciplined operations and unity
of effort described in FM 3-0. This planning creates a more responsive and survivable system for moving
casualties in dynamic environments.
4-55. Commanders strengthen CASEVAC deconfliction by integrating battle tracking into their C2 systems.
Shared communication networks, map overlays, and real-time tracking tools help monitor casualty movement
and avoid route conflicts. Leaders allocate key resources, such as radio frequencies for the evacuation
operations, to maintain clear communications across units. Staffs coordinate CASEVAC planning through
established forums like the operations synchronization meeting and the sustainment synchronization meeting.
These meetings provide opportunities to identify risks, adjust routes, and align priorities across warfighting
functions. Training exercises that simulate contested CASEVAC operations build familiarity with these tools
and processes. When units rehearse communications, rerouting, and decision-making under stress, they create
a more resilient and responsive CASEVAC network that supports mission success.
4-56. The following vignette, "Deconflicting Casualty Evacuation: A Tale of Two Units," is a fictional
scenario based on real-world events. It shows how commanders and units can achieve effective deconfliction
and coordination during intense combat operations. The example highlights the importance of preventing
friendly fire, avoiding evacuation delays, and making sure medical support reaches those who need it most.
Commanders apply these principles by managing CASEVAC operations with deliberate timing, route
planning, and mission prioritization. By studying this scenario, leaders can better understand how to apply
deconfliction techniques to improve the success of their own CASEVAC efforts.
**62**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**

### Deconflicting Casualty Evacuation: A Tale of Two Units

During the Battle of Ironclad Alley, two battalions were operating in the same area. 1st
Battalion was clearing the north side of the district, while 2nd Battalion was clearing
the south side. As 1st Battalion pushed deeper into the district, they came under heavy
fire from enemy forces. Several Service members were wounded, and their medical
team requested help to evacuate the WII personnel.
Since the fighting was so intense, the usual medical helicopters couldn't reach the
area. The unit had to use a nonmedical vehicle, a high mobility multipurpose wheeled
vehicle (HMMWV), to transport the wounded Service members to safety. Meanwhile,
2nd Battalion was also taking casualties on the south side of the district. They too
needed to evacuate their wounded Service members, and they used armored
personnel carriers to do so. However, as these vehicles moved through the area, there
was a risk of confusion and friendly fire, where one unit might accidentally attack the
other.
To prevent delays and confusion, operations officers from both units coordinated with
one another. They agreed on a specific evacuation route, marked with checkpoints, to
guide vehicles safely through the area. Medical teams from both units shared details
about the casualties, including their locations, medical conditions, and evacuation
priority. This coordination allowed both units to allocate their resources and prioritize
casualty movement based on urgency.
As nonmedical vehicles from both units traveled along the planned route, they used
the checkpoints to maintain order and avoid overlap. Their shared understanding
helped prevent delays, miscommunication, and potential fratricide. Because of this
coordination, the units successfully transported wounded Soldiers to a safe location,
where medical teams could provide further treatment.
This event showed how communication and planning between units during CASEVAC
operations reduce risk and improve outcomes, even in combat. By integrating efforts
at the tactical level, these units demonstrated how CASEVAC functions as a supporting
task that helps maintain momentum, preserve combat power, and save lives. This
example reinforces the need for deliberate coordination in future operations, especially
during large-scale combat operations.
4-57. Units operating in the same area take an active role in coordinating CASEVAC operations. They do
this by establishing clear communication channels and deconflicting their operational boundaries.
Predetermined routes and checkpoints help guide nonmedical vehicles safely through the area, reducing the
risk of confusion or friendly fire. Medical teams share real-time updates on casualty locations, conditions,
and evacuation priorities. This shared awareness helps both units allocate resources properly and avoid delays
in treatment. By making coordination and deconfliction a priority, units improve the safety and speed of
CASEVAC missions, even during heavy fighting. Clear planning and open communication support the
overall mission by keeping CASEVAC aligned with tactical movements and preserving combat power.
**Liaison Officers**
4-58. LNOs at the division and brigade levels play a key role in coordinating CASEVAC operations across
units and with allied partners. They serve as the main points of contact for sharing CASEVAC plans, updates,
and changes as the situation develops. LNOs help make sure that all involved units understand the CASEVAC
plan and carry it out correctly, even as combat conditions shift. By working closely with adjacent units and
partner forces, LNOs improve the speed, flexibility, and safety of CASEVAC. Their efforts keep CASEVAC
operations connected to current tactical needs and help translate higher-level planning into action on the
ground.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**63**
**Chapter 4**
4-59. At the brigade level, LNOs often work more closely with subordinate units, providing real-time updates
and ensuring that CASEVAC plans adapt to rapidly changing battlefield conditions. Their role includes
identifying and resolving potential conflicts, such as competing resource demands or route overlap, and
ensuring that all involved parties remain informed. By maintaining strong relationships with unit leaders and
fostering clear, continuous communication, brigade-level LNOs contribute to the effective execution of
CASEVAC operations, ensuring casualties are evacuated safely and efficiently while supporting the broader
operational goals of the brigade.
