division area of operations.

## CASUALTY REPORTING ACCOUNTABILITY

4-61. Units report and manage accountability of casualties during operations. The classification of a casualty
determines whether the unit follows a single or a dual reporting process. The two processes are casualty
reporting via Department of the Army (DA) Form 1156 (Casualty Feeder Card) and treatment reporting via
Department of Defense Form (DD) 1380 (Tactical Combat Casualty Care [TCCC] card).
4-62. Casualty accountability is a command responsibility. Leaders account for all WII personnel throughout
operations, especially during large-scale combat where casualty numbers may overwhelm reporting systems.
Commanders at every echelon build accountability into planning, rehearsals, and execution, not as an
administrative task but as a mission-critical function that affects force readiness and medical support. Units
track casualties from the POI until they are admitted to a Role 3 medical treatment facility for inpatient care.
4-63. Effective casualty reporting supports accurate personnel tracking, timely medical response, and
operational decision-making. Commanders work with both personnel and medical staffs to maintain shared
awareness of each casualty’s status. While the DD Form 1380 supports continuity of medical treatment, units
also use DA Form 1156 to initiate administrative reporting. These two efforts are complementary and should
be coordinated at every handoff.
4-64. In joint and multinational environments, casualty reporting may extend beyond U.S. Soldiers to include
civilians, contractors, detainees, or unified action partners. While formal reporting responsibilities vary by
category, commanders remain responsible for the accountability of all personnel under their control. Even
when automated systems are unavailable or overwhelmed, units rely on clear SOPs and disciplined reporting
chains to prevent loss of information.
4-65. Large-scale combat operations increase the risk of delayed reporting, duplicated efforts, or missed
updates. To avoid this, commanders designate staff sections to manage casualty tracking during both routine
and MASCAL events. Personnel staff sections consolidate casualty data, while medical elements track
treatment and evacuation. These efforts remain linked throughout the operation. Accurate, timely updates
protect the force and allow commanders to adjust task organization, request replacements, or shift resources
as needed.
**64**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
4-66. The Transportation Command Regulating and Command & Control Evacuation System (known as
TRAC2ES) supports patient tracking in theater-regulated movement, no universal tool currently exists for
tactical-level casualty tracking during CASEVAC. Until casualties reach formal medical channels, units often
rely on radio reports, battle tracking tools, or written logs. This gap reinforces the need for disciplined
reporting practices at all echelons and close coordination between CASEVAC teams, medical personnel, and
unit headquarters. In large-scale combat operations, where the scale and tempo of operations can rapidly
degrade communications, accountability remains a priority from the first report to final disposition.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION METHODS AND TACTICAL

## EXECUTION

4-67. CASEVAC is used to keep casualties moving when MEDEVAC is unavailable, delayed, or not
practical. This evacuation support is planned and rehearsed across all operational environments to stay
flexible and responsive. Soldiers should be trained in multiple CASEVAC techniques, including manual
carries, litter movement, and platform-based transport. These methods help preserve combat power and
maintain momentum, even in denied or degraded conditions. ATP 4-02.13 outlines the TTP that guide these
efforts.

### MANUALEVACUATIONMETHODS

4-68. Manual evacuation becomes essential when terrain, enemy activity, or logistics prevent the use of
vehicles. Soldiers conduct drags or one- and two-person carries to remove casualties from danger. Drags are
used under fire and cover short distances rapidly. Carries are used when the threat has diminished, and the
casualty must be transported farther.
4-69. The selection of a manual evacuation technique depends on several operational factors: the casualty’s
weight relative to the rescuer’s strength, the urgency and speed of movement, the distance to be covered, and
the casualty’s injuries, consciousness level, and the security situation. Soldiers evaluate these factors to
minimize further injury and maintain mission tempo.
4-70. Soldiers may be required to perform manual evacuation in complex terrain, including dense forests,
mountains, urban environments, tunnels, or chemically contaminated areas. These conditions increase risk
and may prevent vehicle access. When evacuating in a contaminated environment, units develop plans to
reduce gross contamination before movement to protect both rescuers and casualties. Leaders may also rotate
teams, establish relay points, or delay movement until conditions improve.
4-71. Proficiency in manual evacuation techniques requires regular, realistic training. ATP 4-02.13 details
the carries, drags, and associated TTPs. Training improves Soldier confidence and readiness to act under fire
or in austere conditions.
4-72. These skills support the broader CASEVAC mission by ensuring no casualty is left behind. Effective
manual evacuation strengthens unit cohesion, preserves combat strength, and enables rapid casualty removal
when other assets are not available.

### LITTEREVACUATION

4-73. Litter evacuation provides stable casualty movement over extended distances, reducing further injury
and maintaining safe transport. Terrain, weather, and enemy actions affect litter team effectiveness. Urban
terrain may include stairwells and rubble, while jungles or mountains increase physical demands. In these
environments, relay points or alternative methods, such as rope-assisted extractions, may be necessary.
4-74. Combat conditions complicate litter movement. Indirect fire, CBRN threats, and limited visibility
demand cover and concealment, bounding overwatch, and contingency planning. Well-trained and properly
equipped litter teams help mitigate these risks and maintain effectiveness.
**Types of Litters and Their Applications**
4-75. Commanders understand the different types of litters to match the right equipment to the mission and
environment. Each type has specific strengths based on terrain, threat, and mobility needs:
•
Rigid Folding Litters (Pole Litters) are the standard for most missions, offering stable transport.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**65**
**Chapter 4**
•
Soft Litters offer flexibility in confined spaces.
•
Rescue and Transport System Patient Basic High Density Sheet Systems support high-angle, water,
or restrictive terrain extractions.
•
Stokes Baskets are used for hoist, maritime, or vertical CASEVAC.
•
Improvised Litters may be built from materials on hand when no standard options are available.
4-76. Commanders train and prepare litter teams to meet the physical and technical demands of CASEVAC.
These teams carry load-bearing gear designed for rough terrain and long-distance movement. Training
includes proper casualty handling techniques to reduce the risk of additional injury during transport. Leaders
also incorporate strength and endurance conditioning into regular readiness activities, helping teams move
casualties across long distances or through difficult environments. By developing these skills ahead of time,
commanders increase the unit’s ability to carry out CASEVAC missions in combat conditions.
**Training and Operational Considerations for Litter Evacuation**
4-77. CASEVAC teams operate in complex settings under pressure. ATP 4-02.13 provides detailed guidance
on:
•
Litter movement under fire.
•
CCP setup.
•
Use of relay methods for long routes.
•
Knot tying and techniques for negotiating rough terrain such as steep slopes or in rubble.
4-78. Litter CASEVAC enables timely casualty transport while reducing exposure to hostile fire and
environmental hazards. With proper training and the right tools, using a litter also reduces the risk of further
injury to the casualty by providing stability and controlled movement. Well-trained units can sustain
litter-based CASEVAC operations even in combat conditions.

### CASUALTYEVACUATIONPLATFORMS

4-79. ATP 4-02.13 outlines the use of ground vehicles, aircraft, watercraft, and alternative platforms for
CASEVAC. These platforms allow rapid, safe casualty movement when operational conditions permit.
Commanders select platforms that match the environment, mission urgency, and threat level.
**Ground Vehicles for Casualty Evacuation**
4-80. Ground vehicles are the primary CASEVAC method in mounted operations, providing protected
mobility to Role 1 or Role 2 MTFs. See table C-2 on page 109 platform types and capacities.
4-81. Terrain, poor roads, and enemy threats, including indirect fire, explosive hazards, and drone attacks,
can hinder ground mobility. Current conflict trends, such as in Ukraine, show the increased use of first-person
view drones to target vehicles. Commanders evaluate terrain, security, and threat conditions before
committing ground CASEVAC assets.
**Aerial Vehicles for Casualty Evacuation**
4-82. Rotary-wing and fixed-wing platforms provide rapid casualty movement, especially in areas that are
hard to reach or under threat. Rotary-wing assets, such as UH-60 Black Hawks, allow for extraction close to
the POI.
4-83. However, enemy air defenses, limited LZs, and poor weather can limit their use. Aerial CASEVAC is
planned as part of the larger operations, with attention to LZ security, threat suppression, and viable routes.
See table C-1 on page 105 for aircraft types and capacities.
**Watercraft for Casualty Evacuation**
4-84. In amphibious or riverine operations, watercraft support CASEVAC along inland or coastal waterways.
They are useful in joint operations or when medical support is available from naval forces.
**66**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
4-85. However, tides, currents, and enemy interdiction can delay evacuation. Planning should include
CASEVAC routes, embarkation points, and medical integration. See table C-3 on page 114 for watercraft
options.

### ALTERNATIVECASUALTYEVACUATIONMETHODS

4-86. When standard platforms are not feasible, alternative CASEVAC methods provide flexible solutions
in challenging environments. These methods include litter carries and the use of pack animals, which are
particularly effective in jungle, subterranean, or rubble-filled terrain.
4-87. These options require high endurance and coordination. Manual CASEVAC is labor-intensive, and
pack animals require support. Commanders evaluate risk, effectiveness, and resource availability when
employing these methods.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION IN MASS CASUALTY SITUATIONS

4-88. During a MASCAL event, the ability to rapidly evacuate casualties is crucial to saving lives and
maintaining morale. Appendix B outlines how to manage MASCAL situations by improving CASEVAC
efforts. Units do this by using nonmedical platforms for transport, setting up CCPs, and planning evacuation
routes. These actions help move casualties to MTFs while using medical resources and personnel wisely.
Success during CASEVAC in a MASCAL event depends on detailed planning, effective triage, and a
coordinated response. This includes using host-nation support and rehearsing procedures ahead of time to
stay prepared. When leaders understand the complexity of CASEVAC, they can build solid strategies to
protect life, limb, and eyesight, even in the most difficult operational conditions.
4-89. Effectively managing CASEVAC in MASCAL situations is vital for supporting the maneuver
commander and maintaining the continuity of operations of military units. Appendix D provides detailed
guidance on key tasks such as triage, prioritization, and logistical planning, each critical when dealing with
large numbers of casualties. When commanders follow clear procedures for assessing injuries and setting
priorities, they direct resources to the areas of greatest need. Triage speeds up care, supports quick decisions,
and allows units to keep fighting even in high-pressure situations. Proper execution of CASEVAC during a
MASCAL event helps units stay in the fight, preserve combat power, and keep the mission on track.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION OPERATIONS

4-90. CASEVAC provides a flexible way to move WII personnel when MEDEVAC assets are unavailable,
overtasked, or unable to reach the POI. Rather than offering a one-size-fits-all solution, CASEVAC is adapted
to the terrain, mission, and available resources. This section discusses common considerations such as
platform type, casualty condition, security needs, and coordination challenges. Leaders may use local
checklists, templates, or SOPs to support CASEVAC planning, but they should treat these tools as aids, not
fixed requirements. Commanders retain the flexibility to adjust their approach to fit the operational
environment while preserving combat power and keeping the mission on track.

### CASUALTYEVACUATIONCAPABILITIES ANDLEVELS OFSUPPORT

4-91. Commanders use CASEVAC to maintain casualty movement when MEDEVAC platforms are
unavailable, delayed, or focused on critical patients. By using options like utility vehicles or rotary-wing
aircraft with security escorts, they can keep operations moving and reduce delays in care. CASEVAC
provides flexibility under combat conditions, but its success depends on knowing the strengths and limits of
each platform. Appendix C describes these options to help leaders plan and make informed decisions during
operations.
4-92. While CASEVAC gives commanders a fast and flexible way to evacuate casualties, it comes with clear
limitations. Unlike MEDEVAC platforms, CASEVAC usually lacks dedicated medical equipment and
trained medical personnel, which can increase the risk to patients during transport. Still, commanders can
improve CASEVAC outcomes by using available resources, planning, and training their teams. As discussed
in Appendix C, important planning steps include setting up evacuation routes, assigning the right assets, and
building a sustainment plan that supports the use of non-standard platforms. By focusing on training and
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**67**
**Chapter 4**
integrating different types of transport, commanders can keep casualty care moving, maintain the pace of
operations, and improve the overall success of CASEVAC missions.
**Planned and Unplanned Casualty Evacuation**
4-93. CASEVAC operations are categorized as either planned or unplanned, depending on the level of
preparation and available resources. Planned CASEVAC occurs when commanders pre-allocate designated
platforms, trained crews, and medical resources in advance. This proactive approach minimizes delays,
ensuring that WII personnel receive timely medical care while maintaining unit operational tempo. By
positioning CASEVAC resources in anticipation of potential casualties, commanders can create a streamlined
process that improves efficiency and reduces response time.
4-94. Unplanned CASEVAC happens when commanders respond to a casualty situation using whatever
resources are immediately on hand. This type of evacuation supports rapid extraction during urgent events
but comes with serious risks, such as limited medical care and higher exposure to enemy threats. To reduce
these risks, commanders train their personnel to react with flexibility and confidence. Units learn to weigh
the need for speed against the requirements for security and casualty care, making fast decisions that protect
both the force and the wounded.
**Levels of Casualty Evacuation Support**
4-95. Commanders employ three levels of CASEVAC support to meet the demands of different tactical
situations:
•
Dedicated CASEVAC Assets: Platforms specifically assigned and equipped for CASEVAC. These
assets are prioritized for CASEVAC missions, ensuring that they are readily available and medically
capable of transporting casualties. Dedicated CASEVAC is essential in high-casualty scenarios
where consistent evacuation support is required.
•
Designated CASEVAC Assets: Dual-role platforms that perform other operational tasks but can be
reallocated for CASEVAC when needed. These assets offer flexibility by allowing commanders to
adapt to changing operational demands. However, their availability for CASEVAC is contingent
upon mission priorities.
•
Platforms of Opportunity: Ad hoc evacuation solutions using any available platform to evacuate
casualties in urgent, high-risk situations when no other options exist. These platforms lack medical
personnel and equipment, making them less suitable for prolonged transport but crucial in
emergencies.
4-96. Each level of CASEVAC support provides unique advantages, enabling commanders to align resources
with the operational environment. The use of pre-planned CASEVAC operations or opportunistic extractions
helps commanders prioritize personnel safety, maintain unit effectiveness, and preserve combat power. By
understanding and applying these three levels of CASEVAC support, commanders can make informed
decisions that balance mission requirements with casualty care.

### CASUALTYEVACUATIONCHECKLIST

4-97. A CASEVAC checklist, such as the one in Appendix D, can help commanders stay organized during
fast-moving or high-pressure operations. When used as a planning aid or rehearsal tool, the checklist helps
leaders think through key actions and considerations, such as platform availability, coordination with medical
teams, route security, and communication procedures. It supports decision-making without replacing tactical
judgment or mission-specific adjustments.
4-98. The checklist is not a mandatory format but an example that units may adapt based on the mission,
echelon, and environment. It can support training, help units build effective SOPs, and improve
responsiveness during real-world operations. When applied appropriately, it helps commanders manage risk,
coordinate CASEVAC efforts, and reinforce trust in the unit’s medical response system which are critical for
preserving combat power and maintaining operational momentum.

## COMMUNICATION AND COORDINATION

4-99. Commanders know that strong communication and coordination are critical to evacuating WII Soldiers.
To support successful CASEVAC operations, they apply standardized procedures, guide accurate execution,
and monitor performance throughout the process. By reviewing each part of the evacuation, leaders better
understand its complexity and the central role coordination plays in preserving life. Teamwork, discipline,
and attention to detail help units achieve the best possible outcomes for WII personnel in fast-moving
operational environments.
4-100. When a Soldier becomes WII, commanders act quickly because every minute matters. To provide
timely and effective care, they build reliable communication between the medical team, evacuation team, and
command center. These teams work side by side, sharing vital information and making rapid decisions that
improve survival chances and support the overall mission.

### MOVEMENTSYNCHRONIZATION

4-101. Effective movement control is critical to successful joint operations, synchronizing transportation
assets with both tactical maneuvers and sustainment requirements. Movement Control Teams and Battalions
are the primary organizations responsible for planning, managing, and coordinating transportation, resolving
conflicts for asset utilization, and ensuring timely delivery of essential supplies, personnel, and equipment.
This capability directly supports the commander’s operational objectives. For additional information on
movement control, refer to ATP 4-16.
**Deconfliction and Resource Prioritization in Movement Control**
4-102. Most CASEVAC missions occur below the brigade level and are managed by tactical units using
internal coordination. However, during deliberate operations, such as clearing a Role 2 MTF or evacuating
casualties during a large-scale offensive, movement control elements may play a critical role. These units
help synchronize high-priority transportation tasks, using tools like movement request systems and road
movement tables to reduce congestion and route conflicts. They track asset use across multiple units, identify
overlapping plans, and support the commander’s scheme of maneuver by keeping CASEVAC and other
time-sensitive missions on schedule.
4-103. In such operations, movement control units work closely with sustainment planners and maneuver
staffs to prioritize limited resources. CASEVAC may take precedence over routine resupply or retrograde
tasks based on the urgency of medical need and its impact on combat power. These units help classify
transportation requests by operational importance, redirect assets when threats disrupt routes, and secure
alternative pathways when needed. By supporting disciplined movement across the area of operations,
movement control elements contribute to coordinated CASEVAC execution and reinforce the broader
sustainment and maneuver plan.
**Cross-Border Movement Coordination in Joint Operations**
4-104. Cross-border coordination for CASEVAC is primarily a theater-level responsibility, led by the
combatant command or the designated joint force headquarters. These higher echelons establish formal
agreements, permissions, and routing procedures with host-nations to support multinational operations.
While lower echelons may execute CASEVAC missions near borders, they rely on policies and agreements
developed during theater setting and Phase 0 activities. Early coordination with allied partners and
host-nations, especially through regular medical exercises, helps expose gaps and build interoperability
before actual operations begin.
4-105. Although cross-border coordination usually refers to movement between countries, similar
coordination challenges can occur within a theater between different components or echelons. For example,
patient handoffs from a land component to an air component or between corps and division-level commands
may require deliberate deconfliction. In both cases, success depends on shared policies, pre-designated
corridors, and coordinated command relationships.
4-106. Combatant command and joint force headquarters level staffs work with movement control elements,
medical planners, and operational headquarters to establish CASEVAC pathways across national or
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**69**
**Chapter 4**
component boundaries. This includes securing crossing procedures, overflight permissions, and agreements
on patient eligibility at multinational treatment facilities. These agreements should define documentation
standards, care responsibilities, and transfer authorities. Sustainment commands and medical brigades may
contribute to coordinating support zones and regulating medical movement between echelons.
4-107. Even when formal agreements are in place, unexpected events, such as sudden diplomatic tensions,
denied airspace access, or damaged routes, can block planned CASEVAC efforts. Higher echelons develop
and maintain alternate evacuation routes and redundant permissions to keep operations moving. J-4, J-5, and
J-7 staff elements coordinate with the Department of State or embassy representatives to manage diplomatic
clearances and facilitate emergency approvals during crises.
4-108. Real-time situational awareness is critical to prevent delays in cross-border CASEVAC. Joint and
multinational staffs use LNOs, shared communication networks, and C2 platforms to monitor threats,
changes in diplomatic posture, or environmental hazards. Commanders improve CASEVAC reliability by:
•
Holding pre-mission coordination meetings with relevant stakeholders.
•
Preparing customs and transit documentation in advance.
•
Synchronizing movement plans across national and component timelines.
4-109. LNOs assigned to allied and host-nation units serve as critical links for cross-border CASEVAC. They
provide direct communication to clarify national procedures, interpret policy in real time, and prevent
misunderstandings that could delay medical movement. Their integration strengthens multinational
command cohesion and improves responsiveness during evacuation missions.
4-110. Training units on cross-border CASEVAC goes beyond small-unit training. Regular multinational
rehearsals, including live evacuation drills and simulated border clearances, build readiness and help refine
coordination practices. These exercises test agreements, surface logistical or legal friction points, and
promote shared understanding between partners. When higher echelons conduct deliberate planning,
maintain updated agreements, and test them through rehearsal, they set the conditions for successful
CASEVAC across national and organizational boundaries.

### COMMUNICATION ANDCOORDINATION INCASUALTYEVACUATIONOPERATIONS

4-111. Commanders understand that clear and consistent communication is critical during CASEVAC
operations, where every minute can impact a wounded Soldier’s survival. To support fast and accurate
coordination, they apply established communication procedures, such as the 9-line MEDEVAC request and
standard reporting formats. These tools help medical teams, evacuation platforms, and command elements
share a common operational picture and respond to each evacuation request.
4-112. Effective communication enables teams to rapidly exchange critical information, including the
casualty’s location, injury severity, and available evacuation assets. This coordination allows the command
center to validate evacuation requests, medical teams to prepare for patient arrival, and evacuation units to
adjust routes based on real-time battlefield conditions. By enforcing standardized communication practices,
commanders reduce confusion, minimize response times, and enhance patient outcomes in both routine and
high-threat evacuation scenarios.
4-113. Clear communication is critical to preventing misunderstandings and miscommunications that can
lead to delays or mistakes in CASEVAC operations. For example, if the medical team is not informed of the
wounded Soldier's condition or the evacuation team is not aware of the safest route to take, it can result in
causing further harm to the casualty, delaying or jeopardizing the evacuation. In contrast, clear and effective
communication enables teams to work together seamlessly, ensuring that WII Soldiers receive the best
possible care. Effective communication also reduces stress and anxiety among teams, allowing them to focus
on their tasks and perform at their best.
4-114. Commanders coordinate the efforts of the medical team, evacuation team, and command center to
achieve a common goal: the safe and timely evacuation of WII Soldiers. This requires planning, precise
execution, and continuous monitoring of the evacuation effort. The command center plays a critical role in
coordinating the evacuation effort, tracking the location and status of the WII Soldier, as well as the progress
of the evacuation team, making informed decisions and providing guidance to the teams. Effective
coordination also involves the use of standardized procedures and protocols, ensuring that all teams work
toward the same objective, and use the same language and terminology.
**70**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
4-115. Commanders highlight the need for clear and standardized communication during CASEVAC
operations to help units adapt to changing battlefield conditions. Strong communication reduces the risk of
delays, mistakes, or mission failure that could cost lives. In large-scale combat operations, CASEVAC teams
respond to shifting threats, damaged infrastructure, or blocked evacuation routes. When a route becomes
unusable, whether due to enemy action, indirect fire, or sudden obstacles, standardized communication helps
teams reroute casualties, coordinate with supporting units, and continue providing care. By enforcing clear
reporting and communication protocols, commanders keep evacuation teams, medical facilities, and
operational elements aligned, allowing for fast decisions and steady CASEVAC operations even in chaotic
environments.
4-116. Commanders recognize that clear communication and coordination in CASEVAC operations are
essential to maintaining operational efficiency and ensuring quality care. CASEVAC missions prioritize the
rapid evacuation of casualties, but the process is highly stressful for both the WII Soldier and their unit.
Maintaining effective reporting and coordination throughout the mission helps reduce confusion, alleviate
stress, and support timely decision-making. By prioritizing accurate, professional communication across
command channels, CASEVAC teams enhance both mission effectiveness and the overall level of care
provided.

### EVACUATIONREQUEST

4-117. The 9-line MEDEVAC request includes nine standardized pieces of information, such as the pickup
location, patient priority, and type of injury or illness. Although commonly used for MEDEVAC, the 9-line
also plays a vital role in CASEVAC operations, especially when MEDEVAC assets are not available. In
these cases, nonmedical units conducting CASEVAC still send accurate and timely MEDEVAC request to
support proper coordination and resource use. CASEVAC platforms may lack en route medical care,
commanders evaluate patient conditions carefully to choose the safest transport method. Units that regularly
train with the MEDEVAC request format improve their response speed and reduce communication mistakes,
helping casualties reach medical care faster.
4-118. Commanders understand that different organizations, such as American, British, Canadian,
Australian, and New Zealand (ABCANZ) forces, as well as NATO, use their own evacuation request formats.
Supporting multinational operations requires familiarity with these differences. The U.S. 9-line MEDEVAC
request format focuses on sending evacuation details quickly, while NATO’s version includes extra items
like escort needs and threat levels. During joint operations, CASEVAC requests may need to match coalition
formats to support clear communication and fast patient movement. Units working with coalition partners
should train on multiple formats to coordinate across command structures. Appendix E provides a comparison
of U.S. and coalition MEDEVAC request formats.
4-119. Using the U.S. military’s 9-line MEDEVAC request format enhances unit communication and
CASEVAC efficiency. Standardization is especially critical in high-pressure environments where every
minute counts. CASEVAC operations integrate the MEDEVAC request while accounting for variations in
platform capabilities, medical support availability, and threat conditions. By ensuring that the format is
clearly communicated and embedded in unit training and operational planning, commanders reduce
confusion, improve coordination, and ultimately save lives.

## AUTHORITY AND RISK IN CASUALTY EVACUATION

4-120. In large-scale combat operations, CASEVAC requires more than medical proficiency, it depends on
clearly defined and coordinated authority across all levels of warfare. Commanders understand how medical
mission authority and launch authority operate independently yet in concert to ensure responsive,
risk-informed evacuation. These roles are established before combat begins and aligned across echelons from
national strategic to tactical levels. When treated as a supporting task at echelon, CASEVAC preserves
combat power, improves survivability, and enables mission success under fire.

### MEDICALMISSIONAUTHORITY ANDLAUNCHAUTHORITY INEVACUATIONOPERATIONS

4-121. Medical mission authority and launch authority are both required to conduct evacuation missions.
These authorities apply to MEDEVAC and CASEVAC alike, and commanders understand their differences
and how they work together. Medical mission authority confirms that the casualty qualifies for evacuation.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**71**
**Chapter 4**
Launch authority allows the mission to proceed based on the current operational risk. When these approvals
are not synchronized, delays, gaps in communication, or operational failure may occur.
4-122. Medical mission authority confirms that the evacuation request meets the unit’s standing policies,
including medical eligibility, the theater evacuation policy, and commander’s intent. It is typically delegated
to the lowest level practical, such as a Role 1 or Role 2 medical officer, so that decisions can be made during
operations. Higher approval is needed only when the request involves unique cases, such as civilians or
detainees. Medical mission authority does not determine the casualty’s destination. That decision, called
medical regulating, occurs later based on patient condition and treatment availability.
4-123. Launch authority belongs to the commander who owns the movement platform. For air missions, this
is the aviation commander. For ground CASEVAC, launch authority may rest with the supported maneuver
commander or a sustainment unit commander who owns the platform. This commander evaluates operational
risk, such as enemy contact, degraded routes, weather, or platform readiness, before approving the mission.
Even when a casualty qualifies medically, launch authority may delay or cancel the mission based on tactical
conditions.
4-124. These authorities are exercised in parallel. Medical teams identify valid patients and recommend
evacuation, while supported commanders decide when and how the mission will move based on the fight.
This coordination allows leaders to balance medical urgency against operational risk. Delegating medical
mission authority helps accelerate decisions at the POI, while maintaining launch authority at the command
level protects the force and supports the broader operation.
4-125. Commanders understand CASEVAC’s limitations. CASEVAC platforms may not include medical
personnel or stabilization equipment. This increases risk to casualties, particularly in prolonged or high-threat
movements. CASEVAC should not be treated as a lesser option but as a supporting task that requires
deliberate planning, appropriate launch authority, and operational protection. Commanders plan for these
missions just as they would for MEDEVAC, including route security, casualty tracking, and prioritization
based on the tactical situation.

### DOCTRINALDISCUSSION: AUTHORITYRELATIONSHIPS IN THEBATTLE OFMOGADISHU

4-126. Confusion between medical mission authority and launch authority has led to historical failures.
During the Battle of Mogadishu in 1993, delayed medical evacuation, lack of launch authority clarity, and
breakdowns in risk acceptance led to prolonged exposure of wounded personnel. When helicopters could not
safely launch and CASEVAC platforms were not directed to move forward, Rangers and special operations
teams were forced to carry their wounded by foot under fire. This event illustrates how the absence of
integrated CASEVAC and clear, synchronized authorities can result in mission breakdown, increased
casualties, and tactical defeat.
4-127. The 1993 Battle of Mogadishu shows that failure to deliberately plan and synchronize CASEVAC
and authorities across all levels of warfare, national-strategic, theater-strategic, operational, and tactical, can
lead to mission breakdown. Though the operation was tactically focused, failure to align medical mission
authority and launch authority across echelons contributed to degraded responsiveness, delayed evacuation,
and avoidable risk to combat forces. This historical case offers insight into the importance of integrating
CASEVAC as a supporting task rather than treating it as an afterthought.
4-128. At the national-strategic level, military forces operated under a mandate shaped by shifting political
priorities and ambiguous guidance. U.S. national leadership authorized the deployment of a special
operations task force without fully integrating it into the broader multinational command structure. As a
result, the mission lacked unity of command, and supporting enablers, such as armored vehicles or robust
MEDEVAC platforms, were not resourced or approved. This misalignment weakened the operational
planning framework and affected CASEVAC responsiveness before the mission began.
4-129. At the theater-strategic level, command authorities did not clearly designate how Task Force Ranger,
a joint special operations task force comprised of ARSOF, U.S. Air Force Special Tactics units, and other
supporting elements, would integrate with existing theater CASEVAC infrastructure. The lack of pre-
coordinated procedures for link-up, mutual support, or ground route clearance created delays when urgent
evacuation was needed. Medical mission authority remained with the Joint Special Operations Command
(JSOC), while launch authority for air assets resided with the 160th Special Operations Aviation Regiment
**72**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
(SOAR). This split authority worked under ideal conditions but was strained by rapid tactical changes and
limited interoperability with conventional forces.
4-130. At the operational level, CASEVAC was not prioritized as a critical component of mission planning.
While JSOC approved evacuation requests based on medical necessity (medical mission authority), the 160th
SOAR retained launch authority and was responsible for evaluating threats, route security, and airframe
availability. In the absence of effective pre-mission coordination with maneuver and support elements, such
as the Quick Reaction Force, CASEVAC became reactive. The supporting ground convoys were not prepared
for sustained engagement, and medical assets lacked the flexibility to respond when helicopter extractions
became impossible.
4-131. At the tactical level, Soldiers fought to secure crash sites while coordinating air and ground evacuation
under fire. The initial helicopter evacuations removed only two casualties. Ground-based CASEVAC
operations were limited by heavy fire, urban terrain, and poor situational awareness. Despite the bravery of
small-unit leaders and aircrews, the lack of scalable CASEVAC options forced prolonged exposure and
delayed treatment. Evacuation remained a supporting task, but in this case, it required real-time adjustments
across command levels that had not been anticipated or rehearsed.

### Authority in action: A Mogadishu Casualty Evacuation

On October 3, 1993, during Operation Gothic Serpent in Mogadishu, Somalia, U.S.
forces conducted a daylight raid in a dense urban environment to capture enemy
personnel. Two U.S. Army Black Hawk helicopters were shot down, and the mission
shifted from raid execution to rescue and recovery. This shift tested how well medical
mission authority and launch authority had been coordinated before the operation.
Medical mission authority remained with Joint Special Operations Command (JSOC),
which assessed each casualty against MEDEVAC criteria. JSOC prioritized CASEVAC
based on combat injuries, asset availability, and mission urgency. However, JSOC did
not have direct control of launch authority for air assets. That authority rested with the
160th Special Operations Aviation Regiment (SOAR), whose aviation commanders
had to weigh weather, enemy threats, platform survivability, and airspace conditions
before approving any launch.
The 160th SOAR launched MH-6 helicopters for rapid CASEVAC, but these lacked
medical personnel or equipment. As enemy fire intensified, conditions degraded. Only
two casualties were evacuated by air in the initial stages. Additional helicopter
evacuations became too risky. Ground convoys, including the Quick Reaction Force,
attempted CASEVAC but encountered roadblocks, ambushes, and delayed routing
due to limited planning and coordination. Urban combat and limited platform
redundancy halted further extractions until the following morning.
The CASEVAC effort in Mogadishu demonstrates three key lessons:
•
Pre-coordination between medical mission authority and launch authority is
essential before operations begin.
•
Tactical units understand who holds authority at each level and how decisions
interact under pressure.
•
CASEVAC, when treated as a supporting task at echelon, preserves combat
power, and avoids compounding mission failure.
This example reinforces that CASEVAC is not just a medical response but a
mission-critical function that requires deliberate planning, real-time communication,
and clear command relationships.
4-132. The Mogadishu case reinforces the doctrinal imperative to synchronize medical mission authority and
launch authority across echelons. Commanders recognize CASEVAC as an operational requirement that
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**73**
**Chapter 4**
affects tempo, survivability, and mission outcomes, not a medical function. When CASEVAC is deliberately
planned as a supporting task, and authority is clearly defined and coordinated, units can manage risk, maintain
combat power, and adapt to rapidly changing battlefield conditions.
4-133. This historical example highlights three critical lessons for modern commanders:
•
Pre-coordinated CASEVAC procedures prevent delays.
•
Clear distinction between the medical mission authority and the launch authority prevents
miscommunication.
•
Risk assessment in denied environments is considered before launching a mission.
4-134. By applying these principles, commanders can improve CASEVAC response times and increase the
survivability of WII personnel in complex battle conditions. Effective coordination, established evacuation
procedures, and strong situational awareness help units adjust to changing threats and operational limits.
These efforts support timely and efficient CASEVAC across the formation.

## TRAINING AND EQUIPMENT

4-135. Commanders recognize that CASEVAC plans and SOPs are effective only when reinforced through
training and regular rehearsals. They include battle drills and unit-level training events to build proficiency
in both individual and collective CASEVAC tasks. Through repeated practice, Soldiers learn how to respond
under stress. This preparation helps units carry out CASEVAC operations with precision, supports mission
success, and improves survival rates on the battlefield.
4-136. CASEVAC and casualty response depends on trained personnel at every level. All Soldiers assigned
to deployable positions are certified in TCCC Tier 1. This tier provides baseline lifesaving skills, such as
hemorrhage control, airway management, and casualty movement, and are renewed within 12 months prior
to deployment. In addition, each squad, crew, or similar-sized element must have at least one Soldier certified
in TCCC Tier 2, also known as the CLS. Tier 2 builds on Tier 1 and prepares nonmedical personnel to provide
initial treatment and support CASEVAC under fire. These mandatory certification and staffing requirements
ensure units can deliver far-forward care and maintain lethality while responding to casualties. For more
information on TCCC training standards and recertification timelines, refer to AR 350-1. For more
information on casualty response, TCCC, and first aid refer to ATP 4-02.11.
4-137. Joint and multinational training is vital for building a highly effective CASEVAC capability. Training
and exercises improve interoperability, allowing combined forces to operate smoothly during real-world
events. These exercises build proficiency in critical areas like casualty loading, route selection, and
communication, while simultaneously familiarizing personnel with the platforms and procedures of our
allies.
4-138. By regularly training together and aligning SOPs, forces gain a clear understanding of each partner’s
CASEVAC strengths and limitations. This proactive approach improves overall readiness, accelerates
response times, and cultivates the trust and confidence essential for success within combined formations.
Ultimately, these exercises translate into a more resilient and capable CASEVAC system, ready to address
complex challenges alongside our partners.

### COMPREHENSIVETRAININGSCOPE

4-139. Commanders train their personnel in CASEVAC skills that go far beyond manual and litter carries.
Training includes TCCC, linkups with other tactical elements, the use of camouflage and concealment, noise
and light discipline, responses to enemy fire, terrain navigation, tactical convoy operations, and sending a
9-line MEDEVAC request. Commanders also incorporate simulation-based training that mirrors real-world
conditions. These exercises expose teams to simulated combat, changing weather, and different terrain types.
This approach helps units build the skills needed to operate under pressure and adapt to a wide range of
battlefield situations.

### FOCUS ONSAFETY ANDEFFICIENCY

4-140. Commanders understand that rehearsing manual and litter handling techniques is essential, as outlined
in ATP 4-02.13. This training helps reduce the risk of injury to both casualties and the personnel moving
them, whether using ground vehicles, aircraft, or watercraft. Commanders also train CASEVAC platform

**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**
operators on the specific demands of their role, considering platform layout and the operational setting. In
addition, commanders stress the importance of clear communication and coordination across teams and units.
They use standard protocols and reliable equipment to help CASEVAC operations run smoothly from pickup
to handoff.

### OPERATIONALPREPAREDNESS ANDROLEDESIGNATION

4-141. Commanders assign drivers, CLS, and litter teams ahead of time to support a quick and organized
response when CASEVAC is needed. They also mark CASEVAC platforms to reflect their role and priority
while balancing the need for concealment, so these vehicles do not become easy targets. In addition,
commanders train leaders to make fast decisions, set priorities, and manage resources under pressure. This
preparation helps units respond to CASEVAC needs in complex operational environments.

### UTILIZATION OFAVIATIONASSETS

4-142. Commanders incorporate Army aviation assets into CASEVAC training to significantly enhance
Soldier proficiency with these critical platforms. When live aviation support is unavailable, commanders use
static aircraft to practice essential skills, such as establishing LZs and mastering loading and unloading
procedures.
4-143. Commanders use simulation-based training as an effective alternative to live aviation support,
allowing Soldiers to experience realistic aviation scenarios. Commanders use these simulations to practice
emergency procedures, refine communication protocols with aircrews, and manage the complexities of
coordinating CASEVAC under combat conditions. By integrating aviation-focused training, commanders
improve unit readiness to utilize aviation assets during actual operations, ensuring swift and efficient
CASEVAC.

### ENHANCINGSECURITYMEASURES ANDMEDICALINSIGHT

4-144. Commanders conduct rehearsals that focus on securing sensitive items and equipment during
CASEVAC to prevent loss or compromise. They work with medical personnel during training to improve
casualty movement procedures, stressing the need to prevent further injury and reduce physical strain on litter
teams. Commanders also incorporate casualty handling into physical fitness plans to build strength and refine
techniques. In addition, they include medical simulation training to help personnel build skills in casualty
assessment, treatment, and evacuation.

contaminated by chemical agents, where additional precautions and specialized procedures are required.
Commanders emphasize the importance of understanding work-rest cycles, as outlined in ATP 3-11.32, to

teams to adapt to diverse challenges and perform CASEVAC operations in any situation.

### EVALUATION ANDASSESSMENT

4-147. Commanders regularly evaluate and assess CASEVAC training plans to maintain their effectiveness.
Commanders use various methods, including training evaluation forms, detailed observation of training
scenarios, and gathering feedback from participants, to identify strengths and areas for improvement in the
training process.
4-148. Commanders consistently analyze and refine CASEVAC training to enhance the efficiency, safety,
and effectiveness of evacuation operations. By making this ongoing commitment to improvement,
commanders boost mission success and increase survival rates on the battlefield, ensuring that personnel are
better prepared to respond to casualties under real-world conditions.

### IMPORTANCE OFTRAINING ANDEQUIPMENT INCASUALTYEVACUATIONOPERATIONS

4-149. Training and equipment are critical to ensuring CASEVAC operations are conducted safely and
effectively. The ability to rapidly evacuate WII personnel can mean the difference between life and death,
making proper training and reliable equipment essential.
**Casualty Evacuation Training Requirements**
4-150. CASEVAC personnel receive focused training to build the skills needed for safe and efficient casualty
evacuation. This includes instruction in first aid, casualty assessment, and basic evacuation techniques.
Personnel also learn how to operate nonmedical platforms, navigate difficult terrain, and make fast decisions
under pressure.
4-151. Since CASEVAC uses nonmedical vehicles, such as trucks, High Mobility Multipurpose Wheeled
Vehicles (HMMWVs), and rotary-wing aircraft, personnel know how to load, secure, and transport casualties
safely. Leaders train their teams in patient securing methods and prepare them to adjust procedures based on
terrain, platform type, and environmental conditions common in austere environments.
**Casualty Evacuation Equipment and its Role**
4-152. Specialized medical equipment, such as stretchers, litters, and patient securing devices, helps stabilize
casualties during transport. Nonmedical platforms used for CASEVAC should also carry safety gear designed
to reduce movement and protect casualties from further injury across different operational environments.
4-153. Depending on the terrain and method of evacuation, additional nonmedical equipment may be needed.
For waterborne operations, life vests help keep casualties afloat and steady. In extreme cold environments,
additional supplies may be needed to keep the casualty warm. Extra litter straps, tie-down systems, and
improvised securing tools reduce patient movement in high-mobility platforms like helicopters, ground
vehicles, and boats. Leaders equip CASEVAC teams with the right tools to safely move casualties in a wide
range of conditions.
4-154. Strong communication is critical during CASEVAC operations. Radios and satellite phones allow
personnel to coordinate movements, share real-time casualty updates, and guide WII personnel to the right
MTF.
4-155. First aid kits, splints, and tourniquets support immediate medical care during transport. These tools
help reduce the risk of further injury or patient deterioration before reaching a medical treatment facility.
Proper stabilization during evacuation improves survival rates and leads to better outcomes for WII
personnel.
**Sustaining Readiness Through Training and Equipment Maintenance**
4-156. Regular training and equipment maintenance keep CASEVAC personnel ready to respond during
emergencies. Ongoing training in new techniques and equipment helps teams remain proficient and capable.
Routine inspections and upkeep reduce the risk of equipment failure that could put casualties at greater risk.
4-157. When personnel receive continuous training and work with reliable equipment, they can react and
perform during CASEVAC operations. Well-executed procedures improve casualty outcomes, support
operational success, and protect WII personnel in any environment.
**76**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Executing Casualty Evacuation at Echelon**

### TRAINING ANDEQUIPMENTREQUIREMENTS FORCASUALTYEVACUATIONOPERATIONS

4-158. Successful CASEVAC operations rely on specialized training and mission-specific equipment to
support the safe and efficient evacuation of WII personnel. Training and equipment requirements vary by
mission and environment but should cover first aid, medical care, evacuation techniques, platform operation,
communication, and risk management.
4-159. CASEVAC personnel train in key areas to perform in different environments. TCCC builds the skills
needed to treat and stabilize casualties during transport. Evacuation technique training teaches safe movement
using litters, hoists, and other tools. Platform operation training prepares personnel to use vehicles such as
HMMWVs and tactical trucks and to work with rotary-wing aircraft crews. All Soldiers know how to load,
secure, and unload casualties from their unit’s CASEVAC platforms.
4-160. Communication training gives personnel the skills to use radios and satellite phones and follow
standard procedures for coordinating evacuations. Risk management training helps identify and reduce
hazards during casualty movement. Navigation skills improve through hands-on practice, whether using GPS
or basic field techniques. These include reading topographic maps, estimating distances, recognizing terrain
features, maintaining direction, and operating vehicles at night. Such skills support accurate and timely
CASEVAC across all conditions.
4-161. CASEVAC teams require both medical and transport equipment to move casualties safely. Stretchers
and litters provide stability, while straps and harnesses help secure patients during movement. In
non-dedicated CASEVAC vehicles like medium tactical trucks, Soldiers often use improvised materials, such
as rope or 550 cord, to secure litters to seat backs or cargo areas. Unit SOPs should clearly define these
practices, and commanders equip their units accordingly. First aid kits should carry bandages, tourniquets,
pain management supplies, and other essentials. Communication gear, including radios and satellite phones,
connects CASEVAC teams with other units and medical treatment facilities. Navigation tools, such as maps,
compasses, and GPS devices, help teams find the fastest and safest routes.
4-162. CASEVAC equipment should match the environment and method of evacuation. Air operations
require litters that fit inside helicopters, along with hoists and proper securing gear. Waterborne operations
call for boats, flotation devices, and marine stretchers. Evacuations over rough terrain require ropes,
harnesses, and climbing systems to move casualties safely through steep or unstable ground. Using the right
equipment for each environment improves both safety and mission success.
4-163. Regular maintenance and inspections keep CASEVAC equipment ready for use and includes checking
for wear and damage and repairing or replacing faulty items. Inspections help identify problems early before
they affect performance or safety. Commanders track and manage equipment status to keep CASEVAC assets
ready for immediate use when called upon.

### SIMULATEDCASUALTYSCENARIOS TOIMPROVECASUALTYEVACUATIONOPERATIONS

4-164. Simulated casualty scenarios strengthen evacuation readiness at every level of command. From
ground teams to senior leaders at the corps level and above, these exercises help units improve how they
move and treat casualties under realistic conditions. At the theater strategic level, senior commands use
simulations to plan CASEVAC support across multiple brigades, reinforce engaged units, and integrate joint
or coalition evacuation assets. These exercises help planners evaluate how CASEVAC fits into the broader
operational plan and how to scale support for large casualty numbers. This kind of preparation builds an
evacuation system that can sustain combat operations over time.
4-165. At the operational level, simulated casualty scenarios help brigade and division staffs practice how to
synchronize CASEVAC across multiple units. These exercises give staffs the chance to assign evacuation
resources, manage coordination across unit boundaries, and build casualty distribution plans. For example, a
brigade staff may need to decide when to reinforce a battalion in contact or how to combine ground and air
evacuation into the maneuver plan. Division-level training focuses on coordinating CASEVAC across
brigades and reinforcing units with extra medical support. These exercises also help division staffs identify
when to request corps-level capabilities, like fixed-wing assets, or reassign CASEVAC resources from
reserve or neighboring units. This type of planning helps align CASEVAC with the pace of operations and
the commander’s intent.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**77**
**Chapter 4**
4-166. At the tactical level, simulated casualty scenarios give medics and CASEVAC teams the chance to
train in realistic conditions. These events focus on casualty movement, triage, en route care, and
communication under stress. Tactical teams rehearse loading and unloading casualties, navigating to and
from CCPs, and providing care in degraded environments. Training in low-visibility conditions, under fire,
or in nonstandard vehicles adds complexity and realism, helping teams become confident and capable under
pressure.
4-167. To be effective, these scenarios challenge units at all echelons. MASCAL events test how brigade and
division staffs manage casualty surges, perform triage, and allocate resources across multiple units. Trauma
and medical drills allow medics to practice rapid treatment and stabilization. Multi-unit CASEVAC
rehearsals build coordination, requiring one unit to reinforce another and testing staff-to-staff integration.
Low-visibility and nighttime operations test the effectiveness of CASEVAC in conditions where GPS,
communications, and air superiority may be limited or contested.
4-168. Including simulated casualty scenarios in training helps commanders treat CASEVAC as an essential
part of the operation, not an afterthought. Realistic, repeated practice allows leaders and staffs to build clear
plans, define command and support roles, and prepare for casualty evacuation under combat conditions.
CASEVAC rehearsals at every level, from squad to division, improve survivability, strengthen unit cohesion,
and support overall mission success.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION’S ROLE IN FORCE PRESERVATION

## AND RECOVERY

4-169. CASEVAC plays a critical role in preserving combat power by allowing commanders to remove WII
personnel from the battlefield. When MEDEVAC platforms are not available, commanders may direct
nonmedical assets to conduct CASEVAC. This flexibility helps deliver casualties to care in time to prevent
their condition from worsening and increases the likelihood of returning to duty. Although FM 3-08 does not
dictate how RTD or reconstitution is executed, CASEVAC supports both by reducing preventable deaths and
starting medical treatment as early as possible. Reconstitution is an operation that commanders plan and
implement to restore units to a desired level of combat effectiveness commensurate with mission
requirements and available resources (ATP 3-94.4).
4-170. Timely decision-making at the POI is critical to maximizing the recovery potential of WII Soldiers
and their eventual RTD. This, in turn, directly contributes to maintaining unit cohesion, morale, and combat
effectiveness. CASEVAC tasks, when integrated into tactical and operational plans, allow units to adapt to
complex battlefield conditions while continuing to preserve the force.
4-171. Commanders at every level should treat CASEVAC as a key part of achieving operational success. It
goes beyond moving casualties, it helps sustain combat power through timely coordination of assets, triage,
and movement. When well executed, CASEVAC reduces the need for replacements, speeds up recovery, and
supports the force’s overall readiness and endurance.
**78**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**

### Appendix A

## Casualty Evacuation in Specific Environments

Conducting CASEVAC in complex environments requires commanders to tailor strategies to
the unique challenges of each setting. These environments may limit access, restrict movement,
or delay evacuation timelines, demand adaptable planning, specialized equipment, and
rehearsed procedures. This appendix introduces how commanders modify CASEVAC
execution based on environmental factors to maintain operational momentum, preserve life,
and support the maneuver force across any terrain.

## URBAN AND SUBTERRANEAN ENVIRONMENTS

A-1. In urban and subterranean environments, dense infrastructure, confined spaces, and the presence of
civilians create complex challenges for military operations, including CASEVAC. These environments often
hinder movement, reduce visibility, and increase the risk of ambushes, requiring adaptable and innovative
CASEVAC approaches. Proper planning and execution are critical to minimizing risks and ensuring timely
evacuation of casualties in these demanding environments. ATP 3-06, ATP 3-06.1, ATP 3-06.11, and
ATP 3-21.51 provide essential guidance for conducting CASEVAC in urban and subterranean settings.
A-2. These publications emphasize the need to tailor operations to the unique challenges posed by urban and
subterranean environments. Leaders anticipate threats, plan across all levels, and account for the specific
injuries and movement considerations that arise in these environments.

### THREATS

A-3. Urban and subterranean environments present a dual threat: the operational challenges of manmade
infrastructure and the tactical risks posed by adversaries. High population density, structural obstructions,
and concealed enemy positions necessitate advanced planning and coordination to execute CASEVAC
operations.
A-4. The urban environment complicates CASEVAC with obstacles such as narrow streets, rubble from
destroyed buildings, and damaged infrastructure. Subterranean spaces like tunnels, sewers, and basements
amplify these challenges with confined spaces, poor ventilation, and limited visibility. CASEVAC teams
navigate these hazards while managing risks like structural collapse and exposure to hazardous materials.
A-5. Adversaries use the dense, complex terrain of urban and subterranean environments to launch ambushes,
set up strongholds, and place booby traps. Subterranean networks also give them the ability to move
undetected and strike unexpectedly. To reduce these threats and support safe evacuation, CASEVAC teams
work closely with intelligence, surveillance, reconnaissance, and security elements. This coordination helps
identify hazards and develop protected routes for casualty movement.

### INJURIES

A-6. The urban and subterranean environments influence the types of injuries sustained, the triage process,
and treatment considerations. These factors require tailored medical interventions to address the complex
challenges of operating in confined and hazardous conditions.
A-7. Urban and subterranean operations often result in injuries such as crush injuries from collapsing
structures, smoke inhalation from fires, and chemical burns from hazardous materials. Blunt trauma and
gunshot wounds are also common due to close quarters combat and ambushes. In subterranean spaces, blast
overpressure from explosions can amplify injury severity, leading to lung trauma, internal hemorrhaging, and
traumatic brain injury (known as TBI).
A-8. Triage prioritizes casualties with life-threatening injuries such as airway compromise, severe
hemorrhage, and crush injuries. Blast-related injuries require rapid assessment to detect internal damage,
which may not be immediately visible but can rapidly deteriorate. Subterranean conditions necessitate swift
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**79**
**Appendix A**
evacuation due to poor air quality, oxygen depletion, and the potential for secondary threats such as flooding,
gas leaks, or structural collapse.
A-9. Treatment addresses trauma stabilization, including hemorrhage control, pain management, and airway
management in confined spaces. Teams are equipped to treat inhalation injuries and chemical exposure, often
requiring specialized protective equipment and medications. For blast-related injuries, personnel recognize
symptoms of blast overpressure trauma and be prepared to manage pulmonary injuries, traumatic brain injury,
and internal bleeding.

### MOVEMENT

A-10. Movement in urban and subterranean environments presents unique challenges due to structural
damage, confined spaces, limited evacuation routes, and enemy activity in complex or multi-level structures.
Unlike open environments, CASEVAC teams may have to navigate through rubble, collapsed buildings, and
underground passages, often facing restricted mobility and limited platform access. Successful movement in
these settings requires specialized evacuation techniques, route reconnaissance, and coordination with
maneuver forces to secure pathways for casualty extraction.
A-11. CASEVAC is adapted for urban terrain, often requiring additional armor for protection and compact
designs for maneuverability. In subterranean environments, manual extraction methods like stretchers, ropes,
and winches may be necessary to navigate narrow tunnels and vertical shafts.
A-12. Route planning in urban settings accounts for blocked roads, rubble, and civilian congestion.
Subterranean routes require reconnaissance to identify safe passages and assess structural integrity.
CASEVAC teams anticipate choke points, ambushes, and hazards like flooding, or gas leaks. Redundant
routes and backup plans are essential for navigating these unpredictable environments.
A-13. By addressing these unique challenges, military forces can adapt CASEVAC operations to the complex
demands of urban and subterranean environments. For additional information on urban and subterranean
environments, refer to ATP 3-06, ATP 3-06.1, ATP 3-06.11, and ATP 3-21.51.

## MOUNTAIN AND COLD WEATHER ENVIRONMENTS

A-14. In mountain and cold weather environments, extreme cold, steep terrain, and unpredictable weather
conditions create significant challenges for military operations, including CASEVAC. CASEVAC becomes
critical in these environments, where limited access, low temperatures, and difficult terrain can delay care
and worsen injuries. By adapting CASEVAC practices to these unique challenges, military forces can
enhance casualty outcomes and maintain mission readiness. ATP 3-90.97 provides critical guidance for
planning and executing CASEVAC in such demanding conditions.
A-15. This publication emphasizes the importance of tailoring CASEVAC operations to the environmental
and operational realities of mountain and cold weather environments. Leaders can improve operational
success by understanding the specific threats, planning considerations, injury profiles, and movement
challenges associated with these settings.

### THREATS

A-16. Threats in mountain and cold weather environments are twofold: natural environmental challenges and
enemy activity. Harsh weather, altitude, and rugged terrain affect personnel, equipment, and operational
timelines, while adversaries use these factors to their advantage. Recognizing and mitigating these threats is
essential for successful CASEVAC operations.
A-17. The mountain environment presents dangers such as hypothermia, frostbite, avalanches, and altitude
sickness. Prolonged exposure to freezing temperatures can lead to cold-weather injuries, while altitude
impairs oxygen availability, causing fatigue and reduced effectiveness. Equipment often fails in cold weather,
with batteries draining faster and vehicle mobility hindered by snow and ice. CASEVAC teams prioritize
weather-appropriate clothing, proper vehicle maintenance, and altitude acclimatization to reduce these risks.
A-18. Adversaries in mountainous regions exploit terrain for ambushes and engage targets with precision
fire. They use steep slopes, ridgelines, and poor visibility from snow or fog to limit friendly movement and
observation. CASEVAC teams plan by incorporating reconnaissance, positioning overwatch elements for
**80**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation in Specific Environments**
security, and maintaining reliable communication to reduce the risk of enemy engagement and support safe
evacuation.

### INJURIES

A-19. Mountain and cold weather environments affect the types of injuries sustained, influence triage
decisions, and shape how medical teams provide care. These challenges demand tailored approaches that
align with the physical constraints of the environment.
A-20. Common injuries include hypothermia, frostbite, altitude sickness, and trauma caused by falls or
avalanches. High altitudes also slow wound healing and increase infection risk due to reduced oxygen levels,
which can delay recovery and complicate CASEVAC efforts. To reduce these risks, CASEVAC teams move
casualties to lower altitudes when possible and carry mission-essential supplies, such as oxygen, rewarming
gear, antibiotics, and splints.
A-21. Triage should give priority to casualties with severe cold-related injuries or altitude sickness. These
conditions require fast action to prevent rapid deterioration. In extreme cold, full-body exposure during
assessment can cause heat loss. As in CBRN conditions, patients should first be moved to a warmer space
before a detailed evaluation. Medical personnel should use selective exposure, checking for critical injuries
while keeping the rest of the body insulated. Harsh weather and limited resources call for quick decisions on
who to evacuate first. During transport, CASEVAC teams protect patients from further heat loss using
thermal blankets, wind barriers, and enclosed vehicles when available.
A-22. For hypothermia, treatment prioritizes gradual rewarming using thermal blankets and warm fluids
when available. Protecting the casualty from further cold exposure is essential. Frostbite treatment involves
slow warming and immobilization of affected areas to prevent further damage. For altitude sickness,
evacuation to a lower elevation, coupled with oxygen and fluid administration when possible, is the primary
intervention. Because severe hypothermia can slow metabolism to the point where vital signs may be
undetectable, personnel should continue rewarming and monitoring until a full medical assessment can be
completed. During transport, CASEVAC teams prevent further heat loss and maintain warming through
continued application of thermal blankets or buddy warming.

### MOVEMENT

A-23. CASEVAC movement in mountain and cold-weather environments is often the most challenging
aspect due to steep, loose rocky terrain, icy terrain, and limited mobility. Proper planning and execution of
movement are critical to ensuring casualty survival while maintaining operations security. Additionally, the
physical demands of movement in extreme cold significantly increase energy expenditure for CASEVAC
personnel, which can lead to rapid fatigue and reduced effectiveness. Excessive sweating from overexertion
can also accelerate heat loss and heighten the risk of cold-weather injuries such as hypothermia.
A-24. CASEVAC often requires specialized vehicles such as snowmobiles or tracked carriers. In some cases,
evacuation by rotary-wing aircraft may be necessary, though weather conditions and high altitude often limit
air operations. When vehicle platforms are not viable, teams are prepared for litter carries or manual transport,
which require management of physical exertion to prevent fatigue and overheating. Rotating personnel, using
proper layering techniques, and ensuring hydration are key to sustaining energy levels in cold-weather
evacuations.
A-25. Route planning in mountain and cold weather conditions consider snowpack stability, avalanche
danger, and the limits of narrow passes. Planners should identify alternate routes in case weather or terrain
changes suddenly. Reconnaissance helps assess snow and ice conditions and supports safe movement through
the area. CASEVAC teams account for the physical demands of traveling through deep snow, ice, or thin air
at high altitudes, adjusting speed and rest periods to match the environment. In open, snowy terrain, teams
can reduce the risk of detection by using camouflage and terrain masking techniques.
A-26. Effective CASEVAC operations in mountain and cold-weather environments require addressing
specific challenges to ensure mission success and optimal casualty care. Mitigating the effects of increased
energy expenditure and sweating in cold environments is essential to sustaining CASEVAC personnel and
preventing additional cold-weather injuries. ATP 3-90.97 and TC 3-97.61 provide a framework for
overcoming these obstacles and improving overall operational effectiveness.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**81**
**Appendix A**

## JUNGLE ENVIRONMENT

A-27. The jungle environment poses significant challenges for military operations, including CASEVAC.
Triple-canopy forest, dense vegetation, high humidity, and limited visibility hinder movement and
complicate CASEVAC. Extraction times may be prolonged due to the terrain, environmental hazards, and
lack of infrastructure. ATP 3-90.98 provides doctrinal guidance for conducting operations in jungle
environments, including specific considerations for CASEVAC.
A-28. This publication highlights the need to adapt CASEVAC practices to the jungle's unique challenges.
Leaders address environmental threats, develop robust planning at all levels, and prepare for injuries and
movement obstacles unique to jungle operations.

### THREATS

A-29. Jungle environments present threats from both nature and enemy forces. Thick vegetation,
triple-canopy cover, high humidity, and dangerous wildlife create serious environmental risks. At the same
time, adversaries use the terrain for concealment and ambushes. CASEVAC operations address both types
of hazards to support safe and effective casualty evacuation in this complex environment.
A-30. Jungle terrain is characterized by dense foliage, swamps, steep inclines, and unstable ground, all of
which impede movement and visibility. High humidity accelerates equipment degradation, while heavy rains
can cause flooding and create impassable areas. Wildlife, including venomous snakes, insects, and
disease-carrying organisms, add another layer of risk. CASEVAC teams employ durable equipment, maintain
strict hygiene protocols, and plan for contingencies like alternate routes or extraction methods.
A-31. Adversaries in the jungle environment use dense vegetation for camouflage, sniper positions, and
ambushes. Booby traps, tripwires, and guerrilla tactics are common threats. CASEVAC teams maintain
situational awareness, conduct thorough reconnaissance, and implement security measures to protect
themselves and casualties during evacuation.

### INJURIES

A-32. The jungle environment influences the types of injuries encountered, triage priorities, and treatment
considerations. Common injuries include:
•
Heat-related illnesses (heat exhaustion, heatstroke).
•
Dehydration.
•
Tropical diseases (e.g., malaria, dengue fever, leishmaniasis).
•
Skin infections (e.g., trench foot, jungle rot).
•
Bites and stings from venomous snakes, insects, and other wildlife.
•
Traumatic injuries from falls, vegetation lacerations, or enemy contact.
A-33. Tropical diseases pose a greater threat in jungle environments, as the combination of mosquitoes,
stagnant water, and limited medical access increases infection risk. CASEVAC teams carry appropriate
medications, insect repellents, bandages, and hydration supplies to mitigate these risks.
A-34. Triage in the jungle prioritizes severe trauma, heat-related illnesses, and rapidly worsening infections.
CASEVAC teams monitor casualties for signs of dehydration, systemic infections, or tropical disease
symptoms, as these conditions can deteriorate rapidly without treatment. Limited access to higher levels of
care necessitates rapid care at the POI before evacuation.
A-35. Trench foot and other foot-related injuries require close attention in wet environments where prolonged
moisture exposure is common. CASEVAC teams regularly inspect casualties’ feet, dry and treat affected
areas, and promote the use of proper footwear and routine sock changes to keep conditions from getting
worse.
A-36. Treatment in the jungle often focuses on:
•
Managing wounds to prevent infection.
•
Addressing dehydration and electrolyte imbalances.
•
Providing treatment for venomous bites and stings.
•
Immediate intervention for heatstroke or hypothermia (which can occur during night operations).
**82**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation in Specific Environments**
A-37. In jungle environments, CASEVAC efforts focus more on evacuation and treatment than on preventive
measures. CASEVAC teams prioritize moving casualties to higher roles of care where they can receive more
advanced medical treatment suited to the risks and conditions of the environment.

### MOVEMENT

A-38. Movement in the jungle environment is often slow and resource-intensive due to dense vegetation,
narrow trails, steep terrain, and muddy conditions. Effective planning and execution are critical for timely
and safe evacuation of casualties.
A-39. CASEVAC often relies on foot movement, as vehicles are frequently unable to traverse jungle terrain.
Litter teams, manual extraction methods, and makeshift carrying devices are commonly employed.
Waterways, if available, provide an alternative evacuation route, while rotary-wing aircraft can be used for
aerial extraction if LZs, or hoist capabilities are established.
A-40. Route planning in the jungle consider thick vegetation, mud, and water crossings. Reconnaissance
helps identify safe trails and avoid both natural hazards and enemy ambushes. CASEVAC teams should
prepare multiple route options to respond to sudden changes such as flooding, enemy contact, or shifting
terrain. Coordination with aerial assets, GPS tools, and local guides improves navigation and supports a
faster, more reliable evacuation process.
A-41. By addressing the unique demands of jungle operations, military forces can tailor CASEVAC
procedures to improve casualty survival and support mission success. Effective injury management, detailed
movement planning, and adaptation to the environment are key to successful CASEVAC in jungle terrain.
ATP 3-90.98 provides guidance for overcoming these challenges and improving evacuation operations in
dense, tropical conditions.

## DESERT ENVIRONMENT

A-42. In arid desert environments, extreme heat, rugged terrain, and limited resources create unique
challenges for military operations, including CASEVAC. CASEVAC becomes critical in such conditions,
where medical resources may be scarce, and immediate extraction is necessary to save lives. By tailoring
CASEVAC operations to the unique challenges of the arid desert environment, military forces can improve
casualty outcomes and maintain operational effectiveness. ATP 3-90.99 serves as a foundational resource for
adapting to these demanding conditions.
A-43. This section emphasizes the importance of adapting CASEVAC practices to meet the challenges of
desert environments. By understanding these conditions, leaders can support effective joint operations, plan
across all levels of command, and provide quality care for casualties while keeping the mission on track.

### THREATS

A-44. In the arid desert environment, threats are multifaceted and stem from both natural conditions and
