enemy actions. The harsh climate can impair personnel performance and degrade equipment, while
adversaries exploit the terrain to their advantage. Understanding these threats is vital for mitigating risks
during CASEVAC operations and maintaining mission effectiveness.
A-45. The desert environment presents extreme heat, dehydration risks, and dust storms. Prolonged exposure
to high temperatures can cause heatstroke, heat exhaustion, and dehydration. Sand and dust infiltrate vehicle
components and electronic systems, causing malfunctions that complicate evacuation efforts. These
environmental threats necessitate constant maintenance of vehicles and robust hydration and cooling plans
to protect personnel and equipment.
A-46. Adversaries in desert environments often use hit-and-run tactics, ambushes, and explosive hazards.
They leverage the open terrain for long-range attacks while utilizing natural concealment in dunes and rocky
outcroppings. CASEVAC teams maintain heightened situational awareness, employ effective route
reconnaissance, and integrate security measures to counter these enemy threats.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**83**
**Appendix A**

### INJURIES

A-47. The arid desert environment shapes the types of injuries that occur, influences how casualties are
triaged, and affects treatment decisions. Understanding these factors helps medical teams provide timely and
appropriate care that addresses the specific challenges of desert operations.
A-48. Heat-related injuries, such as dehydration, heat exhaustion, and heatstroke, are prevalent. Blunt trauma
from vehicle rollovers and burns from direct sunlight exposure or explosions are also common. Evacuation
teams anticipate these injuries and carry appropriate medical supplies.
A-49. Triage in desert conditions prioritizes casualties suffering from heat-related illnesses and severe
trauma. Environmental factors, such as heat and limited shade, require quick assessment, and care efforts to
prevent secondary complications.
A-50. Treatment focuses on rapid cooling for heat injuries and providing adequate hydration. For trauma,
hemorrhage control and pain management are critical. The scarcity of resources necessitates creative
solutions, like using vehicle air conditioning systems for cooling, or leveraging natural terrain features for
shelter during treatment.

### MOVEMENT

A-51. Moving casualties in the arid desert environment demands close attention to terrain, platform
limitations, and route selection. Effective planning supports the safe and timely evacuation of WII personnel
while reducing their exposure to environmental hazards and enemy threats.
A-52. CASEVAC, including tactical trucks and armored personnel carriers, are often adapted in the desert.
These platforms should be equipped with shade, cooling systems, and medical supplies to address
environmental challenges.
A-53. Route planning accounts for loose sand, rocky terrain, and potential ambush points. Teams should
avoid predictable paths and use reconnaissance to identify safe routes. Nighttime movements can mitigate
heat exposure but require enhanced navigation capabilities to prevent disorientation.

## ARCTIC AND EXTREME COLD ENVIRONMENTS

A-54. The arctic and extreme cold environment presents unique challenges for military operations, including
CASEVAC. Extreme cold, limited visibility, and vast, barren landscapes make CASEVAC complex and
resource intensive. CASEVAC becomes critical in this environment due to the potential for rapid
deterioration of casualties exposed to the elements. ATP 3-90.96 provides guidance for adapting CASEVAC
practices to the unique challenges of cold-weather operations.
A-55. Extreme cold environments require specific adjustments to equipment, planning, and procedures.
Leaders address environmental hazards, account for cold-related injuries and movement challenges, and plan
across all levels of operation to support successful CASEVAC missions.

### THREATS

A-56. In the arctic and extreme cold environment, threats stem from harsh environmental conditions and
enemy exploitation of terrain. Severe cold can incapacitate personnel and equipment, while adversaries
leverage the challenges of the environment to disrupt operations. CASEVAC operations adapt to mitigate
these threats.
A-57. Extreme cold puts personnel at risk for frostbite, hypothermia, and other cold-weather injuries that can
develop. Snowstorms and poor visibility slow movement and disrupt communication, while frozen terrain
makes it harder for vehicles and personnel to operate. CASEVAC teams rely on specialized vehicles,
cold-weather gear, and constant weather monitoring to carry out operations safely in these conditions.
A-58. Adversaries operating in arctic environments often use the terrain for concealment and ambushes.
Limited infrastructure and harsh weather can delay reinforcements or support. CASEVAC teams maintain
heightened situational awareness, employ security measures, and plan for adversary disruption, such as
attacks on evacuation routes.
**84**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation in Specific Environments**

### INJURIES

A-59. Arctic and extreme cold environments affect the kinds of injuries that occur, shape triage priorities,
and change how medical teams approach treatment. Addressing these factors with the right planning and care
methods improves casualty outcomes and increases survival in harsh conditions.
A-60. Injuries common in extreme cold environments include frostbite, hypothermia, and blunt trauma from
falls on ice. Additionally, prolonged exposure to wet and cold conditions, even above freezing temperatures,
can lead to trench foot, which remains a concern in winter operations. CASEVAC teams anticipate and
prepare for these injuries by carrying appropriate warming devices, insulating materials, and advanced
life-support equipment.
A-61. Triage in extreme cold prioritizes casualties with life-threatening cold injuries, such as severe
hypothermia. Prolonged exposure to the elements can exacerbate other injuries, necessitating rapid care and
evacuation. Teams should balance the urgency of care with the operational challenges of moving casualties
in harsh weather.
A-62. Treatment emphasizes rewarming techniques for hypothermia, including warm intravenous fluids and
insulated blankets. Frostbite requires rewarming and protection from refreezing, while injuries like fractures
are stabilized with insulated splints to prevent cold-related complications.

### MOVEMENT

A-63. Movement in arctic and extreme cold environments is severely constrained by snow, ice, and extreme
weather conditions. Effective planning and execution of casualty movement is critical to ensuring safety and
minimizing exposure to environmental hazards.
A-64. CASEVAC often requires specialized vehicles such as snowmobiles, sleds, or tracked transport
vehicles. Rotary-wing aircraft equipped for cold-weather operations may be used but are often limited by
snowstorms and low visibility. Teams are prepared to carry casualties manually over short distances in
extreme weather.
A-65. Route planning in extreme cold conditions considers snowpack stability, ice thickness, and avalanche
risks. CASEVAC teams conduct detailed reconnaissance to identify the safest routes and develop alternate
plans for sudden weather shifts. GPS tools and pre-marked evacuation paths help guide movement, while
teams remain ready to adjust routes as new obstacles or hazards appear.
A-66. By addressing the unique challenges of the arctic and extreme cold environment, military forces can
adapt CASEVAC operations to improve casualty outcomes and operational effectiveness. ATP 3-90.96
provides comprehensive guidance to meet these demands and optimize CASEVAC in these extreme
conditions.

## MARITIME AND LITTORAL ENVIRONMENTS

A-67. Maritime and littoral environments pose unique challenges for Army operations, especially during
joint and multinational missions where naval forces control much of the operational environment. These
environments include coastal zones, open seas, and riverine areas, where CASEVAC operations overcome
movement limitations, minimal infrastructure, and harsh environmental conditions. Unlike land-based
operations, Army units operating in maritime settings often rely on joint partners, amphibious platforms, and
nonstandard evacuation assets to stabilize and evacuate casualties. For additional information, refer to
ATP 3-04.18.
A-68. To conduct effective CASEVAC in maritime and littoral settings, Army commanders coordinate with
joint and multinational partners, identify alternative evacuation routes, and manage the logistical challenges
of moving casualties across water. These operations involve ship-to-shore transfers, waterborne evacuations,
and often longer transport times due to limited access to MTFs. Commanders align CASEVAC plans with
naval and amphibious procedures to support operational tempo and deliver timely medical care under
demanding conditions.
A-69. Maritime CASEVAC operations present unique challenges, including patient stability, environmental
exposure, and medical sustainment limitations. Unlike ground or air transport, maritime evacuation is
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**85**
**Appendix A**
constrained by weather, sea conditions, and vessel capabilities. Many watercraft lack enclosed compartments,
climate control, or sanitation facilities, exposing casualties to cold, heat, dehydration, and infection risks.
A-70. During wet-gap crossings or riverine operations, commanders designated CASEVAC vessels and
verify that these platforms can carry and secure litter casualties. Improvised or nonmedical watercraft may
require temporary modifications, including litter tie-down systems, waterproof storage for medical supplies,
and shoreline transfer points to support patient movement under changing conditions.
A-71. Personnel aboard CASEVAC watercraft are prepared for motion-induced instability, which can
complicate intravenous fluid administration and casualty monitoring. In amphibious operations, pre-planned
integration with rotary-wing MEDEVAC assets is essential to minimize prolonged waterborne transport
times.

### THREATS

A-72. The maritime and littoral environments present threats ranging from natural hazards to operational
constraints and enemy actions. CASEVAC operations account for rough seas, limited LZs, and adversarial
threats while also addressing broader operational risks that could restrict MEDEVAC capabilities.
A-73. In maritime and littoral settings, environmental threats include rough seas, high winds, and limited
visibility due to fog or storms. Saltwater exposure accelerates equipment corrosion, and fluctuating tides can
render key access points or LZs inaccessible. CASEVAC teams employ weather-resistant watercraft or aerial
assets and continuously reassess viable extraction points.
A-74. Enemy threats in these environments range from tactical to strategic risks. These threats can disrupt
CASEVAC operations at multiple levels, from direct attacks on evacuation assets to broader efforts that
degrade C2, restrict maneuverability, or deny access to critical evacuation routes.
A-75. Tactically, adversaries may use small boats, coastal artillery, or amphibious forces to disrupt
CASEVAC operations. In littoral zones, dense vegetation and narrow waterways provide cover for ambushes.
CASEVAC teams implement robust reconnaissance, maintain situational awareness, and coordinate with
naval and air assets for protection.
A-76. Operationally, reliance on riverine or coastal routes creates vulnerabilities. If CASEVAC operations
are restricted to waterways, adversaries can disrupt them using naval mines, maritime blockades, or
shore-based missile systems. This denial of movement could prevent evacuation altogether, forcing
commanders to identify alternate routes, pre-position medical assets inland, or rely on airborne evacuation
when feasible.
A-77. Strategically, adversaries may employ anti-ship missiles, long-range coastal defenses, cyber or
electronic warfare to disrupt C2 of CASEVAC assets. Interdiction of CASEVAC forces at the operational
level could overwhelm available medical resources, impacting force health and combat power. Commanders
and staff develop risk mitigation strategies, including dispersed medical support zones, redundancy in
evacuation platforms, and deception techniques to reduce vulnerability.

### INJURIES

A-78. The maritime and littoral environments influence the types of injuries sustained, triage priorities, and
treatment approaches. These conditions do not replace traditional combat injuries but add complexity to
casualty care. TCCC principles still apply in theses environments. In addition to injuries from penetrating
trauma or blasts, Soldiers may face environmental hazards that worsen combat wounds or create new threats.
Common injuries in maritime and littoral environments include:
•
Drowning and near-drowning.
•
Hypothermia from prolonged exposure to cold water.
•
Blunt trauma from vessel collisions or rough seas.
•
Lacerations from sharp objects on ships, coral reefs, or submerged debris.
•
Crush injuries from heavy equipment movement on unstable platforms.
•
Immersion injuries from prolonged exposure to wet environments, particularly in already WII
personnel.
**86**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation in Specific Environments**
A-79. Wet water immersion injuries differ from general exposure injuries, particularly for WII individuals.
Open wounds exposed to seawater, river water, or stagnant pools are highly susceptible to infection, including
necrotizing fasciitis and other bacterial complications. Maceration of the skin from prolonged immersion
weakens tissue integrity, delaying wound healing and increasing the risk of secondary injury. Additionally,
immersion foot (a form of non-freezing cold injury) can develop in prolonged wet conditions, causing
numbness, swelling, and infection risk.
A-80. In maritime and littoral environments, triage decisions account for both the type of injury and the
tactical situation. When penetrating or blast trauma is not present, casualties with signs of respiratory distress
from near drowning or severe hypothermia may require early intervention due to the risk of rapid
deterioration. However, traditional combat injuries, such as hemorrhage or airway compromise, still take
triage precedence when present. Personnel apply TCCC guidelines while considering environmental
conditions that can complicate injuries or delay treatment. CASEVAC planning includes clear protocols for
recognizing and managing environmental injuries, even when evacuation options are limited.
A-81. CASEVAC teams remain alert for infection risks in all wounds, especially when evacuation is delayed
or casualties are exposed to wet or contaminated environments. While penetrating trauma carries a high
infection risk regardless of setting, prolonged exposure to water can worsen that risk or complicate wound
care. Casualties should receive prophylactic antibiotics in accordance with TCCC guidelines, and timely
evacuation to a higher role of care is critical to prevent sepsis and promote proper healing.

### MOVEMENT

A-82. Movement in maritime and littoral environments involves unique challenges, such as navigating
waterborne obstacles, coordinating between multiple platforms, and dealing with variable weather
conditions. Proper planning and execution are essential for safe and efficient CASEVAC. CASEVAC teams
account for wet gap crossings and riverine operations, where terrain, currents, and limited bridging or ferrying
assets complicate evacuation efforts.
A-83. CASEVAC in these environments often relies on small watercraft, amphibious vehicles, and
rotary-wing aircraft. In some cases, ships serve as forward MTF, requiring casualties to be transferred
between platforms. During wet gap crossings or riverine operations, CASEVAC teams may need to use
improvised rafts, bridges, or ferries to transport casualties across water obstacles. Riverbanks with steep
embankments, mud, or dense vegetation further complicate movement. Teams are proficient in transferring
casualties safely in dynamic environments, such as moving from a small boat to a larger vessel.
A-84. Route planning for wet-gap crossings consider tidal patterns, water depth, currents, and hazards like
reefs, submerged debris, or sandbars. CASEVAC teams coordinate with engineers to confirm the availability
of bridges or rafts and identify alternate evacuation points on both sides of the crossing. To reduce exposure
to enemy observation or attack, teams avoid predictable routes and time movements carefully. Coordination
with naval and aerial assets adds flexibility and lowers the risk during evacuation operations.
A-85. By addressing the specific challenges of maritime and littoral environments, military forces can adapt
CASEVAC operations to improve casualty outcomes and mission success. ATP 3-04.18 provides essential
guidance for optimizing CASEVAC in these dynamic and high-risk settings.

## CHEMICAL, BIOLOGICAL, RADIOLOGICAL, AND NUCLEAR

## ENVIRONMENTS

A-86. A CBRN environment poses significant challenges for military operations, including CASEVAC.
CBRN hazards, requirements for personnel protective equipment, and the need to protect both casualties and
responders from contamination, create a highly complex operational situation. CASEVAC in a CBRN
environment requires thorough preparation, training, hazard awareness, and understanding.
A-87. For additional information on planning and conducting CASEVAC operations in CBRN conditions,
refer to ATP 3-05.11, ATP 3-11.32, ATP 3-11.33, ATP 3-11.36, ATP 4-02.7, ATP 4-02.83, ATP 4-02.84,
ATP 4-02.85, and FM 3-11. These publications highlight the need to integrate CBRN defense measures,
specialized medical response, and thorough planning to maintain force readiness and carry out successful
CASEVAC missions.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**87**
**Appendix A**

### THREATS

A-88. CBRN threats include enemies and adversaries with the intent and capability to deliver CBRN hazards.
CBRN hazards include, CBRN material created from accidental or deliberate releases of toxic industrial
materials, chemical and biological agents, radiological materials, nuclear materials and hazards resulting
from the employment of weapons of mass destruction or encountered by U.S. Armed Forces during the
execution of military operations. CBRN hazards create conditions that can damage or destroy life or vital
resources or prevent mission accomplishment. CASEVAC teams use hazard awareness and understanding to
protect themselves and evacuate casualties safely.
A-89. A CBRN environment is an operational environment that includes CBRN threats and hazards and their
resulting effects. These hazards can cause immediate or delayed casualties, depending on the hazard, and
effectiveness of mitigation measures. Weather characteristics (such as wind, humidity, temperature), time of
day, and terrain determine the effects of environmental impacts on CBRN hazards. Predictive modeling
assists in understanding how weather and terrain affect the hazard.
A-90. Adversaries may target land and maritime areas for CBRN attacks, such as choke points, key terrain,
and transportation nodes. They may deliberately target CASEVAC operations using contamination to deny
freedom of movement or employ follow-on attacks to disrupt evacuation efforts. Maintaining movement
control, keeping lines of communication open, and marking CBRN hazards are critical to continuing
CASEVAC operations in a CBRN environment.

### INJURIES

A-91. A CBRN environment significantly influences the types of injuries sustained, triage priorities, and
treatment approaches. CBRN injuries often occur with other injuries (such as shrapnel, burns, or fractures).
Heat stress and dehydration from personnel protective equipment, if not mitigated by increased fluid intake,
may create heat injuries. The perception of a CBRN threat, whether real or not, may increase stress in Service
members and increase the likelihood of combat operational stress reactions. Understanding these factors is
essential for effective care and the safe evacuation of casualties.
A-92. CBRN injuries vary depending on the hazard and contamination mitigation measures. Chemical
warfare agents are classified by their physiological action or their military use. A choking agent produces
irritation to the eyes and upper respiratory tract and damage to the lungs, primarily causing pulmonary edema.
A nerve agent interferes with the transmission of nerve impulses, causing cholinergic crisis. Blood agents
prevent the normal utilization of oxygen by body tissues, resulting in cessation of respiration followed by
cardiovascular collapse. Blister agents injure the eyes and lungs, and burn or blister the skin, and when
inhaled, damage mucous membranes and respiratory tract. Symptoms from biological hazards may take days
to weeks to manifest. Radiological injuries occur from the effect of ionizing radiation on tissue, most notably
acute radiation syndrome and cutaneous radiation syndrome. Nuclear detonation may cause blast injuries
from direct blast wave overpressure (such as perforation of the ear drums), crush and translational injuries
and missile injuries. Thermal burns will be the most common injuries from nuclear detonation. Combined
blast, thermal and radiological injuries will be common.
A-93. Triage prioritizes casualties with immediate life-threatening conditions, such as severe respiratory
distress or hemorrhage. In a CBRN environment, triage also considers the level of contamination of the
patient to achieve the most orderly, timely, and efficient use of resources. Casualties are simultaneously
triaged as to their need for medical care, their priority for patient decontamination, and their priority for
evacuation to the next role of care. One patient may be medically triaged as immediate but not have priority
for decontamination until they are medically stable. Another may have priority for evacuation but requires
decontamination. The purpose of triage is to effectively allocate the medical resources available.
A-94. Treatment often requires specialized interventions, such as administering medical countermeasures for
chemical agents, providing antibiotics or antivirals for biological hazards, and managing combined injuries
from nuclear detonations. Decontamination is a critical step before and during treatment to minimize the
spread of contamination. Evacuation teams may assist a casualty in performing immediate decontamination
by using the casualty’s individual decontamination equipment. Patient decontamination is performed on
patients requiring treatment or evacuation to a higher role of care. Teams monitor casualties for delayed
**88**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation in Specific Environments**
effects, as some CBRN injuries manifest hours or days after exposure. Refer to ATP 4-02.7 for more
information.

### MOVEMENT

A-95. Moving casualties in a CBRN environment is particularly challenging due to cross contamination risks,
limited mobility in personal protective gear, and the need for dedicated evacuation platforms in the
contaminated area. Effective planning and execution are critical to ensuring casualty safety and mission
success.
A-96. CASEVAC in a CBRN environment requires unit leaders to carefully assess how many evacuation
platforms they will commit to contaminated areas. Ground vehicle routes that cross contaminated areas are
considered dirty routes and should not be used by clean vehicles unless necessary. Commanders consider the
effects of wind and time on contaminants, as some agents can persist in an area for extended periods. Aerial
assets may support CASEVAC operations but mitigate contamination risks and be prepared to conduct
decontamination. Movement through contaminated areas is planned to minimize exposure and prevent
unnecessary contamination. Commanders should make every effort to minimize the number of evacuation
assets exposed to contamination, preserving evacuation capabilities for future operations.
A-97. Patient protection during evacuation should match the unit’s MOPP posture. Patient decontamination
teams at the MTF’s patient decontamination site should remove the patient’s MOPP ensemble. The forward
deployed MTFs will not have replacement MOPP ensembles for the patients. The MTFs place these patients
in a patient protective wrap before they are evacuated from the clean treatment area. The patient protective
wrap provides the same level of protection as the MOPP ensemble.
A-98. If evacuation personnel enter a radiologically contaminated area, it is conducted in accordance with
the operational exposure guidance. Once a unit or individual is exposed to radiation, exposure records are
maintained by the CBRN officer or noncommissioned officer and made available to the commander, staff,
and command surgeon. The exposure is also entered into the individual’s medical and or dosimetry record.
Additional personnel may be required for evacuation teams, so individuals do not exceed the operational
exposure guidance.

## ROUGH TERRAIN

A-99. Rough terrain is found in many environments, including mountains, forests, and urban areas, and it
poses significant challenges to CASEVAC operations. These landscapes include both natural and man-made
obstacles such as ravines, cliffs, canals, embankments, and unstable surfaces. Moving casualties through
rough terrain requires more time, effort, and resources due to the physical difficulty of extraction and the
potential for delays.
A-100. CASEVAC operations in these environments demand specialized planning, equipment, and
techniques to navigate obstacles safely. Delays in evacuation can severely impact casualty survival rates and
mission effectiveness, making terrain adaptation a critical factor in operational success.
A-101. ATP 3-21.18, ATP 3-21.50, ATP 4-02.2, and ATP 4-02.13 provide essential guidance on conducting
CASEVAC in rough terrain. These manuals emphasize adapting operations to rugged landscapes to preserve
the force and maintain operational readiness.

### THREATS

A-102. In rough terrain environments, CASEVAC teams face two main threats: natural hazards and enemy
forces. Natural hazards, such as steep slopes, loose rocks, and rivers, make movement difficult and slow
down evacuation efforts. At the same time, enemy forces take advantage of these challenges by setting up
obstacles, ambushes, and indirect fire attacks to disrupt CASEVAC operations.
A-103. To conduct successful evacuations, units understand the full range of threats and take action to reduce
risk. Terrain analysis, detailed route planning, and reconnaissance help CASEVAC teams move through
challenging environments. Security measures, real-time intelligence, and coordinated fire support help
counter enemy activity. By preparing for both environmental and enemy hazards, CASEVAC teams increase
the likelihood of safely evacuating casualties and completing the mission.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**89**
**Appendix A**

### NATURALTERRAINHAZARDS

A-104. Rough terrain poses significant mobility challenges, including steep slopes, loose rocks, dense
vegetation, and river crossings. These conditions slow evacuation efforts, increase the risk of vehicle damage,
and heighten the chance of injury to casualties and litter teams. Additionally, navigating terrain obstacles
may require detours or specialized movement techniques, such as rappelling or constructing makeshift
crossings. Other environmental constraints include:
•
Limited visibility and navigational difficulty, requiring CASEVAC teams to use enhanced route
planning and GPS mapping.
•
Time-of-day restrictions, as movement may be restricted to darkness to reduce the likelihood of
detection.
•
Prolonged care requirements, as delays in evacuation may necessitate sustained medical intervention
in austere conditions.
A-105. Postponing evacuation may reduce immediate risk but could also prolong the casualty’s exposure to
environmental hazards and medical deterioration. These considerations are factored into mission planning to
determine the best course of action.

### ENEMYTHREATS ANDEXPLOITATION OFTERRAIN

A-106. Adversaries will exploit rough terrain hazards by reinforcing obstacles, deploying mines, and using
direct and indirect fires to target CASEVAC teams. The restricted mobility of rough terrain makes
CASEVAC elements particularly vulnerable to ambushes and artillery strikes. To mitigate these risks,
reconnaissance and planning are essential. CASEVAC teams must:
•
Identify danger areas through detailed reconnaissance, including drone surveillance, scouting, and
terrain analysis.
•
Develop TTPs to mitigate enemy threats, such as using route deception, decoy movements, or
coordinating suppressive fire support.
•
Integrate security elements into CASEVAC missions, ensuring force protection assets accompany
evacuation teams when operating in high-risk areas.
•
Establish contingency plans for alternate CASEVAC routes in the event of ambushes, blocked
terrain, or sudden enemy reinforcement.
A-107. By incorporating terrain analysis, enemy threat assessments, and CASEVAC security measures, units
can minimize risks and enhance the survivability of evacuation operations. Effective coordination with
intelligence, reconnaissance assets, and maneuver elements further improves CASEVAC success by
identifying potential threats in advance and ensuring responsive support during evacuation.

### INJURIES

A-108. The rough terrain environment significantly influences injury patterns, triage priorities, and treatment
approaches. Effective medical care in these conditions requires tailored interventions based on
terrain-associated risks. Medical planners conduct terrain-based medical threat analysis to anticipate injury
types and logistical challenges, using this information to adjust medical supply requirements and refine
CASEVAC TTPs to mitigate risk.
A-109. Injuries in rough terrain commonly include:
•
Fractures, sprains, and blunt trauma from falls, slips, or rolling debris.
•
Environmental exposure injuries, such as hypothermia, dehydration, and heat exhaustion, depending
on terrain and climate.
•
Altitude-related conditions, such as acute mountain sickness, high-altitude pulmonary edema, and
high-altitude cerebral edema.
•
Overuse injuries, such as stress fractures and soft tissue damage from prolonged movement in
unstable terrain.
A-110. CASEVAC teams anticipate these types of injuries and carry the right supplies to manage them. This
includes first-aid materials, hydration systems, and gear to protect against environmental hazards.
**90**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation in Specific Environments**
A-111. Personnel use a trauma algorithm to identify and treat life-threatening injuries during triage. This
algorithm, known as MARC2H3-PAWS-L, stands for massive hemorrhage/MASCAL, airway, respirations,
circulation, communications, hypo/hyperthermia and head injuries, pain control, antibiotics, wounds
(including nursing and burns), splinting, logistics. This standard, established by the Joint Trauma System and
the Committee on TCCC, provides a clear format for treatment prioritization. In rough or mountainous
terrain, where evacuation is often delayed, following the MARC2H3-PAWS-L algorithm helps determine
which casualties require the highest priority for evacuation and care.
A-112. Treatment in rough terrain prioritizes:
•
Hemorrhage control with tourniquets and hemostatic dressings.
•
Fracture stabilization using splints and improvised supports.
•
Environmental injury management, including rewarming for hypothermia and aggressive hydration
for heat-related conditions.
•
Immobilization techniques for spinal injuries to reduce further damage during transport.
A-113. CASEVAC teams prepare for prolonged care scenarios when immediate evacuation is not possible.
This requires portable medical equipment and sustainment planning to manage casualties over extended
periods. Building this capability supports continuous care, protects life during evacuation delays, and helps
maintain operational effectiveness in austere or contested environments.

### MOVEMENT

A-114. Movement in rough terrain is one of the most complex and physically demanding aspects of
CASEVAC operations. Steep slopes, gaps, cliffs, and ravines require specialized techniques and equipment
to safely and efficiently evacuate casualties.
A-115. CASEVAC in rough terrain often requires manual extraction using stretchers, ropes, pulley systems,
or specialized litter-carrying techniques. In mountainous areas or dense jungle, teams may use pack animals
when vehicles cannot reach the casualty. All-terrain vehicles, aerial hoists, and other specialized platforms
may also support movement if the terrain and threat level allow. CASEVAC teams adjust their movement
methods based on terrain stability, weather conditions, and the casualty’s medical needs to support safe and
effective transport.
A-116. Route planning accounts for multiple terrain challenges, including:
•
Terrain stability, slope gradient, and natural obstacles (such as, fallen trees, boulders, rockslides).
•
Seasonal and weather effects, such as rain, snow, flooding, or extreme heat, which can alter terrain
conditions.
•
Enemy threats, including potential ambush points and the need for security measures along
evacuation routes.
•
Alternative routes and contingency plans to mitigate environmental disruptions or battlefield
conditions.
A-117. Extended evacuation distances and rough terrain may require multiple litter teams to relay casualties
along the route. This approach keeps movement steady while lowering the chance of fatigue-related injuries
among personnel. To improve CASEVAC operations in challenging environments, units should:
•
Pre-position medical assets along extended routes to maintain continuous care.
•
Train personnel in specialized CASEVAC techniques, such as high-angle rescue, jungle extractions,
or mountain operations.
•
Coordinate with aviation or ground support units to reduce transport time when possible.
A-118. By addressing these challenges, military forces can adapt CASEVAC operations to improve casualty
outcomes and sustain mission effectiveness. For detailed guidance on conducting CASEVAC in rough
terrain, refer to ATP 3-21.18, ATP 3-21.50, ATP 4-02.2, and ATP 4-02.13.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**91**

## This page intentionally left blank.

### Appendix B

## Mass Casualty Events

MASCAL events occur when the number of casualties overwhelms available medical
resources, making timely evacuation and treatment critical to saving lives. These situations
require precise planning, coordination, and adaptability across all echelons. CASEVAC plays
a vital role in moving WII to MTFs where they can receive further care.
This appendix examines the complexities of managing MASCAL situations, with a focus on
the critical role of nonmedical platforms in supporting CASEVAC operations. It explores
planning considerations, coordination strategies, execution of CASEVAC, addressing CBRN
hazards, and leveraging technological advancements. By understanding these elements,
military planners can optimize their response to MASCAL events and improve outcomes in the
most demanding conditions.

## MASS CASUALTY SITUATIONS

B-1. MASCAL situations present some of the most challenging and resource-intensive conditions in military
and operational environments. The ability to manage MASCAL scenarios depends on pre-established
strategies, integration of CASEVAC platforms, and adaptability to unique threats, including communicable
diseases, and biological hazards.
B-2. This section explores the core challenges of MASCAL operations, beginning with triage and
decision-making under extreme conditions, including the psychological and ethical burdens placed on
medical responders. It then highlights the critical role of CASEVAC assets in managing MASCAL events
when traditional MEDEVAC resources are overwhelmed or unavailable. Finally, it addresses the unique
considerations for MASCAL scenarios involving communicable diseases and biological threats, emphasizing
the need for isolation procedures, bio-containment capabilities, and coordination with public health
authorities. By understanding these key elements, commanders and medical planners can develop more
effective MASCAL strategies to enhance casualty care and operational effectiveness in high-stress
environments.

### CHALLENGES OFMASSCASUALTYSITUATIONS

B-3. MASCAL scenarios occur when the number of casualties exceeds the available medical resources,
overwhelming the system’s capacity to provide immediate treatment and evacuation. These events are
characterized by chaos, resource constraints, and the urgent need for rapid triage and decision-making.
Without pre-planned strategies and effective coordination among medical, operational, and logistical teams,
delays in evacuation and care can result in preventable loss of life.
**Note.** While the definition of a MASCAL does not change, its scale and operational impact vary
by echelon. What overwhelms a squad’s ability to respond may differ significantly from what
stresses a brigade’s resources. Commanders and staff assess available capabilities and respond
accordingly when medical requirements exceed local capacity.
B-4. Triage is one of the most critical parts of a MASCAL response. Protocols are clear, flexible, and applied
consistently so that casualties with the greatest chance of survival receive priority care. Triage becomes more
complex during combat, where enemy contact, chemical or biological threats, and limited medical personnel
make decision-making even more difficult.
B-5. Beyond the physical toll, MASCAL situations also present psychological and ethical challenges for
responders. Moral injury, the distress experienced when individuals make difficult life-and-death decisions
under extreme conditions, can have long-term impacts on medical personnel, leaders, and Soldiers. Failing
to prepare first responders for these realities can lead to long-term psychological distress, reduced combat
effectiveness, and diminished unit morale.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**93**
**Appendix B**
B-6. Triage during MASCAL events presents both operational and ethical challenges for medical personnel.
The overwhelming number of casualties forces split-second decisions on who receives immediate care and
who may not survive. These choices can lead to moral injury; psychological distress caused by violating
one’s ethical or professional standards. Personnel are prepared for the emotional and ethical burden of triage,
particularly in scenarios where limited resources mean some casualties cannot be treated immediately.
B-7. To reduce psychological strain, commanders provide training that prepares personnel to make triage
decisions under stress during MASCAL events. Support mechanisms, such as AARs, embedded behavioral
health teams, and chaplain services, should be built into CASEVAC operations to support responders after
the mission. Ignoring the mental well-being of CASEVAC teams can weaken operational performance and
harm long-term force readiness.

### THEROLE OFCASUALTYEVACUATION INMASSCASUALTYSITUATIONS

B-8. CASEVAC platforms are essential during MASCAL events, especially when MEDEVAC assets are
unavailable, overwhelmed, or unable to operate due to mission constraints. Whether military or nonmilitary,
CASEVAC platforms offer flexible and scalable options for moving casualties in austere conditions.
•
Rapid Evacuation: CASEVAC enables the quick removal of casualties from the POI, easing
congestion onsite and allowing medical teams to focus on stabilizing those who remain.
•
Resource Bridging: When MEDEVAC is not available, CASEVAC still provides a means to move
casualties, helping prevent conditions from worsening.
•
Integration into MASCAL Planning: Commanders improve evacuation efficiency by identifying
CASEVAC-capable vehicles and aircraft in advance, reducing the need for improvised solutions.

### MASSCASUALTYCONSIDERATIONS FORCOMMUNICABLEDISEASE ANDBIOLOGICAL

### THREATS

B-9. MASCAL scenarios are not limited to combat-related injuries, they also include disease outbreaks,
pandemics, and biological warfare incidents. In these cases, traditional triage and evacuation procedures may
need modification to prevent the spread of disease while still ensuring casualty care.
•
Infectious Disease Triage: In disease-driven MASCAL incidents, triage accounts for both medical
severity and infection control measures, ensuring that highly contagious individuals do not
compromise the safety of responders or other casualties.
•
CASEVAC and Containment: Evacuating infected personnel requires dedicated isolation
procedures, including specialized transport equipment (such as bio-containment units),
decontamination measures, and PPE for responders.
•
Logistical Strain on Medical Infrastructure: A MASCAL event involving communicable diseases
can overwhelm both military and civilian medical infrastructure, requiring coordination with public
health agencies, quarantine protocols, and strategic use of medical supply chains.
B-10. Commanders and planners integrate communicable disease control measures into MASCAL strategies,
especially in high-risk environments where biological threats may arise. This includes setting up isolation
procedures, equipping CASEVAC platforms with bio-containment features, and coordinating closely with
medical and public health authorities to prevent disease spread while still providing timely care for casualties.
B-11. Communicable disease outbreaks present unique challenges for CASEVAC operations, requiring strict
biosecurity measures to prevent further spread. Unlike combat trauma, infectious casualties are transported
under isolation protocols to protect medical personnel and uninfected patients. Units should designate
biocontainment CASEVAC platforms, establish decontamination procedures, and implement PPE protocols
for medical teams.
B-12. Commanders work with medical planners to integrate biosecurity measures into MASCAL and
CASEVAC operations. Important considerations include using negative-pressure isolation stretchers,
identifying patient transfer points in advance, and setting up dedicated transport routes for contagious
casualties. In multinational environments, units coordinate their procedures with host-nation and coalition
disease control policies to support unified and effective CASEVAC across all forces.
**94**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Mass Casualty Events**

## PLANNING AND COORDINATION

B-13. Effective MASCAL management starts with detailed planning and coordination. Units develop plans
that identify available resources, assign roles, and outline procedures for executing CASEVAC. Close
coordination with logistics and operations officers helps medical personnel access critical assets such as
evacuation platforms, medical supplies, and communication systems. Rehearsals and training exercises help
expose gaps in the plan and build readiness across all participating elements.
B-14. Integrating CASEVAC into MASCAL plans requires advanced preparation. Commanders identify
platforms, train drivers and nonmedical personnel in CASEVAC procedures, and equip platforms with
medical supplies such as Warrior Aid and Litter Kits. Coordination with host-nation support and civilian
resources further expands the units’ capabilities. By planning proactively, units can reduce delays and
improve casualty outcomes during high-stress situations.

### HOST-NATIONSUPPORT

B-15. Depending on the assigned area, host-nation support agreements may provide evacuation assets
ranging from austere to extensive support. Coordination with civil affairs planners at echelon or the local
embassy country team can provide information on the availability of assets. This information should be
included in the operation plans. Some of the types of assets which might be available for support are:
•
Buses.
•
Barges and other watercraft.
•
Civilian cargo vehicles.

### MEDICALSTAFFING INNONMEDICALPLATFORMS

B-16. Staffing CASEVAC platforms with medical personnel to provide en route care requires deliberate
planning and coordination. Since CASEVAC often relies on vehicles of opportunity, commanders track
available medical personnel, equipment, and transport assets to support timely response. The current medical
system supports this approach by allowing for task-organized medical teams, including four-person treatment
teams assigned to brigade support medical companies, area support medical companies, and many battalion
headquarters.
B-17. Most support organizations maintain trained CLSs who can provide basic en route care for less
seriously wounded, injured, or ill patients when medical personnel are unavailable. By incorporating CLS
personnel and leveraging medically manned CASEVAC principles, commanders can maximize patient
movement efficiency while ensuring casualties receive timely evacuation and care.

## EXECUTION OF CASUALTY EVACUATION

B-18. CASEVAC plays a vital role in executing evacuation during MASCAL events, especially in austere
environments where weather, terrain, or enemy activity limits MEDEVAC operations. Properly equipped
and staffed CASEVAC platforms help move casualties safely and efficiently to higher roles of care. Training
nonmedical personnel to secure patients and provide basic en route care further strengthens the effectiveness
of CASEVAC efforts.
B-19. The successful use of CASEVAC relies on clear communication and coordination. Commanders
establish procedures for allocating these assets, ensuring they are readily available when needed. Regular
rehearsals that include the use of CASEVAC help teams refine their response capabilities. By integrating
CASEVAC into the broader evacuation framework, units can adapt to the dynamic demands of MASCAL
scenarios and save lives.
B-20. Successful management of MASCAL situations depends on developing and rehearsing detailed
MASCAL plans. Several additional factors can contribute to the effectiveness of a unit’s response. These
include, but are not limited to:
•
Identify and train nonmedical personnel to serve on litter teams.
•
Maintain trained and equipped CLSs capable of providing immediate care.
•
Locate WII personnel and move them from the POI to secure locations.
•
Provide effective TCCC to stabilize casualties before movement.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**95**
**Appendix B**
•
Conduct accurate triage to prioritize evacuation based on injury severity.
•
Prepare and stage CASEVAC platforms, including nonmedical vehicles, for rapid loading.
•
Establish and operate CCPs to consolidate casualties prior to onward movement.
•
Coordinate evacuation routes, ambulance loading points, and AXPs to link with higher roles of care.
•
Integrate CASEVAC into the ambulance shuttle system to maintain continuous casualty flow when
MEDEVAC is unavailable or overwhelmed.
•
Assign medical personnel to provide en route care on CASEVAC platforms when possible.
•
Exchange medical equipment (litters, patient securing straps, blankets) to keep platforms mission
ready.
•
Coordinate with key personnel and adjacent units for casualty transfer and onward movement to
MTFs.
•
Track capabilities and locations of MTFs to match casualty needs with available resources.
•
Synchronize communications frequencies and call signs to support coordinated movement.
•
Coordinate additional medical support, including MEDEVAC and forward resuscitative surgical
teams, as required.
•
Plan for CBRN-specific MASCAL considerations, including containment of hazards, protection of
responders, identification of contaminated collection points, evacuation platforms and routes, and
establishment of patient decontamination sites.
B-21. MASCAL situations often present significant challenges. One common obstacle during these events is
poor communication and coordination among medical, logistical, and operational units, especially in
dangerous environments. To overcome these issues, units should conduct regular, realistic training exercises
that include MASCAL scenarios. These rehearsals improve coordination and help teams practice using
interoperable communication systems. Pre-positioning critical supplies, like medical kits, in high-risk areas
also supports faster response times. Host-nation support can play a crucial role in evacuation efforts, but
challenges such as language barriers and operational differences may arise. Joint exercises and maintaining
LNOs can help improve collaboration with host-nation assets.
B-22. Ground assets can be used for CASEVAC when the MEDEVAC system is overwhelmed. All available
ground vehicles should be considered for augmenting MEDEVAC assets in an emergency. The key to success
is identifying the platforms, drivers, and medical personnel or the CLS who will accompany the casualties.
Each of these platforms should be equipped with a Warrior Aid and Litter Kit. The kit is designed to provide
the user with enough medical supplies and a stable evacuation platform for two critically WII casualties. For
more information on the Warrior Aid and Litter Kit, refer to ATP 4-02.13.
B-23. During MASCAL events, units decide when to prioritize CASEVAC over MEDEVAC. CASEVAC is
ideal when MEDEVAC resources are unavailable or overwhelmed. For example, if weather prevents air
MEDEVAC, nonmedical platforms like HMMWVs or cargo trucks can serve as CASEVAC platforms. To
prepare for such scenarios, units should train nonmedical personnel on CASEVAC procedures, including
securing patients, and providing basic care. Equipping platforms with Warrior Aid and Litter Kits helps keep
them ready for emergency use.
B-24. Clear procedures for allocating and releasing CASEVAC assets are established during the planning
phase to support mission success. Proactive coordination allows units to identify and prepare platforms and
personnel in advance, rather than reacting once a MASCAL event begins. Strong planning and
communication help integrate ground-based nonmedical assets into CASEVAC operations, improving
response times and casualty outcomes.
B-25. All organizations have procedures in place to respond to MASCAL situations. The potential for
disasters in war and other operations requires that the medical element be prepared to support MASCAL
situations. They can receive, triage, treat, and evacuate large numbers of casualties within a short period of
time. Contingency plans for supporting MASCAL operations are developed by all units in coordination with
their surgeon, senior physical assistant or senior healthcare noncommissioned officer, operations staff officer,
and logistics staff officer.
**96**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Mass Casualty Events**

## ADDRESSING CHEMICAL, BIOLOGICAL, RADIOLOGICAL, AND

## NUCLEAR, AND COMPLEX HAZARDS IN A MASS CASUALTY

## SITUATION

B-26. CBRN threats add complexity to MASCAL events and require specialized planning and response
procedures. Units include contamination control zones and mitigation measures in their operations. For
example, CCPs for contaminated personnel should be placed upwind of hazard areas and clearly marked for
triage, decontamination, and evacuation staging. Responders are trained to use personal protective equipment
and recognize signs of CBRN exposure. Equipping evacuation platforms with decontamination kits and
maintaining contamination-free zones helps protect both patients and responders. For more on CBRN threats
and symptoms, refer to ATP 4-02.7. For guidance on MASCAL operations in CBRN environments, refer to
ATP 3-11.33.
B-27. Incorporating CBRN scenarios into MASCAL rehearsals is essential for preparedness. Realistic
training allows responders to practice contamination mitigation, decontamination, and evacuation procedures
under simulated hazardous conditions. Exercises should include the use of CBRN detection systems, such as
physical detectors and diagnostic algorithms, casualty triage in contaminated environments, and coordinated
efforts with host-nation resources.
B-28. During a MASCAL event in a CBRN environment, units identify suspected CBRN hazards and report
them. The CBRN Warning and Reporting System (known as CBRNWRS) helps medical personnel and first
responders focus on removing casualties from the battlefield. By coordinating with CBRN units and using
information from CBRNWRS, teams can find safe evacuation routes and locate decontamination sites. For
more details on the CBRN Warning and Reporting System, refer to TM 3-11.32.
B-29. Proper preparation reduces risk and supports a faster, safer response to complex hazards. MASCAL
planning should also include long-term considerations such as monitoring contamination levels, disposing of
contaminated equipment, and treating personnel who were exposed. By addressing both immediate and
ongoing challenges, units strengthen their ability to manage CBRN threats and safeguard both responders
and casualties.

## TECHNOLOGICAL AND PROCEDURAL ADVANCEMENTS

B-30. Technological advancements provide new tools to enhance MASCAL responses. Unmanned systems
can scout evacuation routes, deliver medical supplies, and reduce risks to personnel. Telemedicine devices
allow field medics to consult specialists remotely, enabling advanced care for critically WII patients even in
austere environments.
B-31. Modern vehicles, like the Armored Multi-Purpose Vehicle, provide enhanced protection and flexibility
for CASEVAC operations. These platforms are equipped with advanced medical systems and modular
configurations, enabling rapid adaptation to various mission needs. Integrating these tools into training and
planning streamlines operations, reduces response times, and improves outcomes during MASCAL
scenarios. For instance, rehearsals that incorporate telemedicine devices and unmanned systems have
demonstrated a measurable reduction in treatment delays during exercises.
B-32. While these technologies offer clear benefits, integrating them into MASCAL operations can be
challenging due to cost, logistics, and training demands. Units invest in infrastructure and develop personnel
to use these tools. Updating training to include emerging technologies helps units stay adaptable and ready
for the evolving demands of modern operations.
B-33. Leveraging technology across multimodal CASEVAC operations creates a cohesive and efficient
system. For example, unmanned systems can relay real-time information about evacuation routes to air and
ground assets, while artificial intelligence tools coordinate casualty prioritization across platforms. By
integrating these advancements into planning, training, and execution, units can address challenges, saving
more lives in the most complex scenarios.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**97**
**Appendix B**

## MEASURING AND IMPROVING PERFORMANCE

B-34. Evaluating the success of a MASCAL response requires tracking metrics such as evacuation times,
survival rates, and resource utilization. These metrics provide insights into operational strengths and
weaknesses, enabling units to identify areas for improvement. AARs are essential for analyzing how well
teams adhered to protocols, how they responded, and what improvements are needed.
B-35. Structured evaluation frameworks, like Army’s AAR process, NATO’s lessons framework, and the
Joint Trauma System, help standardize how MASCAL events are assessed. The Joint Trauma System, the
DOW Center of Excellence for Trauma, offers data-driven analysis and best practices for TCCC. These tools
guide units in turning lessons learned into better plans and training programs. Units that regularly revise their
plans using these insights improve their readiness and ability to respond in complex situations.
B-36. Metrics and lessons learned only matter when used to make real improvements. For instance,
identifying evacuation delays might identify a need to adjust CCP placement or communication procedures.
If survival rates are low, leaders may need to improve casualty care training. Digital tools, such as tracking
systems and dashboards, can give commanders real-time updates on patient movement and resource use
during a MASCAL event. Sharing these findings across units and with coalition partners builds shared
understanding and supports better coordinated responses in future operations.

## MASS CASUALTY PLAN

B-37. A well-structured MASCAL plan provides a detailed framework for CASEVAC, resource allocation,
and coordination with supporting units. Figure B-1 below and through page 102 illustrates a sample plan for
a brigade-sized unit. While not exhaustive, this plan serves as a foundation that units can adapt to specific
operational environments and requirements and include in their SOP.
**Mass Casualty Standard Operating Procedure**
1. (U) Purpose. This standard operations plan establishes the procedures and responsibilities in the event
a MASCAL situation occurs within the brigade combat team assigned area.
2. (U) Scope. This standard operations plan is designed to provide procedures for planning for and reacting
to a MASCAL event. The goal of this standard operations plan is to reduce the loss of life and limb by
providing clear and concise guidance.
3. (U) Applicability. All assigned and attached personnel.
4. (U) References.
a. ATP 4-02.2.
b. ATP 4-02.13.
5. (U) Definitions.
a. Ambulance exchange point is a location where a patient is transferred from one ambulance to
another en route to a medical treatment facility. Also called AXP.
b. Casualty is any person who is lost to the organization by having been declared dead, duty
status – whereabouts unknown, excused absence – whereabouts unknown, missing, ill, or injured.
c. Casualty collection point is a location that may or may not be staffed, where casualties are
assembled for evacuation to a medical treatment facility.
d. Casualty evacuation is the movement of wounded, ill or injured personnel aboard any nonmedical
platform; also called CASEVAC.
e. Mass casualty is any number of human casualties produced across a period that exceeds available
medical support capabilities. Also called MASCAL.
f. Medical evacuation is the timely and effective movement of the wounded, injured, or ill to and
between medical treatment facilities on dedicated and properly marked medical platforms with en route
care provided by medical personnel. Also called MEDEVAC.
**Figure B-1. Sample of a mass casualty standard operating procedure**
**98**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Mass Casualty Events**
**Mass Casualty Standard Operating Procedure (continued)**
g. Triage is the system of sorting and prioritizing casualties based on the tactical situation, mission,
available resources, and medical urgency.
6. (U) Concept for a MASCAL situation.
a. A MASCAL situation exists when the casualty load exceeds the capability of the medical treatment
assets available to the unit.
b. The scene commander will be the senior nonmedical person (medical personnel will be providing
treatment). The scene commander will coordinate the efforts of site security, medical treatment teams, and
evacuation teams.
c. Units without organic medical personnel will immediately contact their closest supporting medical
unit, upon suffering mass casualties.
d. All casualties will be moved to designated CCPs.
e. Available medically trained personnel will assess and triage casualties at the CCP.
f. The forward support company of the brigade support battalion will send designated CLSs and
CASEVAC vehicles to assist in the movement of casualties to designated battalion aid stations and MTFs.
The brigade support medical company will send designated medical personnel and ambulances to assist.
g. The scene commander will establish a rotary-wing aircraft LZ for use by air MEDEVAC or CASEVAC
rotary-wing aircraft.
h. A medical officer or physician assistant will head each treatment team of three to four medics.
7. (U) MASCAL triage.
a. Each unit establishes a triage station to sort casualties for treatment. This may be located at a CCP
and staffed initially by trained nonmedical personnel until medical personnel arrive.
b. Units train designated personnel, including combat medics, leaders, and selected nonmedical
Soldiers, in triage procedures. This allows medical personnel to focus on treatment, casualty regulation,
and coordination with CASEVAC assets.
c. The most senior qualified medical provider present serves as the triage officer when available.
d. When multiple casualty waves are expected, the next most qualified and available person takes over
as triage officer for subsequent arrivals, allowing the primary medical provider to direct overall treatment
operations.
e. Combat Lifesavers will not normally perform triage when medical personnel are available but should
be trained in its principles to support MASCAL operations. These personnel may provide the following:
(1) Trauma management within their training limits.
(2) TCCC.
(3) Treatment and monitoring of MINIMAL category casualties.
f. Triage categories. Casualties are sorted and color coded by marking the casualty’s Department of
Defense Form (DD) 1380 (Tactical Combat Casualty Care [TCCC] Card) as follows:
**Mass Casualty Standard Operating Procedure (continued)**

**Treatment Category**

**Figure B-1. Sample of a mass casualty standard operating procedure (continued)**
**30 May 2026**

**99**
**Appendix B**
**Mass Casualty Standard Operating Procedure (continued)**
g. During limited-visibility or night operations, triage areas should be clearly marked using appropriate
light sources to maintain visibility and support casualty management. Units may use color-coded chemlights
(red, yellow, green, and blue or infrared) markers to correspond with triage categories and improve
identification in low-light environments. These markings should be coordinated with unit SOPs and aligned
with tactical considerations to avoid confusion or compromise of position.
h. Minimally injured patients may be used to assist the treatment teams at the discretion of the
treatment or triage officers.
8. MASCAL medical treatment.
a. The focus of medical treatment at a MASCAL site is the preservation of life, limb, or eyesight
(IMMEDIATE and DELAYED categories).
b. Medical treatment personnel will establish a post treatment evacuation holding area, clearly marked
by MEDEVAC precedence. The recommended method of marking the area is to affix signs to stakes in the
ground.
c. If available, the chaplain and combat and operational stress control personnel should be in the
holding area.
d. The holding area should allow for the Soldier’s leaders to take custody of personal effects that do
not accompany the patient through the evacuation process (for example, weapons, night vision devices,
and communication equipment). This holding area is easily accessible to the rotary-wing aircraft LZ and the
ground ambulance route.
9. MASCAL evacuation procedures.
a. All casualties will be taken to the designated company CCP by designated litter teams. The first
sergeant or their representative is responsible for identifying nonmedical Soldiers as members of litter
teams.
b. All company CLSs not actively engaged in force protection will go to the company CCP to assist
medical teams.
c. The medical noncommissioned officer in absence of a physician or physician assistant will supervise
the assessment, triage, and evacuation of casualties at the designated site.
d. The company’s first sergeant or their designated representative will be responsible for the
transportation of casualties from the company CCP, to the designated MTF, or AXP via CASEVAC vehicles.
10. CASEVAC vehicles.
a. The forward support company commander will create an ordered list of the number of vehicles to be
used.
(1) The forward support company supply trucks (for example M1081) will be downloaded with
supplies and designated as evacuation vehicles. These vehicles can be rapidly used to clear the company
CCP and facilitate rapid evacuation of litter patients.
(2) A driver and assistant driver are designated.
(3) The vehicles will be prepared, cleaned, and ready for evacuation of patients.
(4) All CASEVAC vehicles will have litters and litter straps present.
(5) An M1093 5-ton or M1081 2 1/2-ton cargo vehicle can transport a total of seven or eight litter
patients, respectively.
(6) A cargo HMMWV can transport a total of five litter patients, and a four-seat configuration
HMMWV can transport three litter patients.
**Figure B-1. Sample of a mass casualty standard operating procedure (continued)**
**100**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Mass Casualty Events**
**Mass Casualty Standard Operating Procedure (continued)**
b. Vehicle marking.
(1) Whenever the tactical situation allows, CASEVAC vehicles should be marked to indicate that
they are carrying casualties.
(2) Day: Signaling marker panel will be placed on the hood or roof of the vehicle, orange side up,
to indicate that the vehicle is carrying casualties.
(3) Night: Secure two infrared chemical lights to the top front corners of the vehicle, one on the
driver’s side and one on the passenger’s side.
12. MASCAL AAR.
a. Following all MASCAL events, units conduct a structured AAR to evaluate the effectiveness of
response efforts, coordination between medical and operational elements, and adherence to triage,
treatment, and evacuation protocols.
b. The AAR should assess:
(1) The timeliness and accuracy of triage decisions.
(2) Effectiveness of CASEVAC and MEDEVAC coordination
(3) Any shortfalls in medical supplies, staffing, or equipment.
(4) The integration of CASEVAC for casualty movement.
c. Communication and coordination challenges encountered.
d. Findings from the AAR should be documented and disseminated to improve MASCAL SOPs,
training plans, and logistical support planning.
13. Cleaning and Decontamination Procedures for CASEVAC Vehicles and Equipment.
b. All vehicles, equipment, and personnel involved in CASEVAC operations are assessed to determine
the need for and level of decontamination following casualty transport.
c. Cleaning requirements depend on the nature of injuries, patient exposure to bodily fluids, and
potential infectious disease concerns.
d. Standard decontamination procedures include:
(1) When a biological agent, such as anthrax, is suspected, follow contamination mitigation
procedures, including personnel, operational, and thorough decontamination.
(2) Determine the most safe and effective way to remove biological hazards. This will depend on
the nature of the hazard and equipment available.
(3) Removing all biological hazards from vehicle interiors and exteriors, paying attention to
frequently used touch points.
(4) Proper disposal of medical waste, including bandages, intravenous lines, and PPE.
(5) Replacement or sterilization of litter straps, cushions, and protective coverings.
(6) Decontamination of personnel before resuming CASEVAC operations.
e. If operating in a CBRN environment, consolidate decontamination efforts whenever possible. The
needs of CASEVAC and decontamination operations will compete for resources, such as water and
augmentation. Patient decontamination sites should be collocated with personnel and equipment
decontamination to prevent the spread of contamination and consolidate resources.
14. MASCAL Considerations for Communicable Disease and Biosecurity Measures.
a. If a MASCAL event involves casualties exposed to a communicable disease, additional triage,
treatment, and transport considerations are implemented to limit disease spread while ensuring adequate
medical care.
**Figure B-1. Sample of a mass casualty standard operating procedure (continued)**
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**101**
**Appendix B**
**Mass Casualty Standard Operating Procedure (continued)**
b. Triage stations incorporate infection control procedures, including:
(1) Isolation of those infected with a communicable disease and quarantine of those exposed but
not yet ill.
(2) Use of PPE by all responders handling infectious casualties.
(3) Specialized triage categorization for infectious casualties requiring isolation or priority
evacuation to designated MTF.
c. CASEVAC procedures include:
(1) Coordinate for bio-containment transport capability for highly contagious casualties (such as
Air Force negative pressure isolation pods).
(2) Designation of infectious CASEVAC vehicles separate from standard transport assets.
(3) Pre-planned routes to MTFs equipped for infectious disease management.
(4) All medical personnel, CASEVAC crews, and responding units follow post-mission
decontamination protocols before returning to duty.

and the NATO’s five triage categories. The NATO fifth color code is for deceased personnel.

B-38. A comprehensive MASCAL plan should address key components, including CCP locations, triage
prioritization, communication protocols, and the integration of available resources. Assigning clear roles and
responsibilities helps all personnel understand their part in the response. For example, the plan should detail
who oversees patient tracking, manages vehicle dispatch, and coordinates with the higher role of care MTF.
B-39. Customizing the MASCAL plan allows units to address specific challenges, including host-nation
support, terrain, and CBRN threats. For instance, urban plans may emphasize non-linear evacuation routes,
while rural plans may focus on long-distance ground movement. Frequent updates and rehearsals keep the
plan current and help teams identify and fix potential weaknesses before an actual event occurs.
B-40. Incorporating technology can further enhance the effectiveness of MASCAL plans. Tools such as
digital mapping systems help identify optimal CCP locations, while digital casualty tracking systems provide
real-time updates on patient movement and resource allocation. Additionally, interoperability with allied
forces and host-nation assets strengthens coordination during joint operations, ensuring seamless integration
of resources and support.
B-41. A clear and actionable MASCAL plan is critical to improving response times and saving lives during
MASCAL events. By focusing on comprehensive planning, customization, and regular practice, units can
maintain readiness and adapt to the dynamic demands of modern operational environments.
B-42.
**102**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**

### Appendix C

## Casualty Evacuation Capabilities

While MEDEVAC remains the preferred method of evacuation, the realities of large-scale
combat operations will often make CASEVAC the primary means of moving casualties. In
such environments, nonmedical personnel conduct most evacuations using platforms not
originally designed for medical missions. The ability of MEDEVAC to land at the POI for a
single patient are unlikely during large-scale combat operations, where commanders plan to
employ available assets in bulk and under threat.
The integration of these CASEVAC platforms into the Army’s evacuation system supports
operations at echelon by sustaining casualty movement when MEDEVAC is limited or delayed.
This approach enhances operational flexibility while meeting the demands of diverse and
contested environments. Effective use of these assets requires planning for CASEVAC routes,
resource allocation, and logistical sustainability, as well as rigorous training and coordination.
By treating CASEVAC as a mission-critical supporting task, commanders can preserve combat
power and maintain continuity of care even when MEDEVAC is not available.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION VEHICLE STANDARDIZATION AND

## DESIGN CONSTRAINTS

C-1. The lack of standardized CASEVAC platforms across Army formations remains a critical operational
challenge. Most Army platforms do not have dedicated litter configurations, forcing units to retrofit
nonmedical vehicles without a formal design requirement. This limitation has been evident in past conflicts,
where ad hoc modifications led to delays, unsafe casualty transport, and degraded medical care during
movement.
C-2. To mitigate these challenges, commanders assess available platforms and pre-designate vehicles capable
of supporting CASEVAC. Ideally, a CASEVAC-capable vehicle should be able to secure standard NATO
litters, provide basic environmental protection, and integrate with other evacuation assets. Commanders
should advocate for integrating CASEVAC considerations into future Army vehicle acquisition programs,
ensuring platforms can accommodate evacuation requirements without impeding combat operations.

## AIR CASUALTY EVACUATION CAPABILITIES

C-3. CASEVAC aircraft add flexibility and scalability to evacuation operations, especially in complex
environments where traditional platforms face limitations. These aircraft allow the Army to reach casualties
in remote, contested, or logistically restricted areas. Their use, however, requires detailed planning,
coordination, and adaptation to meet mission requirements and provide adequate care during transport.
C-4. U.S. Army aviation assets play a critical role in CASEVAC operations by offering rapid and reliable
casualty movement. While they may be affected by weather, terrain, or airspace restrictions, their adaptability
and range of capabilities make them essential to modern evacuation planning. Through deliberate planning
and training, units can make the most of these assets and improve their ability to save lives in challenging
environments.

### AVIATIONASSETSOVERVIEW

C-5. CASEVAC air assets, including fixed-wing aircraft and rotary-wing aircraft, significantly enhance
CASEVAC capabilities by enabling rapid evacuation over long distances. These platforms, though not
MEDEVAC aircraft, can be configured to transport multiple casualties. They offer extended range, allowing
them to bridge the gap between forward operating areas, and higher role of care MTFs. Additionally, their
speed and ability to bypass ground obstacles make them invaluable in scenarios requiring swift medical
response.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**103**
**Appendix C**
C-6. Despite their advantages, all CASEVAC air assets face inherent constraints. They often lack the
specialized medical equipment and personnel found on MEDEVAC platforms, requiring modifications to
accommodate medical care during transport. Additionally, their reliance on established airfields or suitable
LZs not only limits their operational reach but also their ability to access casualties in restrictive terrain,
dense urban areas, or contested battlefields. Weather conditions and airspace restrictions may further hinder
their deployment, necessitating contingency planning and coordination with other assets.

### CAPABILITIES

C-7. U.S. Army aviation assets provide a wide range of CASEVAC capabilities, including medium-lift utility
helicopters and heavy-lift cargo helicopters. These platforms are not purpose-built for MEDEVAC but can
be configured to move casualties based on mission requirements and aircraft availability.
C-8. Utility helicopters, such as the UH-60 Black Hawk, offer CASEVAC support for small-unit operations.
Depending on configuration, the Black Hawk can carry up to four litter patients or eleven ambulatory
casualties, making it a flexible option for point-of-injury evacuation and short-range medical movement.
C-9. Cargo helicopters, such as the CH-47 Chinook, provide greater capacity for mass CASEVAC. The
CH-47’s CASEVAC capacity varies significantly depending on its internal cargo load and configuration:
•
Not configured with litter support equipment: The CH-47F can accommodate up to eight litter
casualties placed directly on the aircraft floor, assuming minimal other cargo.
•
Configured with 24-litter support equipment:

The aircraft can carry up to 24 litter patients, but this setup requires a specialized litter kit
(formerly LIN M62062), which was removed from the Basis of Issue Plan in 2011 and is no
longer centrally procured.

Units may still order the kit components individually through the supply system if assigned a
24-litter mission. Refer to ATP 4-02.13 and TM 1-1520-240-23&P for full configuration
options.
C-10. For long-range evacuation, the U.S. Army relies on joint capabilities, including U.S. Air Force fixed-
wing aircraft such as the C-130 Hercules. These platforms support the transport of large numbers of casualties
over extended distances and provide essential reach for strategic missions. Table C-1 on page 105 highlights
key aircraft types and their characteristics. For loading techniques and best practices, refer to ATP 4-02.13.
