**Note 1****.** The listed litter and ambulatory capacities for all rotary-wing aircraft are not combined totals. Each aircraft can
carry either litter casualties, ambulatory casualties or a combination of, this is considered carefully during planning.
**Note 2.**The listed litter and ambulatory capacities are based on typical mission configurations without specialized litter
kits unless otherwise specified.
**Note 3.**The CH-47F is structurally capable of supporting up to 24 litter casualties when fully outfitted with the 24-litter
kit, though most units do not possess or routinely configure to this level. Individual components remain available in the
supply system and may be ordered to meet mission-specific requirements. For planning purposes, refer to
ATP 4-02.13 and applicable TMs for configuration options.

### ONBOARDMEDICALEQUIPMENTADAPTABILITY

C-11. While MEDEVAC platforms are equipped with comprehensive medical systems, non-MEDEVAC
Army aviation assets can be adapted to support CASEVAC operations. Modular systems such as litter racks,
oxygen delivery units, and medical monitoring equipment can be installed in rotary-wing and fixed-wing
aircraft to enhance casualty care during transit. However, reconfiguring these platforms requires time, proper
equipment, and trained personnel, which may limit their immediate availability for evacuation missions.
Despite these challenges, their adaptability provides an additional means of CASEVAC when task-organized
MEDEVAC assets are unavailable.

### OPERATIONALRANGE ANDDEPLOYMENTFLEXIBILITY

C-12. Army aviation assets offer wide operational ranges that increase their flexibility in evacuation
missions. Rotary-wing aircraft like the UH-60 can operate within a 122-nautical-mile radius without
refueling, while fixed-wing aircraft can cover much longer distances. This capability supports the rapid
movement of casualties from forward operating bases to higher-echelon medical facilities, even when those
facilities are far away. Vertical takeoff and landing features allow rotary-wing aircraft to reach remote or
restricted areas where other platforms cannot land.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**105**
**Appendix C**

### CONSTRAINTS

C-13. Adverse weather conditions, including low visibility, high winds, and heavy precipitation, pose
significant challenges to aviation-based CASEVAC operations. Rotary-wing aircraft are sensitive to weather
constraints that may limit their ability to take off, navigate, or land safely. Extreme cold or heat can also
impact engine performance and payload capacity, reducing the efficiency of CASEVAC missions in harsh
climates. Additionally, high-altitude environments present significant operational limitations. As elevation
increases, reduced air density diminishes engine power, lift, and overall aircraft performance, restricting
payload capacity and requiring extended takeoff and landing distances.
C-14. Fixed-wing aircraft require developed airfields with runways long enough for safe takeoff and landing,
which can limit their use in austere environments. Rotary-wing aircraft offer greater flexibility but still need
flat, clear LZs for safe operations. High-altitude terrain creates additional challenges by reducing lift and
maneuverability, which can make certain mountainous areas inaccessible to some aircraft. In combat zones
or dense urban settings, finding secure and suitable LZs is difficult, especially when adversaries target these
sites to disrupt evacuation efforts.

### EXAMPLEUSECASE

C-15. Army aviation assets excel in evacuating casualties from remote or austere locations where ground
transportation is impractical or impossible. For instance, in mountainous terrains, rotary-wing aircraft can
access otherwise unreachable areas, extract casualties, and transport them to field hospitals. In one notable
example, during Operation Enduring Freedom, rotary-wing aircraft were used extensively to evacuate
casualties from remote outposts in Afghanistan, overcoming challenges posed by rugged terrain and hostile
threats.
C-16. During large-scale combat operations, Army aviation assets provide critical CASEVAC capabilities
by bridging the gap between forward MTFs and higher-echelon care facilities. Rotary-wing platforms offer
rapid casualty movement under fire, while fixed-wing aircraft like the C-130 support extended-range
evacuation from theater-level MTFs to continental U.S. hospitals. However, commanders balance the
urgency of evacuation with the operational risk of employing aviation assets in contested environments.
Deploying airframes into close areas for CASEVAC is weighed against their availability for other critical
missions. A risk-to-force versus risk-to-mission analysis should guide decisions on when and how to employ
air assets for CASEVAC, ensuring protection of critical capabilities and alignment with broader operational
priorities. This approach emphasizes prioritizing the protection and employment of critical assets based on
mission impact, availability, and operational need.

## GROUND CASUALTY EVACUATION CAPABILITIES

C-17. Ground-based platforms, such as trains, buses, tracked vehicles, trucks and utility vehicles, provide
essential CASEVAC capabilities in operations where air or maritime options are unavailable or impractical.
Trains were adapted as mobile medical wards, offering continuous care during long transits. In modern
operations, trains remain relevant for large-scale casualty movement across secure lines of communication,
particularly in regions with robust rail infrastructure. For example, rail systems in Europe have been
integrated into evacuation plans to manage high casualty volumes efficiently.
C-18. Trucks and utility vehicles are frequently employed for CASEVAC in both combat and non-combat
environments. These platforms can be modified with litters, medical supplies, and communications systems
to support immediate casualty care during evacuation. Their adaptability allows them to navigate diverse
terrains, such as rugged mountain roads or urban environments. However, the effectiveness of trucks and
utility vehicles depends on road conditions and off-road capabilities. In areas with poor infrastructure, tracked
vehicles provide a reliable alternative, ensuring CASEVAC even in challenging conditions.
C-19. Ground CASEVAC operations face unique challenges, including vulnerability to ambushes and
roadblocks in contested environments. Commanders should balance the urgency of evacuation with the need
for security, often deploying armored vehicles or escorts to protect CASEVAC convoys. Advances in
platform technology, such as mine-resistant ambush-protected vehicles, enhance the safety of ground-based
evacuations by reducing the threat from explosive hazards, and small arms fire.
**106**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation Capabilities**
C-20. Integrating ground vehicles with air and maritime CASEVAC platforms supports a smooth and
effective evacuation process. Trucks can move casualties from forward positions to staging areas, where
helicopters or watercraft carry the evacuation forward to higher-role medical facilities. This multimodal
approach helps units use available resources efficiently and maintain continuous care in a variety of
operational environments. By applying the strengths of ground vehicles, units can sustain CASEVAC
capabilities under a wide range of conditions.

### TRAINS FORCASUALTYEVACUATION

C-21. Historically, rail systems have been a critical means of transporting casualties during large-scale
conflicts. During World War I and World War II, railways were extensively used to evacuate WII Soldiers
from the front lines to rear-area MTF. Eventually, specialized hospital trains were developed, outfitted with
medical staff, surgical equipment, and recovery areas to provide continuous care during transit. These trains
allowed for the mass movement of casualties, reducing the burden on local MTF, and freeing up other
evacuation assets for shorter-range missions. The use of rail demonstrated its capacity to sustain operations
across vast theaters of war, offering an organized and efficient means of casualty transport.
C-22. In modern contexts, rail-based CASEVAC remains relevant, particularly during large-scale operations
or areas with robust railway infrastructure. For example, during conflicts in Europe, rail systems have been
integrated into evacuation plans to address the challenges posed by high casualty volumes and logistical
constraints. Modern rail systems offer the advantage of speed, capacity, and the ability to operate in adverse
weather conditions, which can limit air, and ground-based evacuation methods. ATP 4-02.2 highlights the
importance of using all available transportation assets, including rail, to maximize the efficiency of
CASEVAC operations.
**Adaptation of Rail Cars for Medical Purposes**
C-23. The adaptation of railcars for MEDEVAC has evolved through lessons learned in previous large-scale
wars. During World War I and World War II, the U.S. Army recognized the logistical difficulty of shipping
fully outfitted hospital trains overseas due to competition for transport space with combat forces, ammunition,
and supplies. As a result, commanders often relied on intratheater resources, using existing railcars and
locomotives encountered in theater to create ambulance trains. These converted trains included cots, basic
medical supplies, and space for medical personnel, with later versions incorporating surgical areas and critical
care capabilities. In the European Theater of Operations, officers like LTC Sidney Bingham led efforts to
retrofit rolling stock in Italy, England, France, and Germany, demonstrating how local adaptation enabled
timely evacuation under resource-constrained conditions. These historical efforts underscore the enduring
value of rail-based CASEVAC and its relevance in modern large-scale combat operation environments where
infrastructure exists, but deployment timelines are constrained.
C-24. Rail-based CASEVAC remains a relevant option in modern large-scale operations, especially in
regions with developed railway infrastructure. In recent European conflicts, military planners have integrated
rail systems into evacuation plans to manage high casualty volumes and overcome logistical challenges.
Modern rail platforms offer significant advantages, including high capacity, reliable speed, and the ability to
operate in adverse weather, conditions that may limit air or ground evacuation. As noted in ATP 4-02.2,
commanders should leverage all available transportation assets, including rail, to improve the reach, speed,
and efficiency of CASEVAC operations.
**Advantages During Large-Scale Combat Operations**
C-25. In large-scale combat operations, rail offers distinct advantages like a CASEVAC platform, especially
in MASCAL scenarios. Large-scale combat increases the likelihood of mass casualties, which places greater
demands on HSS, mortuary affairs, and replacement operations. Rail systems can transport large numbers of
casualties at once, making them particularly useful when traditional evacuation methods are overwhelmed.
Unlike road or air transport, rail is less affected by terrain, weather, or fuel logistics and can operate
continuously across long distances. These characteristics make it a reliable and efficient option for sustaining
large-scale combat operations while preserving medical capacity and improving casualty survival.
C-26. Additionally, rail-based CASEVAC reduces the operational strain on air and ground evacuation assets.
By leveraging existing rail networks, commanders can allocate limited air and ground transport resources to
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**107**
**Appendix C**
more immediate evacuation needs near the front lines. This multi-modal approach is consistent with the
principles of integrated logistics and evacuation planning, which stresses the importance of optimizing all
available resources to maintain operational tempo, and casualty survivability.

### BUSES FORCASUALTYEVACUATION

C-27. Buses have been modified in the past to move WII U.S. Service members. This capability no longer
remains in the Army inventory, but buses are in most cities and can provide an additional CASEVAC
capability.
C-28. Buses are a good CASEVAC platform for intermediate distances (less than 250 miles) and can easily
move ambulatory patients. Movement of litter patients may be limited to the floor or other methods depending
on the configuration.
C-29. There are several different bus designs and capabilities. Most buses can move approximately
38 ambulatory or 18 litter patients. These numbers vary to some degree depending on model and type of bus.
Differences include the following:
•
An intercity bus has a latrine but only a front door and can hold up to 40 ambulatory casualties.
•
A suburban bus has only front doors but lacks the luggage space and latrines of the intercity bus.
The capacity is similar.
•
A transit bus is a bus with front and center doors with a rear mounted engine. These buses can hold
30 to 42 ambulatory casualties depending on platform type.
•
An articulated bus consists of a rear section connected to the main body by an accordion like joint.
This bus can hold up to 60 ambulatory casualties.
•
A minibus is manufactured by several companies where a specially made body is placed on a truck
chassis. They have walk-in and front doors. The minibus varies by design and configuration with
capacity ranges of 16 to 28 ambulatory casualties.
•
A school bus capacity varies by manufacturer and type. A conventional U.S. school bus has 13 rows
of seats on each side which can support up to 52 ambulatory casualties.

### TRACKEDVEHICLES, TRUCKS,ANDUTILITYVEHICLES

C-30. Tracked vehicles, trucks, and utility vehicles play a critical role in CASEVAC, especially in
environments where traditional MEDEVAC assets are unavailable or impractical. These ground vehicles,
ranging from military cargo trucks to locally sourced civilian vehicles, offer immediate solutions for
transporting WII personnel from the battlefield to medical care. With proper customization, such as adding
stretchers and medical supplies, these vehicles can be transformed into effective transport platforms for
stabilizing and moving casualties. However, challenges such as difficult terrain and limited protection against
enemy attacks highlight the need for planning and risk assessment. By understanding their capabilities and
limitations, military forces can integrate trucks and utility vehicles into broader CASEVAC operations,
ensuring critical support in both logistics and combat scenarios.
**Types of Tracked Vehicles, Trucks, and Utility Vehicles used for Casualty Evacuation**
C-31. Ground vehicles are often used for CASEVAC in situations where traditional MEDEVAC assets are
unavailable or impractical. These vehicles include armored personnel carriers, military cargo trucks, tactical
utility vehicles (such as HMMWVs), and even commercial or locally sourced vehicles adapted for casualty
transport. For example, in austere or resource-limited environments, pickup trucks or civilian sport utility
vehicles may be commandeered and repurposed for moving casualties. These vehicles provide immediate
solutions to transport WII personnel from the POI to initial medical care.
C-32. Vehicles are especially valuable in unconventional operations or in areas lacking established
evacuation infrastructure. ATP 4-02.2 emphasizes the importance of flexibility in selecting evacuation
platforms and notes that vehicles are integrated into broader CASEVAC planning. Commanders assess the
availability, capacity, and suitability of vehicles to confirm they support evacuation efforts.
C-33. Platform types available for CASEVAC operations will vary based on the supported unit's composition
and mission requirements. However, certain platforms are commonly employed due to their availability and
versatility. These platforms range from light to heavy transport platforms and include both wheeled and

tracked assets, ensuring adaptability across different operational environments. Table C-2 highlights key

**Customization of Platforms for Medical Use**
C-34. Customizing platforms for medical use can greatly improve their effectiveness in CASEVAC
operations. Modifications often include stretchers, stabilization equipment, and basic medical supplies such
as tourniquets, intravenous kits, and oxygen. Some platforms are also equipped with communication systems
to support coordination with higher medical echelons or surgical teams. These adjustments help make
CASEVAC more responsive and organized in high-pressure environments.
C-35. More advanced customizations involve modular kits that can be rapidly installed or removed, turning
a standard cargo truck into a mobile medical transport unit. This approach is particularly useful for adapting
to diverse operational environments.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**109**
**Appendix C**
**Limitations of Terrain and Platform Protection**
C-36. One major challenge of using trucks and utility vehicles for CASEVAC is navigating difficult terrain.
In mountainous, forested, or urban environments, these platforms may struggle to reach casualties or maintain
speed during transit. Additionally, the lack of standardized medical equipment and personnel onboard
platforms can delay the delivery of care, reducing their effectiveness compared to MEDEVAC platforms.
C-37. Another critical limitation is the vulnerability of these platforms to enemy attacks. Platforms often lack
adequate armor or defensive capabilities, making them susceptible to small arms fire, explosive hazards, or
indirect fire. Commanders weigh the risks of using platforms against the urgency of evacuating casualties,
emphasizing the need to incorporate route security, convoy protection, and thorough risk assessment into
CASEVAC planning to mitigate these vulnerabilities.
**Examples of Application**
C-38. In combat operations, trucks and utility vehicles are essential for CASEVAC when task-organized
MEDEVAC platforms cannot operate due to enemy activity, weather conditions, or terrain. For example,
during the Vietnam War, trucks and jeeps were commonly used to evacuate casualties from forward positions
to rear-area aid stations when rotary-wing aircraft were unavailable. These platforms served as a lifeline,
especially in dense jungle terrain where air evacuation was not feasible.
C-39. In modern combat scenarios, vehicles like the mine-resistant ambush protected vehicle and joint light
tactical vehicle are utilized for CASEVAC due to their improved protection against threats like small arms
fire and explosive hazards. Customization with litters and medical supplies allows these platforms to function
as interim solutions for CASEVAC. The success of CASEVAC relies on pre-planning, ensuring platforms
are ready for dual-use roles, and rehearsing CASEVAC procedures to minimize delays under fire.
C-40. These examples demonstrate the practical application of trucks and utility vehicles in CASEVAC
during both logistics and combat operations. Their adaptability and availability make them indispensable
assets, particularly in environments where traditional evacuation methods face constraints. Military doctrine
underscores the importance of integrating these platforms into overall evacuation planning to enhance their
effectiveness.

## MARITIME CASUALTY EVACUATION CAPABILITIES

C-41. Army watercraft are an essential component of CASEVAC operations in littoral and riverine
environments, where other modes of transport may be impractical. Maritime platforms, including landing
craft and transport vessels, can evacuate large numbers of casualties while providing space for medical
personnel and equipment. These vessels are particularly effective in areas with navigable waterways, offering
a secure and efficient means of transport for WII personnel.
C-42. However, maritime CASEVAC faces challenges such as limited operational speed and dependence on
navigable water routes. In high-threat areas, securing waterways and maintaining situational awareness are
critical to minimizing risks. Additionally, the logistical complexity of transferring casualties from watercraft
to other MTF requires detailed planning and coordination. Despite these limitations, maritime platforms
remain vital for operations in areas where land and air access are constrained.

### WATERCRAFTOVERVIEW

C-43. The U.S. Army operates a variety of watercraft that can be adapted for CASEVAC in specific
environments. These include landing craft, such as the Landing Craft Utility, and transport vessels, such as
the Logistics Support Vessel. While these platforms are primarily designed for transporting troops, vehicles,
and supplies, they can also be modified to evacuate casualties when needed. For example, during amphibious
operations or missions in riverine areas, these vessels can transport WII personnel from the POI to MTF, or
larger ships equipped with advanced care capabilities. ATP 4-15 highlights the versatility of these vessels in
logistical and operational support, including their potential use in CASEVAC missions.
**110**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation Capabilities**

### CAPABILITIES

C-44. Army watercrafts provide significant advantages in CASEVAC operations due to their ability to carry
large numbers of casualties over long distances. Platforms like the Logistics Support Vessel can
accommodate dozens of patients, including those on litters, making them particularly valuable during
MASCAL events or large-scale operations. Additionally, these vessels are equipped for sustained operations,
enabling them to move casualties across vast waterways, or open seas to reach definitive care.
C-45. Watercrafts are particularly effective in littoral (coastal) and riverine environments, where traditional
land or air CASEVAC methods may be hindered. In river-heavy regions like Southeast Asia, watercraft have
historically been used to navigate through dense jungles and areas without reliable road access. For example,
during the Vietnam War, riverine boats served as mobile platforms for CASEVAC and treatment. ATP 4-15
emphasizes that the ability to operate in these challenging environments makes watercraft an essential
component of CASEVAC planning in areas where waterways are the primary means of transport.

### CONSTRAINTS

C-46. Watercraft serves as a valuable but often challenging platform for CASEVAC in maritime and littoral
operations. While they offer an alternative means of transporting casualties, particularly in riverine, coastal,
and amphibious environments, they come with significant constraints that can impact the speed, safety, and
effectiveness of evacuation efforts. These limitations include dependency on navigable waterways,
vulnerability to enemy threats, slow movement, lack of onboard medical facilities, and difficulties in
transferring casualties to higher echelons of care. Commanders assess operational risks, environmental
conditions, and security threats when integrating watercraft into CASEVAC planning. Without proper
mitigation strategies, reliance on waterborne evacuation could delay medical treatment, increase patient
mortality rates, and complicate overall mission effectiveness. The following sections outline the key
constraints of using watercraft for CASEVAC and highlight considerations for commanders in planning and
executing evacuations in maritime environments.

### LIMITATIONS OFWATERCRAFT FORCASUALTYEVACUATION

C-47. One major limitation of using watercraft for CASEVAC is their reliance on navigable waterways. In
areas where rivers, coastal routes, or inland water bodies are inaccessible or contested, watercraft cannot
support evacuation operations. This dependence means that commanders assess water route availability,
seasonal changes (such as flooding or drought), and infrastructure conditions when planning CASEVAC
operations. ATP 4-02.2 emphasizes evaluating terrain and infrastructure to determine whether watercraft can
support CASEVAC in each operational environment.
**Vulnerability to Enemy Threats and Delayed Response Times**
C-48. One significant challenge in using watercraft for CASEVAC is the potential for delayed response times
in high-threat zones. Watercraft typically move slower than air or ground platforms, making them more
vulnerable to enemy threats such as mines, small arms fire, or missile attacks. They also have limited
maneuverability in narrow waterways or open seas, which restricts their ability to evade hostile actions.
Additional vulnerabilities include:
•
Security Risks. In contested environments, watercraft may require naval escorts, air cover, or
onboard defensive systems to protect personnel and equipment during CASEVAC missions.
•
Limited Operation Speed. Compared to helicopters or ground vehicles, watercraft travel much
slower, which can delay the delivery of casualties to higher roles of care.
•
Environmental Factors. Weather conditions, tidal changes, and high winds can further slow
movement, reduce navigability, and disrupt CASEVAC effectiveness.
C-49. This need for enhanced protection and environmental considerations can slow operations and reduce
the overall responsiveness of the evacuation effort. Commanders weigh these operational risks when
determining the suitability of waterborne CASEVAC platforms, especially in contested or denied
environments.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**111**
**Appendix C**
**Medical Care and Patient Welfare Concerns**
C-50. A significant limitation of using watercraft for CASEVAC is the lack of medical infrastructure and
basic patient care accommodations during transport. Unlike ground-based ambulances or rotary-wing
aircraft, many CASEVAC-capable watercraft are not designed to sustain medical treatment for extended
periods. Key concerns include:
•
Limited or No Onboard Medical Facilities: Most small-to-medium CASEVAC watercraft lack
medical bays, power for medical equipment, or climate control, which can worsen patient conditions
during transport.
•
No Sanitation Facilities (Bathrooms, Waste Disposal): Many CASEVAC watercraft do not have
restroom facilities, posing hygiene challenges for prolonged evacuations, especially for critically
WII patients.
•
Open-Air Exposure: Many watercraft used for CASEVAC do not have enclosed compartments,
leaving casualties vulnerable to environmental exposure (such as rain, wind, cold, heat, sea spray),
which can complicate medical care and increase risks of hypothermia, dehydration, or infection.
•
Rough Seas and Patient Stability: Watercraft movement can be highly unstable in rough seas, strong
currents, or high winds, making it difficult to administer intravenous fluids, conduct medical
procedures, or stabilize casualties on litters.
•
Extended Transport Times: Due to the slow speed of watercraft, casualties may be in transit for an
extended period, requiring additional pain management, intravenous fluid administration, and
continuous monitoring, capabilities that many small watercraft lack.
**Challenges in Transferring Casualties from Watercraft to Higher Roles of Care**
C-51. Watercraft used for CASEVAC operations generally lack the capability to support rotary-wing aircraft
landings. Unlike dedicated hospital ships or vessels with flight decks, smaller CASEVAC watercraft require
casualty transfers via hoist when interfacing with rotary-wing aircraft. This constraint complicates patient
movement, particularly in:
•
Adverse Weather Conditions: High winds, rough seas, and unstable platforms increase the difficulty
of hoist operations.
•
High-Threat Environments: Enemy interference or contested waters may make hovering aircraft
vulnerable to attack during patient transfers.
•
Limited Shore-Based Transfer Points: CASEVAC planning considers pre-identified shoreline
transfer zones, where casualties can be moved from watercraft to land-based medical transport
safely and efficiently.

### USE INJOINT ANDMULTINATIONALOPERATIONS

C-52. During joint and multinational operations, Army watercraft often operate alongside Navy and coalition
maritime assets to expand CASEVAC capabilities. Working with the Navy allows for the transfer of
casualties from Army platforms to dedicated medical ships like the U.S. Naval Ship Comfort and U.S. Naval
Ship Mercy. These ships are equipped to deliver advanced medical care at sea. In multinational missions,
coalition partners may also contribute watercraft or medical ships. To support effective CASEVAC across
these forces, units prioritize interoperability, shared procedures, and clear communication. JP 4-02 highlights
the importance of integrated planning in joint environments to align evacuation efforts and reduce delays.
C-53. Partnering with naval and coalition forces helps address the limits of Army watercraft. Navy units can
provide escort or air support to protect CASEVAC missions operating in contested waters. Coalition ships
with medical facilities offer treatment options closer to the POI, reducing the need for extended transport to
shore. These capabilities give commanders more options when planning CASEVAC and allow for flexible
responses under complex maritime conditions. When integrated early and rehearsed routinely, joint
CASEVAC planning strengthens the overall operation by improving casualty care and preserving combat
power at sea.

### TYPES OFUNITEDSTATESARMYWATERCRAFT

C-54. U.S. Army watercraft are often employed for CASEVAC operations in scenarios where traditional air
or ground evacuation assets are unavailable or impractical. These vessels include logistics support vessels,
**112**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation Capabilities**
landing craft utility, landing craft mechanized, and other modular platforms such as causeway ferries. for
example, in littoral environments or during amphibious operations, landing craft utility can transport
casualties from shoreline positions to medical ships or higher-echelon care facilities. The adaptability of
watercraft allows commanders to leverage them for casualty movement in regions where terrain,
infrastructure, or enemy actions restrict other evacuation methods.
C-55. The versatility of watercraft makes them invaluable for operations in coastal, riverine, or amphibious
environments. These vessels provide critical capabilities for MASCAL evacuation during large-scale
operations or when extensive transport capacities are required. ATP 4-15 highlights the flexibility of Army
watercraft and their integration into broader CASEVAC and logistical planning. Commanders evaluate the
suitability of these platforms based on mission-specific requirements, such as the availability of navigable
waterways, the distance to MTF, and the level of medical care needed during transport.
C-56. Watercraft utilized for CASEVAC operations vary in size and capability, ensuring they can support
diverse mission profiles. For example, logistics support vessels can carry large numbers of casualties over
extended distances, making them ideal for MASCAL scenarios. Smaller vessels, such as landing craft
mechanized and rigid hull inflatable boats, offer agility and access to challenging locations, such as
riverbanks or densely forested coastlines. Modular systems, such as the roll-on/roll-off discharge facility,
provide additional flexibility for casualty transport. The selection of watercraft for CASEVAC depends on
operational requirements, available resources, and the urgency of the situation. Table C-3 on page 114
highlights key watercraft types and their characteristics. For loading techniques and best practices, refer to
ATP 4-15 and ATP 4-02.13.

**Note 1**. **Casualty Carrying Capacity:** Capacities are estimates based on available deck space and configuration;
actual numbers may vary depending on mission requirements and equipment loads. While a vessel may be able to
physically accommodate a high number of casualties (such as 300 litters), this does not account for the practical
limitations of medical care, sanitation, and sustainment. Factors such as limited onboard medical personnel, lack of
bathrooms, food, and prolonged transport times significantly impact the feasibility of mass casualty evacuations via
watercraft.
**Note 2.** **Planning Speed and Range:** Ranges assume standard operational loads and weather conditions; adverse
conditions may reduce these values. CASEVAC operations accounts for sea state, current, and fuel consumption,
which may limit a vessel’s ability to sustain prolonged evacuations.
**Note 3.** **Specialized Equipment:** Larger vessels (such as the LSV, LCU) can be equipped with additional medical or
decontamination facilities for enhanced CASEVAC capabilities. However, even with modifications, most vessels lack
the ability to provide sustained medical treatment, patient hygiene facilities, or climate-controlled environments, making
timely transfer to higher echelons of care critical for casualty survival.

## INTEGRATED CASUALTY EVACUATION SYSTEMS

C-57. Commanders integrate air, ground, and maritime CASEVAC assets into a single, flexible system that
supports operations across multiple environments. Each platform brings different advantages to the
evacuation mission. Rotary-and fixed-wing aircraft move casualties from the POI to a secure location.
Ground vehicles or watercraft then continue the movement to a higher role of care MTF. This tiered,
multimodal approach allows commanders to match platform capabilities to terrain, threat, and urgency. By
coordinating across echelons, units reduce delays in casualty movement and maintain medical support across
the depth of the battlefield.
C-58. Integrating these systems requires robust coordination to overcome challenges in communication and
synchronization. Seamless communication is critical for ensuring that assets are deployed efficiently,
particularly in environments where multiple platforms work together under tight timelines.
Miscommunication can result in delays or gaps in care, during transitions between modes of transport.
**114**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation Capabilities**
C-59. Commanders manage medical handoffs between CASEVAC platforms to maintain continuity of care.
Transferring a casualty from one vehicle or aircraft to another requires shared procedures, compatible
equipment, and accurate documentation. Standard formats for patient records and handoff briefings help
medical personnel stay informed and ready to act during transitions. ATP 4-02.2 highlights the value of these
practices in keeping medical support consistent across different transport modes.
C-60. Using a mix of transportation platforms, such as planes, trains, trucks, and boats, builds a flexible
CASEVAC system that supports large-scale or complex operations. For example, in a European theater, a
casualty might move by air to a forward staging point, travel by rail across secure territory, and then board a
watercraft for transport to a rear-area MTF. This type of multimodal CASEVAC helps commanders adapt to
terrain, threats, and available infrastructure. ATP 4-02.13 encourages units to use all available resources to
move casualties.
C-61. Multimodal CASEVAC requires tight coordination across platforms and commands. Units
synchronize movement timelines and communicate clearly to prevent delays. CASEVAC teams need to know
exactly when other platforms will arrive, so transitions happen without confusion. JP 4-02 emphasizes the
importance of joint planning and shared communication systems to link units, Services, and coalition partners
in a common evacuation effort.
C-62. Handoffs between platforms carry the risk of lost information or delayed care. To reduce that risk,
medical teams follow standard procedures for transferring records, gear, and patient details. For instance,
when moving a casualty from an aircraft to a ground vehicle, both teams review the patient’s status and
current treatments. FM 4-02 stresses the need for strong training and reliable handoff practices to keep care
continuous from the POI through final treatment.
C-63. Standardizing medical equipment across CASEVAC platforms makes transitions faster and safer.
When litters, intravenous kits, and monitors work the same way on aircraft, ground vehicles, and watercraft,
teams spend less time switching equipment and more time focused on patient care. Shared systems also make
it easier to train personnel and reduce confusion during emergencies. ATP 4-02.55 supports this approach to
improve readiness and coordination in demanding operations.
C-64. A well-integrated CASEVAC system uses all transportation options, air, ground, maritime, and rail, to
move casualties. Planning, communication, and equipment standardization help reduce delays and avoid gaps
in care. With these elements in place, commanders and medical planners gain the flexibility to adjust to
changing combat conditions and match the right resources to the mission. These combined efforts strengthen
the overall evacuation network and improve casualty outcomes.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION PLANNING AND OPERATIONAL

## CONSIDERATIONS

C-65. Planning for CASEVAC begins with establishing clear, flexible routes that connect air, ground, and
maritime platforms. These routes match the terrain, anticipate enemy threats, and support movement to
higher-level medical care. Planners identify CASEVAC corridors to protect movements and reduce delays.
For example, air assets might evacuate casualties from the POI to a transfer site, where ground vehicles then
move them to a medical treatment facility. This layered approach improves efficiency during fast-moving
operations. ATP 4-02.2 stresses the importance of route planning that blends all available transportation
methods into a cohesive CASEVAC network.
C-66. Commanders and planners focus on allocating CASEVAC resources to match casualty needs. During
MASCAL events, they may assign large platforms such as trucks or boats to move multiple casualties, while
using helicopters for critical or time-sensitive cases. This balance allows units to manage both urgent
evacuations and sustained movement of wounded personnel. By matching resources to mission demands,
planners preserve combat power and maintain the flow of care across the operational area.
C-67. Sustaining vehicles during CASEVAC operations hinges on logistical planning. Refueling and
maintenance are essential for ensuring platform availability and reliability, particularly in prolonged or high-
tempo missions. For example, tactical utility vehicles repurposed for CASEVAC require frequent fuel
resupply and inspections to prevent breakdowns. FM 4-02 highlights the importance of maintaining logistical
support to sustain evacuation capabilities under all conditions.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**115**
**Appendix C**
C-68. Managing load capacities is equally important. Overloading platforms can compromise safety, delay
operations, or reduce the effectiveness of medical care during transit. Commanders should balance the
urgency of evacuation with the limitations of each platform, ensuring that casualties are safely transported
without exceeding operational capacities. SOPs for load management, combined with real-time assessments
during missions, help mitigate these risks.
C-69. Training personnel to operate and configure platforms for CASEVAC is vital to mission success.
Soldiers are proficient in adapting platforms for medical transport, such as securing stretchers, managing
patient care en route, and using basic medical equipment. Regular drills and simulations of CASEVAC
scenarios enhance readiness and coordination, ensuring that teams can respond under real-world conditions.
ATP 4-02.55 stresses the importance of hands-on training to develop operational competence.
C-70. Personnel understand the strengths and limits of different CASEVAC platforms, including trucks,
watercraft, aircraft, and trains. This knowledge helps teams choose the right asset for each mission and adjust
when conditions change. Commanders incorporate CASEVAC into larger training exercises to test the full
evacuation process under realistic conditions. These integrated rehearsals build confidence across the force
and prepare units to respond during actual operations.
C-71. By integrating detailed medical planning, robust logistical support, and comprehensive training,
commanders can optimize CASEVAC operations, ensuring rapid and effective CASEVAC in any operational
environment. Well-coordinated evacuation efforts reduce delays, improve patient outcomes, and enhance the
overall efficiency of the AHS.
C-72. CASEVAC platforms such as planes, trains, trucks, and watercraft give commanders flexible options
to support casualty evacuation when MEDEVAC resources are limited or unavailable. These platforms allow
the Army to adapt to different operational environments and maintain medical support across wide areas.
Planners and operators understand how to match each platform’s capabilities and limits to the mission. By
integrating these assets into the overall medical plan, commanders improve response times and provide life-
saving care to WII personnel.

**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**

### Appendix D

## Casualty Evacuation Checklist

The success of CASEVAC operations depends on deliberate planning, coordinated execution,
and ongoing assessment. CASEVAC helps preserve combat power and morale by moving WII
personnel to medical care. The CASEVAC checklist serves as a practical tool for commanders
and staff. It provides a structured way to address each phase of the operation, from initial
planning to after-action reviews. This tool aligns with FM 3-0, FM 4-02, ATP 4-02.2,
and ATP 4-02.13, blending doctrine with hands-on techniques to support readiness across
different missions and environments.
This appendix presents a detailed checklist to guide CASEVAC operations. It starts with
planning and preparation, emphasizing the need to identify available resources, establish CCPs,
and confirming that personnel are trained and ready. The appendix then shifts to operational
tasks, including execution, logistics, and C2. Together, these sections help leaders at every
level manage CASEVAC as a supporting task that sustains operations and contributes directly
to mission success.

## PLANNING AND PREPARATION FOR CASUALTY EVACUATION

D-1. Thoughtful planning and preparation form the foundation of effective CASEVAC operations. Leaders
anticipate challenges and address key details before a mission begins. This includes estimating casualty
numbers, identifying CCPs, and assigning available vehicles. Each step contributes to getting WII personnel
safely off the battlefield. When done well, planning saves lives and helps the unit maintain momentum under
pressure. This section outlines the critical planning elements for CASEVAC. It highlights how proactive
thinking, teamwork, and coordination with higher headquarters help create a reliable and efficient evacuation
process. By focusing on these areas, leaders prepare their teams to act with speed and confidence when
casualties occur.
D-2. At each echelon, specific leaders carry responsibility for planning CASEVAC. The Medical Officer
manages casualty care and medical protocols. The executive officer ensures CASEVAC planning aligns with
the overall mission. The sustainment leader oversees transportation assets and logistical support. By clearly
assigning these roles, units improve coordination and streamline execution during CASEVAC operations.

### DEVELOPINGCASUALTYESTIMATES

D-3. Before each mission, leaders estimate the number and types of casualties they might face. This estimate
helps medical teams and CASEVAC planners prepare the right resources to treat and evacuate the wounded.
Leaders should examine the mission’s goals, likely enemy actions, terrain, and weather to develop a realistic
picture of what to expect. For instance, operations in the mountains may result in more injuries from falls or
cold exposure. In contrast, urban missions often involve more blast or shrapnel injuries due to confined spaces
and complex threats. A solid estimate allows units to tailor their medical support to the mission and
environment.
D-4. The unit commander holds overall responsibility for ensuring that casualty estimates are completed,
with support from the personnel officer, the operations officer, and the senior medical officer. These
personnel are tasked with analyzing past mission data, intelligence reports, and operational risks to refine
predictions and identify potential patterns. Accurate casualty estimates are used to pre-position the right
medical resources, such as supplies for treating burns, fractures, or gunshot wounds, ensuring that casualties
receive the best possible care in a timely manner. Additionally, these estimates guide decisions regarding the
allocation of CASEVAC assets, and the prioritization of evacuation efforts based on injury severity.
D-5. Having a solid casualty estimate also allows leaders to plan for worst-case scenarios, such as MASCAL
events. By anticipating the maximum number of injuries, they can allocate additional resources, identify
backup CASEVAC routes, and prepare contingency plans. The unit commander and staff use the casualty
estimate to coordinate with medical teams and higher headquarters, ensuring sufficient capacity to manage
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**117**
**Appendix D**
high casualty volumes and reduce the risk of delays in evacuating WII personnel, which could otherwise
result in preventable fatalities. To address these scenarios, leaders should work closely with their supporting
sustainment unit and movement control elements to secure additional CASEVAC vehicles and prioritize
route clearance and security. By incorporating external support channels, units can enhance their
responsiveness and maintain operational momentum.
D-6. Leaders plan for worst-case scenarios, including MASCAL events, by working with their sustainment
units and higher headquarters. This coordination helps secure extra CASEVAC vehicles in advance. Having
additional vehicles ready allows the unit to move large numbers of casualties and reduces the chance of
delays that could lead to preventable deaths. By using support from outside the unit, commanders improve
their ability to respond and keep the mission moving forward.

### ESTABLISHINGCASUALTYCOLLECTIONPOINTS

D-7. CCPs are critical locations where WII personnel are gathered, stabilized, prepared for evacuation to
higher roles of care, and where casualties are transloaded between different echelon’s nonmedical vehicles.
Leaders choose these points carefully, considering factors like accessibility, security, and position relative to
the forward line of troops. A well-placed CCP can significantly reduce the time it takes for casualties to
receive treatment, improving their chances of survival.
D-8. Leaders set up CCPs by working closely with medical teams to make sure they have the right supplies
and enough trained personnel. Leaders choose both primary and backup CCP locations in case the situation
on the battlefield changes, such as enemy actions or terrain problems. Once selected, they clearly mark these
points and share that information with all units taking part in the mission to avoid confusion when time and
clarity matter most.
D-9. After setting up the CCPs, units rehearse evacuation drills to practice the entire process. These rehearsals
include how to move casualties to the CCP, who will provide the first medical care, and how to move them
from the CCP to a medical treatment facility. By practicing these steps ahead of time, units can fix problems
early and improve how fast and safely they evacuate casualties during real missions.

### ALLOCATINGVEHICLES ANDMEDICALSUPPLIES

D-10. Proper allocation of vehicles and medical supplies is essential to support CASEVAC operations.
Leaders assign specific vehicles to the task of evacuating casualties, ensuring that both dedicated medical
vehicles and nonmedical vehicles are available. Dedicated medical vehicles, such as ambulances, are
equipped with advanced life-saving equipment, while repurposed combat vehicles can provide flexibility in
emergencies. Additionally, aid and litter teams and vehicle drivers are trained in proper CASEVAC
procedures, including patient loading and unloading, medical intervention techniques, and route navigation.
D-11. Along with assigning vehicles, leaders make sure each one is stocked with the right medical supplies.
These supplies should include bandages, litters, intravenous fluids, and medications needed to treat casualties
during transport. Leaders conduct precombat inspections to check that everything is in place and working.
They plan resupply points along CASEVAC routes, especially for longer missions or when operating in
remote areas. Aid and litter teams, along with drivers, know the locations of CCPs, AXPs, and MTFs. Units
should rehearse the CASEVAC process so that every team member understands their role and follows the
procedures in the unit’s tactical standing operating procedure. These rehearsals should include triage
markings and how to prioritize casualties during evacuation.

### SYNCHRONIZINGPLANS WITHHIGHERECHELONS

D-12. Coordinating with higher echelons is critical for allocating CASEVAC resources. Leaders synchronize
their plans to match the mission requirements, confirm vehicle assignments, and identify any gaps in support
early. When shortfalls are found, they should request reinforcements or additional assets through established
command channels. This coordination ensures that CASEVAC remains a reliable part of the larger medical
and operational plan. By managing resources efficiently and keeping communication open with higher
commands, units can maintain the ability to respond to casualties under changing battlefield conditions.
D-13. To move casualties securely and efficiently, leaders coordinate closely with movement control
elements. These teams help manage traffic flow, deconflict CASEVAC routes, and assign movement
priorities across the battlefield. Movement control personnel track main and alternate supply routes,
**118**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation Checklist**
anticipate delays, and help develop backup plans when threats like enemy activity or route congestion arise.
Leaders keep aid and litter teams, drivers, and CASEVAC personnel informed about route changes, security
threats, and movement priorities. Including these personnel in coordination efforts helps prevent confusion
and delays, keeping CASEVAC operations synchronized and responsive in both stable and contested
environments.

## EXECUTION AND OPERATIONAL CONSIDERATIONS

D-14. The execution of CASEVAC missions requires precise coordination, adaptability, and an unwavering
focus on maintaining the safety and efficiency of operations. Effective communication is the backbone of
these missions, ensuring that all personnel, from medical teams to command staff, work cohesively to
transport casualties swiftly and safely. This section highlights the critical importance of reliable
communication protocols, the readiness and security of CASEVAC vehicles, the alignment of evacuation
efforts with ongoing combat maneuvers, and the ability to make real-time adjustments to plans as situations
evolve on the battlefield.
D-15. Every part of the CASEVAC system contributes to mission success. Leaders establish communication
protocols that provide reliable and clear channels to share information across units. These protocols help
coordinate casualty movement and keep higher headquarters informed of updates.
D-16. Vehicle readiness is equally important. CASEVAC platforms are inspected, maintained, and staged
for rapid use, especially in contested areas where failure can mean lost lives. Integrating CASEVAC into
maneuver plans reduces the chance of friendly interference, keeps evacuation routes open, and supports the
commander’s overall intent. Leaders plan for real-time changes, such as shifting routes or adjusting priority
levels, to keep casualty care moving despite unexpected threats or delays. These coordinated efforts form the
backbone of successful CASEVAC operations and help preserve both combat power and the lives of WII
personnel.

### COMMUNICATIONPROTOCOLS

D-17. Clear and reliable communication supports every CASEVAC mission by connecting medical teams,
command staff, and transportation assets. Leaders assign specific call signs, radio frequencies, and
emergency signals before operations begin. These measures help prevent confusion during high-pressure
situations. Effective communication allows teams to report casualty locations, describe injuries, and request
support. When units speak a common operational language, they can act faster and with greater accuracy.
D-18. Before the mission starts, leaders verify that all communication equipment works correctly. This
includes testing radios, satellite phones, and backup devices. Teams should prepare alternate methods of
communication, such as hand signals or visual markers, in case electronic systems fail. Regular training helps
personnel apply communication protocols under stress and adjust to changing battlefield conditions.
D-19. During execution, communication stays constant, accurate, and clear. CASEVAC teams should track
casualty movement from the POI to the collection point and on to the MTF. These real-time updates allow
leaders to adjust routes, reassign CASEVAC assets, or prioritize support where it is needed most. This
coordination keeps the mission on track and helps casualties receive care without unnecessary delay.

### ENSURINGVEHICLEREADINESS ANDSECURITY

D-20. The readiness and security of CASEVAC vehicles directly affect the success of casualty evacuation
operations. Leaders inspect all designated vehicles before the mission begins. These inspections confirm that
each vehicle is mechanically sound and fully stocked with required medical supplies. Units should check
critical components, including tires, fuel levels, brakes, lights, and communication systems. If any part of a
vehicle is faulty or missing, teams correct the problem right away. Early identification and repair of
deficiencies help prevent delays and protect both personnel and casualties during evacuation.
D-21. Security is another vital aspect of CASEVAC vehicle preparation. In contested environments, vehicles
may be targeted by enemy forces, making it essential to implement protective measures. This could include
assigning armed escort vehicles, using camouflage, or selecting routes that minimize exposure to threats.
Leaders decide whether to mark vehicles exclusively used for CASEVAC with Geneva Convention symbols,
which can signal their non-combatant status but may also draw attention in some scenarios.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**119**
**Appendix D**
D-22. Teams train to respond to emergencies involving CASEVAC vehicles, including mechanical
breakdowns or enemy ambushes. Leaders should develop and rehearse contingency plans that include
alternate routes, backup vehicles, and security measures. These preparations allow evacuation efforts to
continue even when the situation changes unexpectedly. By focusing on vehicle readiness and route security,
commanders protect both the casualties and the personnel conducting the evacuation, while keeping the
mission on track.

### COORDINATION WITHTACTICALOPERATIONS

D-23. Leaders coordinate CASEVAC missions with ongoing combat operations to protect both medical and
tactical elements. Evacuation routes and timings align with the maneuver plan to avoid disrupting combat
tasks or placing CASEVAC teams in danger. For instance, routes should bypass areas with active fighting or
narrow corridors needed by maneuver forces.
D-24. Commanders maintain continuous communication between CASEVAC teams and combat units to stay
updated on battlefield conditions. Information about enemy movement, terrain changes, or shifting lines of
contact helps CASEVAC teams adjust. Leaders should also reduce risk by scheduling evacuations during
lulls in fighting or by designating separate zones for medical and combat activity.
D-25. CASEVAC teams remain flexible and ready to adjust to unexpected changes, such as a surge in
casualties or a shift in enemy actions. By tying CASEVAC operations directly to the unit’s tactical plan,
leaders ensure that medical support remains available without interfering with combat objectives.

### REAL-TIMEADJUSTMENTS TOOPERATIONALPLANS

D-26. No matter how well planned a CASEVAC mission is, unexpected situations can arise that require real-
time adjustments. Leaders are ready to adapt their plans based on the evolving operational environment, such
as changes in enemy tactics, weather conditions, or casualty numbers. Flexibility in decision-making is
crucial to ensuring the continued success of evacuation operations.
D-27. To make these adjustments, leaders rely on continuous situational awareness and communication.
Teams provide regular updates on the status of casualties, routes, and vehicles, allowing commanders to
respond. For example, if a primary route becomes compromised, teams can be redirected to alternate routes
that were identified during the planning phase. Similarly, if an MTF reaches capacity, leaders may need to
coordinate with higher echelons to find additional resources.
D-28. Real-time adjustments require CASEVAC teams to make immediate decisions when needed. Leaders
train personnel in mission command principles and ensure they understand the commander’s intent. When
teams know the mission purpose and priorities, they can act decisively without waiting for direct orders. This
adaptability helps maintain the continuity of operations and ensures casualties continue to receive timely
care, even under rapidly changing conditions.

## SUSTAINMENT IN CASUALTY EVACUATION

D-29. Sustainment plays a critical role in ensuring the success and continuity of CASEVAC operations.
Proper monitoring and replenishment of medical supplies, the ability to handle MASCAL situations, and the
maintenance of CASEVAC vehicles and platforms are essential for supporting WII personnel and preserving
the continuity of operations. Without a strong sustainment plan, CASEVAC efforts risk delays, resource
shortages, and reduced effectiveness, all of which can negatively impact mission success and troop welfare.
D-30. This section explains the logistical and operational sustainment needed to keep CASEVAC systems
working efficiently. Leaders focus on reliable medical supply management, early planning for potential
MASCAL scenarios, and regular maintenance of evacuation vehicles. These steps help ensure that
CASEVAC operations remain dependable, even in harsh combat conditions. By managing these elements,
leaders create a strong support system that protects the health of their personnel and helps the unit meet its
mission goals.

### MONITORING ANDRESUPPLYINGMEDICALSUPPLIES

D-31. Maintaining an adequate supply of medical items is essential to effective CASEVAC operations.
Leaders regularly check inventories of critical items such as bandages, litters, fluids, and medications. These
**120**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation Checklist**
checks help confirm that CASEVAC vehicles and CCPs are ready to support the mission. Before each
operation, leaders should verify that all medical gear is serviceable and matched to the expected casualty
load.
D-32. During operations, units should position resupply points along CASEVAC routes to keep medical
items flowing. These locations allow teams to reload and stay on mission without delay. Leaders work closely
with sustainment units to forecast needs, identify shortfalls, and arrange timely deliveries. Good coordination
and clear communication help prevent medical supply gaps and keep the CASEVAC system running
smoothly.
D-33. After each mission, teams inventory their supplies and refill anything that was used. Quick post-
mission restocking helps teams stay ready for future operations, especially during long deployments or in
remote areas. By maintaining a steady cycle of monitoring, resupply, and coordination, leaders build a
dependable CASEVAC system that protects their personnel and supports the larger fight.

### HANDLINGMASSCASUALTYSITUATIONS

D-34. MASCAL events present unique challenges that require specialized planning and preparation. These
situations often overwhelm standard CASEVAC systems, making it essential for leaders to have contingency
plans in place. This includes identifying additional CCPs, mobilizing extra vehicles, and coordinating with
higher echelons for supplementary medical support. Having a clear triage process is critical to prioritize
casualties based on the severity of their injuries, and the availability of resources.
D-35. During MASCAL scenarios, leaders coordinate with CASEVAC teams and MTFs to avoid
bottlenecks. Tracking casualty movement allows units to send patients to the right locations and balance the
use of medical resources. Preplanned MASCAL protocols, such as backup routes, overflow holding areas,
and extra personnel, help teams stay organized and maintain steady evacuation efforts, even under intense
pressure.
D-36. Training exercises that simulate MASCAL situations are an invaluable tool for preparing units. These
drills allow teams to practice triage, communication, and evacuation procedures, identifying areas for
improvement before a real emergency occurs. By proactively addressing the challenges of MASCAL events,
leaders can enhance their unit’s ability to manage these critical situations, preserving combat power, and
morale.

### MAINTAININGCASUALTYEVACUATIONVEHICLES ANDPLATFORMS

D-37. CASEVAC operations rely on dependable vehicles. Routine maintenance keeps these platforms ready
to perform under the physical and operational demands of combat. Leaders schedule regular checks for
engines, tires, brakes, fuel, and communication systems. They verify that medical equipment onboard each
vehicle is fully functional. Addressing mechanical problems early helps prevent mission delays and protects
both patients and crew during evacuation.
D-38. During extended operations, vehicle resupply and repair become even more important. Leaders should
place maintenance checkpoints along CASEVAC routes to keep vehicles in service without slowing the
evacuation. When missions operate at a high tempo, commanders should position backup vehicles near
support areas. These extra assets help units recover when primary platforms break down or take damage.
D-39. Security is just as important as mechanical reliability. CASEVAC vehicles operating in contested areas
may need added armor or protective equipment to reduce risks from enemy fire. CASEVAC teams respond
to threats, including ambushes and vehicle failures. Training for these emergencies helps units continue
evacuating casualties, even in hostile conditions. By maintaining vehicle readiness and building in flexibility,
leaders keep their CASEVAC systems effective and responsive throughout the mission.

## CONTINUOUS IMPROVEMENT THROUGH THE ASSESSMENT

D-40. Effective CASEVAC operations require more than just planning and execution, they demand
continuous evaluation and adaptation. Leaders conduct AARs, update SOPs, and share best practices across
units to keep CASEVAC processes relevant and responsive. These actions help teams learn from experience,
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**121**
**Appendix D**
improve their methods, and exchange insights that strengthen overall performance. This approach builds a
culture of improvement and collaboration across the force.
D-41. Structured assessments and feedback help improve CASEVAC operations over time. The AAR gives
units a chance to evaluate performance, identify problems, and find solutions. When leaders update SOPs
based on these findings, they build lessons learned into future missions. Sharing effective techniques across
units also strengthens the Army’s overall CASEVAC capability. These combined efforts improve readiness,
protect Soldiers’ health, and support mission success.

### CONDUCTINGAFTERACTIONREVIEWS

D-42. After every CASEVAC operation, conducting an AAR is essential for identifying successes,
challenges, and areas for improvement. The AAR provides a structured way for all participants, from
CASEVAC teams to commanders, to discuss what went well and what could be improved. These reviews
help capture real-time insights while they are still fresh, ensuring valuable feedback is not lost.
D-43. During an AAR, leaders should focus on key aspects of the operation, such as the timeliness of casualty
movement, the effectiveness of communication, and the coordination between units. Specific issues, like
delays in evacuation or shortages of medical supplies, should be thoroughly examined. Input from all
participants, including medical personnel and support staff, is critical to gaining a complete understanding of
the operation.
D-44. The findings from an AAR should be documented in detail, creating a clear record that can be
referenced for future missions. This documentation allows units to track trends over time, ensuring that
recurring issues are addressed, and that successful strategies are consistently applied. A well-conducted AAR
not only improves the unit’s CASEVAC capabilities but also enhances the overall effectiveness of future
operations.

### UPDATINGSTANDARDOPERATINGPROCEDUREBASED ONOPERATIONALFEEDBACK

D-45. SOPs evolve to reflect the realities of the battlefield, and the lessons learned from CASEVAC
missions. Feedback from AAR provides the foundation for these updates, ensuring that procedures remain
relevant and effective. Leaders should prioritize changes that address identified weaknesses, such as unclear
communication protocols, or inefficient evacuation routes.
D-46. Leaders revise SOPs in collaboration with key stakeholders, including medical personnel, logistics
teams, and commanders. This team approach helps ensure that updates are realistic, relevant, and support all
phases of CASEVAC operations. For instance, if an AAR identifies a gap, such as vehicles being unequipped,
the SOP can be changed to include specific pre-mission inspections and required supply checklists. These
updates improve coordination and readiness at every level.
D-47. After making changes to SOPs, units train in the new procedures to ensure shared understanding.
Routine training exercises help reinforce these updates, giving teams the chance to practice under realistic
conditions. This repetition strengthens confidence and builds muscle memory. By regularly refining and
rehearsing SOPs, units maintain a high level of preparedness and can execute CASEVAC tasks in support of
ongoing operations.

### SHARINGLESSONSLEARNEDACROSSUNITS

D-48. Sharing lessons learned is a key component of improving CASEVAC operations across the Army and
other Services. Insights gained from one unit’s experience can benefit others, creating a collective knowledge
base that enhances the force’s overall capability. Leaders should prioritize communication with adjacent
units, higher echelons, and training centers to disseminate lessons learned, and successful strategies.
D-49. Lessons learned can be shared through formal channels, such as reports, briefings, or training events,
as well as informal discussions between units. For example, if a unit develops an innovative approach to
managing MASCAL situations, this technique can be shared with other units facing similar challenges.
Collaborative learning not only improves CASEVAC operations but also fosters a culture of continuous
improvement and mutual support.
**122**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Casualty Evacuation Checklist**
D-50. The Army’s digital platforms, such as knowledge-sharing portals, can also play a vital role in spreading
lessons learned. Units can upload AAR findings, updated SOPs, and other resources to centralized databases,
making them accessible to a wider audience. By sharing these resources across echelons, commanders
improve readiness and ensure CASEVAC operations adapt to meet the demands of changing environments.

## CASUALTY EVACUATION STANDARD OPERATING PROCEDURE

D-51. The CASEVAC checklist (figure D-1 on page 124) gives leaders at all levels a clear and structured
tool to plan, carry out, and review CASEVAC operations. It walks users through every stage of the mission,
from early planning to post-mission assessment, ensuring that units stay ready to respond to battlefield
injuries in any environment. Aligned with FM 3-0, FM 4-02, ATP 4-02.2, and ATP 4-02.13, the checklist
supports mission planning by integrating CASEVAC as a supporting task at echelon. It also helps address
challenges such as MASCAL events, resupply planning, and coordination with higher commands.
D-52. The checklist highlights the need for deliberate planning, solid preparation, and constant evaluation to
protect the health and morale of Soldiers. It guides leaders through essential actions like estimating casualty
numbers, setting up collection points, preparing vehicles, and managing communications. By following the
checklist, units improve their readiness and can better handle the chaos of combat. This tool helps
commanders support operational success by keeping casualty care reliable and timely. More than a task list,
the CASEVAC checklist is an asset for preserving combat power and supporting the force.
**30 May 2026**

### Appendix E

## Multinational Evacuation Request Formats

This appendix actively compares the evacuation request formats used by various countries and
organizations, including the U.S. military 9-Line, ABCANZ Nine-Line format, and NATO
MEDEVAC 9 Liner formats. We examine the similarities and differences between these
formats and identify areas for standardization to improve interoperability and coordination in
multinational operations. By analyzing these formats and their variations, we provide
recommendations for harmonizing evacuation request formats, ultimately enhancing the
effectiveness of evacuation operations, and improving patient care. This appendix serves as a
critical resource for planners, operators, and medical personnel seeking to optimize their
response to evacuation requests in diverse operational environments.
**The following paragraphs are in consonance with**
**ABCANZ Publication 256 and NATO STANAG 2627**

## OVERVIEW OF INTERNATIONAL EVACUATION REQUEST

E-1. In multinational and joint operations, differences in MEDEVAC request formats can create challenges
for effective communication and patient care. The U.S. Army uses the 9-line evacuation request format, while
other countries and organizations, such as ABCANZ and NATO, have their own formats. Understanding
these differences is crucial for maintaining interoperability in multinational operations.
E-2. To improve coordination and reduce confusion during evacuation missions, all personnel involved in
the process train to understand and apply the appropriate evacuation request formats. This includes
recognizing different reporting styles, using standardized templates, and operating digital communication
systems that support multiple formats. Units may also establish pre-mission agreements on which format will
be used to streamline communication. Proper training and planning ensure that critical information is
transmitted accurately and on time, improving both casualty outcomes and mission success.

## COMPARISON OF EVACUATION REQUEST FORMATS

E-3. The U.S. military 9-Line MEDEVAC request, ABCANZ Nine-Line and NATO 9-liner and formats
share similarities but also have significant differences. The NATO format prioritizes interoperability and
includes elements such as the Mechanism, Injuries, Signs, and Treatment report, which enhances the clarity
and accuracy of patient information. In contrast, the U.S. Army and ABCANZ formats use standardized
codes for brevity and clarity. Table E-1 on pages 126 and 127 highlights the similarities and differences
between the three formats.
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**

**Note.**The codes listed in each line of this table are examples and not all-inclusive. For a comprehensive understanding
of the codes and their applications, readers must reference the specific format's source publication, such as ATP 4-02.2,

E-4. The Senior Surgeon, working with mission leaders, identify and establish a standardized MEDEVAC
request format to support clear communication and effective patient care. Leaders communicate this format
through the orders process and reinforce it during training and planning. By aligning the reporting standard
with the mission’s operational needs, units can reduce confusion and maintain interoperability across
different coalitions and environments.
E-5. Standardizing evacuation request formats helps streamline operations and improve coordination. Units
should:
•
Combining best practices from multiple formats to create one standardized MEDEVAC request.
•
Use uniform terrain and tactical descriptions to include all essential information.
•
Modify the NATO format to include radio frequency and call sign that align with U.S. and
ABCANZ standards.
E-6. By adopting a standardized format and ensuring that it is clearly communicated and incorporated into
unit training and operational planning, units can optimize their response to evacuation requests, ultimately
preserving life, limb, and eyesight in high-stress operational environments. Standardization also fosters
greater trust and cooperation among multinational partners, enhancing the overall effectiveness of combined
medical operations.

### SECTION II—TERMS

**ambulance exchange point**
A location where a patient is transferred from one ambulance to another en route to a medical
treatment facility. Also called AXP. (ATP 4-02.2)
**casualty**
Any person who is lost to the organization by having been declared dead, duty status – whereabouts
unknown, excused absence – whereabouts unknown, missing, ill, or injured. (JP 4-02)
**casualty collection point**
A location that may or may not be staffed, where casualties are assembled for evacuation to a medical
treatment facility. (ATP 4-02.2)
**130**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
**Glossary**
***casualty evacuation**
The unregulated movement of casualties aboard any vehicle. Also called CASEVAC. (JP 4-02)
(Army) The movement of wounded, ill, and injured personnel aboard any nonmedical platform; also
called CASEVAC.
**combat power**
The total means of destructive and disruptive force that a military unit/formation can apply against an
enemy at a given time. (JP 3-0)
**command and control warfighting function**
The related tasks and a system that enable commanders to exercise authority and direction to
accomplish missions. (ADP 3-0)
**fires warfighting function**
The related tasks and systems that create and converge effects in all domains against the threat to
enable operations across the range of military operations. (ADP 3-0)
**intelligence warfighting function**
The related tasks and systems that facilitate understanding the enemy, terrain, weather, civil
considerations, and other significant aspects of the operational environment. (ADP 3-0)
**mass casualty**
Any number of human casualties produced across a period of time that exceeds available medical
support capabilities. Also called MASCAL. (JP 4-02)
**medical evacuation**
The timely and effective movement of the wounded, injured, or ill to and between medical treatment
facilities on dedicated and properly marked medical platforms with en route care provided by medical
personnel. Also called MEDEVAC. (ATP 4-02.2)
**medical regulating**
The coordination for the movement of patients to match patients with an appropriate medical treatment
facility. (JP 4-02)
**medical treatment facility**
A facility established for the purpose of furnishing medical and/or dental care to eligible individuals.
Also called MTF. (JP 4-02)
(Army) Any facility established for the purpose of providing medical treatment. This includes battalion
aid stations, Role 2 facilities, dispensaries, clinics, and hospitals. (FM 4-02)
**movement and maneuver warfighting function**
The related tasks and systems that move and employ forces to achieve a position of relative advantage
with respect to the enemy. (ADP 3-0)
**patient**
A sick, injured, wounded, or other person receiving medical care, dental care, or other medical
treatment. (JP 4-02)
(Army) A wounded, ill, and injured person who receives medical care or treatment from medical
personnel. (ATP 4-02.10)
**protection warfighting function**
The related tasks, systems, and methods that prevent or mitigate detection, threat effects, and hazards
to preserve the force, deny the enemy freedom of action, and enable commanders to apply combat
power. (ADP 3-0)
**reconstitution**
An operation that commanders plan and implement to restore units to a desired level of combat
effectiveness commensurate with mission requirements and available resources. (ATP 3-94.4)
**30 May 2026**
**FM 3-08**
**131**
**Glossary**
**return to duty**
A patient disposition which, after medical evaluation and treatment when necessary, returns a Soldier
for duty in his unit. (FM 4-02)
**survivability**
A quality or capability of military forces which permits them to avoid or withstand hostile actions or
environmental conditions while retaining the ability to fulfill their primary mission. (ATP 3-37.34)
**sustainment warfighting function**
The related tasks and systems that provide support and services to enable freedom of action, extend
operational reach, and prolong endurance. (ADP 3-0)
**triage**
The system of sorting and prioritizing casualties based on the tactical situation, mission, available
resources, and medical urgency. (FM 4-02)
**warfighting function**
A group of tasks and systems united by a common purpose that commanders use to accomplish
missions and training objectives. (ADP 3-0)
**132**
**FM 3-08**
**30 May 2026**
